Foreword
Eric Rodwell and his partner Jeff Meckstroth are the best-known pair in the world
ÔÇö ÔÇśMeckwellÔÇÖ is a neologism that is in every bridge playerÔÇÖs lexicon. Having
reported their efforts for more years than I care to remember, I can safely say that
they are also the most formidable partnership in the world. Brian Clough, a legend
in English soccer, was once quoted as saying, ÔÇśI wouldnÔÇÖt say I was the best manager
in the business, but IÔÇÖm in the top one.ÔÇÖ So, hereÔÇÖs a question that IÔÇÖll answer for Jeff
and Eric (they are far too modest to make such a claim):
ÔÇśAre they the best pair in the world?ÔÇÖ
ÔÇśPerhaps not, but theyÔÇÖre in the top one.ÔÇÖ
The theme of Peter ShafferÔÇÖs play Amadeus is SalieriÔÇÖs envy of Mozart, a classic
example of the inequality of inborn talent. The play contrasts MozartÔÇÖs genius with
the more modest abilities of Salieri, which appear mediocre by comparison. As a
journalist, I have been fortunate enough to be able to enjoy the company of many
world-class bridge players and to discuss with them countless situations in bidding,
play and defense. I am close enough to these stars to realize just how far away I am
from achieving their level of play.
If you canÔÇÖt play with (or against) the stars, the next-best thing is to study their
methods and try to understand how they think about the game. However, it is
very rare for a top-class player to tell you exactly how he or she does it. You will
appreciate, then, the exceptional nature of this book, as someone who is surely one
of the Bridge Immortals lets us in on his unique approach to play and defense. It
has been my privilege to work with him as a researcher, sourcing and selecting
example deals to illustrate the principles he describes.
To some extent bridge is a puzzle without an answer ÔÇö IÔÇÖve been at it for forty
years and I still have no idea how to play. This book represents one of the best
opportunities you will ever have to get inside the mind of a champion ÔÇö donÔÇÖt fail
to take it.
Mark Horton
March 2011
Foreword * 11

---

IntroductIon
Back in the 1980s I developed a series of notes on an approach to hand analysis and
cardplay that I found useful. The methodology included giving my own names to
many different types of plays, strategies, and analytical techniques ÔÇö I had learned
in college that giving unusual, even funny names to things makes them easier to
remember.
I gave a digital copy of the notes (the original ÔÇśRodwell FileÔÇÖ!) to Fred Gitelman,
a promising Junior at the time, and it circulated amongst a small number of players
in Toronto. One person who saw the notes was Ray Lee, who suggested to me
many years ago that I turn them into a book. I resisted the idea, partly because I
knew that my notes were a long way from becoming what I intended, which was
a good book on advanced cardplay. Then at the Washington NABC in 2009, Ray
approached me again, this time with the idea of adding Mark Horton to the project
as a co-author. This idea excited me, as I didnÔÇÖt really have time to research good
examples to illustrate all the concepts.
Mark has done a marvelous job of finding all sorts of suitable deals, mostly
from high-level play. In fact, when I got his material it occurred to me that the
book could be made useful to a much wider range of players. So I wrote some new
introductory chapters that establish the foundation for what is talked about later.
For some time I had not been keeping records of hands I played, but during 2010
and 2011, with the book in mind, I collected interesting deals that illustrated the
concepts and that in some cases introduced additional concepts. The combination
of all these efforts is a larger book than we had first planned, but I believe a better
one.
Most of the deals presented here are ÔÇśrealÔÇÖ ÔÇö they occurred and were played as
described. I wish I had recorded more of my own deals but there are many good
hands here involving other players. I have constructed some deals when none
that we knew of from actual play illustrated the point properly. A few of them are
randomly generated by my computer.
In some cases, I talk about a line of play or defense that was not actually selected
at the table but that would have made things interesting, but I donÔÇÖt then present it
as a first-person account of a hand I actually played. In a few deals I made minor
modifications to spot cards to make the deal more interesting.
Finally, the names. In some cases, the name is descriptive. In other cases it is
just something that came into my mind, often somewhat silly as I think they stick
in the mind better. I hope those few that are named after other players wonÔÇÖt offend
anyone.
12 * The Rodwell Files

---

how the Book Is organIzed
The first two chapters in Part 1 set the groundwork for the more advanced concepts:
how to develop tricks, entries, trump management, counting, calculating the odds,
and the basics of squeezes and endplays. They move quickly, and are intended
more as a refresher than a fundamental text. I recommend you study these chapters
carefully unless you are at a level where it is all easy, and even then you should still
skim them to make sure you can connect the later concepts properly. This part of
the book ends with two important chapters on the analytical process: they describe
the steps involved in selecting a line of play or defense, and include a checklist of
ÔÇśdefoggingÔÇÖ questions to fall back on if you get stuck.
The rest of the book is largely concerned with identifying, naming and
illustrating a wide variety of bridge plays and situations. Where I have invented
names, they made sense to me at the time, and in most cases had something to do
with the play itself. If you want to call them something else, be my guest: at least
the wheels are turning for you and that should be beneficial.
There are numerous examples that just illustrate a principle, but others are
presented as problems in declarer play or defense. The depth of the explanations
does vary somewhat also, to accommodate varying levels of player. As I said earlier,
the emphasis, wherever possible, is on real-life deals instead of the constructed
layouts that fill most ÔÇśsolve these problemsÔÇÖ books. This has made our task both
tougher and more interesting, as real deals sometimes donÔÇÖt have a clear-cut answer.
Quite a few of the examples involve me as a player, and in some cases I went wrong
at the table. I tend to find the decisions I get wrong to be more interesting, and
potentially more instructive, than those I get right.
who should read thIs Book?
This book is for anyone who wants to understand, even partially, higher-level
cardplay, or for anyone who just enjoys reading about it and appreciates its beauty.
There are numerous books on bidding and conventions but I feel that cardplay
technique is a more valuable subject to study, and that improvement in this area
will help your scores quite a bit.
ÔÇó Experts: IÔÇÖm sure you will find this presentation different from anything
you have encountered before, and the examples interesting and useful. I
hope you find the names of things helpful as well.
ÔÇó Strong Regional players: This book gives you the chance to improve the
level of your play significantly.
ÔÇó Flight B players: Depending on your innate ability and desire to delve into
things, you can make enormous progress in cardplay.
ÔÇó Flight C and Club players: DonÔÇÖt be put off! You can learn more than you
imagined was possible, and I predict you will have fun doing it.
Introduction * 13

---

If advanced cardplay concepts are difficult for you, just try to grasp what you can.
Go over it again after a time and you will be able to learn more and more. If you
are having trouble following the play from the text, I suggest using your own deck
of cards to lay out the hand and follow the play trick for trick. I recommend
reading the material in the order it is presented, but there is no reason you canÔÇÖt
skip around if it suits you. Whatever your level, and however far you get into
the book, you will get better results and more pleasure when you play. It is not
necessary to understand everything in here to benefit from it. And in due time, you
just might get there anyway!
As large as this book is, it isnÔÇÖt big enough to include everything you might wish
to study about some topics (especially the more basic ones), and some suggestions
for further reading are given in an Appendix. This book is intended to be many
things: a textbook, a reference book, a book suitable for teachers to use, and a
problem book ÔÇö but most of all, I hope youÔÇÖll find it to be entertainment. Enjoy!
Eric Rodwell
March 2011

Chapter 1
some BAsIc IdeAs
This is a book about cardplay in bridge, which is a vast subject. Many of the
concepts are, if not ÔÇśadvancedÔÇÖ, certainly beyond the basic level. I firmly believe
that many of the ÔÇśsecretsÔÇÖ of expert bridge can be understood by most players if they
are willing to invest some time. And while far too many players get hung up in the
fascination of bidding conventions, the game is usually won or lost in the cardplay,
not the auction.
Let me start by saying that I believe strongly in having names and labels to attach
to concepts. I find that having such labels makes the ideas easier to remember, and
it certainly makes it easier to refer to them when writing, with some assurance that
the reader and I both have the same thing in mind. I have invented terms for many
of the specific cardplay techniques that appear in later chapters.
In the first two chapters I am going review some of the more basic techniques
to allow a larger audience to follow the later narrative. There will be a lot of later
references to squeezes, endplays, trump promotions and the like, so I want to make
sure everyone knows what I am talking about. Even if you think you are familiar
with all this, I urge you to read through it quickly rather than skipping over it ÔÇö if
only so you have a grasp of the terminology. However, even in an area as basic as
finessing, you may find I have something to say that is new to you.
cashIng solId trIcks
The general principle is to cash your tricks in such a way as to allow the fluid
ÔÇśrunningÔÇÖ of the suit. This is usually easier if you play the high card(s) from the
short side first.
´éź A Q J 3
´éź K 2
Start with the king, then cash the ace, queen and jack.
I have somehting ot say taht is new
16 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

If you have no small card to connect with the other hand (often called a link
card), then you have a choice: either cash the winners in the short hand then use a
side entry to the other winners, or overtake the last winner in the short hand.
(a) ´éź A J 3 2 (b) ´é¬ A J 10 2
´éź K Q ´é¬ K Q
In (a), to get the four tricks you have on top, cash the ´éźKQ then cross to dummy
in another suit to cash the ´éźAJ. In (b), cash the ´é¬K then overtake the ´é¬Q with the
´é¬A to cash the ´é¬J10.
If your solid suit has a lot of cards in it (what is called a ÔÇśgood fitÔÇÖ), then running
the solid suit forces the defenders to make a lot of discards. This can be useful
in helping you figure out the distribution of the remaining suits, or as part of a
legitimate line of play such as a squeeze. However, there is a downside ÔÇö you lose
your ability to communicate back and forth if you run the long suit, so you have to
consider the matter on a case-by-case basis.
´éę A Q J 10 4 3
´éę K 9 8 5
Here you can run six diamond tricks, but you lose communication if you do so.
Also, it is possible that discarding twice from your hand will be awkward.
If your solid suit is a short-suit fit, your high cards are not only entries but also
stoppers in the suit that prevent the opponents from running this suit. It is less
attractive to cash out this type of suit early in the play, unless you are trying for an
endplay (where you force them to make a favorable lead).
(a) ´éź K 3 (b) ´é¬ K Q 4
´éź A 2 ´é¬ A J 3
In (a), you have two winners, a.k.a. stoppers. When they lead a spade they knock
out one of your stoppers. You can choose to win the first trick in either hand, and
as with any choice you need to consider whether there is reason to pick one over
the other. Do you want to be in a certain hand now? If not, then consider which
hand might need the entry later, and play accordingly.
Example (b) has three winners, and a surplus high card, the ´é¬J. This means
you can win two tricks in dummy, or two in hand. Another choice.
Some Basic Ideas * 17

---

ProMotIng hIgh cards
Promoting high cards means driving out opponentsÔÇÖ high card(s) so that your lower
honors can become winners. In most respects this is like cashing solid suits except
you have to lose the lead one or more times.
(a) ´éź K Q J (b) ´é¬ K J 10 3 (c) ´éę K J 10 9 3
´éź 4 3 2 ´é¬ Q 2 ´éę Q
The spade suit in (a) above plays itself: when you knock out the ace you have
promoted two winners. The position in (b) is best played by leading the ´é¬Q then
the small one (high card from the short side first). In (c), the diamond suit can be
played first as an overtake, ´éęQ to the ´éęK, then led from the top until they take the
´éęA. Note that the lowest card, the ´éę3, will be a winner if the suit divides 4-3. If
you have enough entries to dummy (at least two, as they will duck the ace on the
first round of the suit), you can play the ´éęQ, then cross using an entry and lead the
´éęK to guarantee four tricks.
You may have to knock out more than one stopper in your suit:
(a) ´éź Q J 10 (b) ´é¬ J 8 4 3 (c) ´éę J 10 9 8 3
´éź 5 4 2 ´é¬ 10 9 2 ´éę Q 2
In (a), spades must be led twice to knock out the two high cards and set up your
one winner. The heart suit in (b) must be led three times. In (c), the diamond
suit must knock out two winners, and in case they refuse to win an early trick, you
should lead the ´éęQ first. You will need at least one side entry to dummy and will
need two entries if they duck the first diamond lead.
If you have honors split between the two hands you can choose which trick(s)
you win in which hand (depending perhaps on when they choose to take their
winners in the suit).
(a) ´éź K J 3 (b) ´é¬ Q 2
´éź Q 6 5 ´é¬ K 3
In position (a), if you want to retain a late entry to hand, play the honors from
dummy first. The defenders might thwart this plan by ducking the ´éźA twice; in
this case you have your two tricks and must now look elsewhere. In (b), say LHO
leads low to his partnerÔÇÖs ace. You can choose between playing low from hand and
winning the next lead there, or unblocking the ´é¬K to win the next lead in dummy.
The high card(s) you knock out donÔÇÖt have to be the highest cards in the suit.
Many such cases involve suits where a finesse (see p. 20) is a possibility.
18 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

(a) ´éź A K J 10 (b) ´é¬ Q J 2 (c) ´éę A K Q 10 9 3
´éź 4 3 ´é¬ A 10 9 5 4 ´éę 8 4
The spades in (a) can be developed for three tricks. You can cash the ´éźAK, hoping
the ´éźQ will drop, then lead the ´éźJ to knock out the queen. You will need a side
entry to dummy to cash the ´éź10. If you have no entry, you can lead the ´éźJ the first
time (if you are in the dummy).
The heart suit in (b) is best played by taking a finesse against the ´é¬K, but if you
need only four tricks you can lead low from hand to the ´é¬Q, then follow by playing
the ´é¬J. If you had ´é¬A10985 in hand, you could afford to lead the ´é¬A, unblocking
the ´é¬Q, and continue with a low heart to the jack.
In (c), the diamond suit produces six tricks if the jack falls in three rounds. If
a defender has four or five to the jack, you continue with the ´éę10 to force out the
´éęJ, then your ´éę9 and ´éę3 are tricks. Of course you need a side entry to cash them; if
you donÔÇÖt have one you can run the ´éę8, or if stuck in dummy, lead the ´éę10. These
plays would make sense if you needed only five winners from the suit.
estaBlIshIng sMall cards
Small cards become good if neither opponent has any cards left in the suit. You will
develop the maximum number of tricks in such a suit when the opponentsÔÇÖ cards
are split as evenly as possible.
(a) ´éź A 8 7 (b) ´é¬ A K 7 6 (c) ´éę 9 7 6 5 4
´éź 9 6 5 4 3 2 ´é¬ 5 4 3 2 ´éę 8 3 2
In (a), you are only missing four spades, the ´éźKQJ10. If they are split 2-2, playing
ace and another establishes your four remaining small spades. If they are 3-1, you
need to surrender another trick before your last three spades are good. If they are
4-0, then you need to come to hand and surrender a third spade trick to establish
the two small spades.
In (b), you are missing ´é¬QJ1098. If they are divided 3-2, you can give one up
and establish one small card. If they are 4-1 or 5-0, you can establish nothing. In
order to avoid establishing two tricks for the opponents, you may consider losing
an early trick in the suit (ducking), so that when you cash the ace next, you will
know whether or not cashing the king will do you any good.
You have no top cards in (c), as you are missing ´éęAKQJ10. You must surrender
at least three tricks in the suit, depending on whether the split is 3-2, 4-1, or 5-0.
Some Basic Ideas * 19

---

FInesses
A finesse is an attempt to trap one or more high cards that one or both opponents
hold. There are many variations. The simplest position is when you lead toward
a medium-strength card.
(a) ´éź A Q 3 (b) ´é¬ Q 3 2
´éź 6 5 4 ´é¬ A 8 7
In (a), you can lead low toward the ´éźAQ, planning to cover the ´éźK with the ´éźA,
and otherwise to play the ´éźQ. This is a 50-50 chance, depending on the location
of the ´éźK. Note that you must lead toward your high cards to take advantage of the
favorable location of the king. This is a sound principle to keep in mind for many
situations. Leading toward your high cards is called lobbing (the same term as in
tennis).
In (b), you can lead toward the ´é¬Q, with a 50-50 chance that the queen will
be worth a trick. A difference from (a) is that with the ace in the opposite hand,
West can take his king and score a fast trick even if you do end up with two tricks.
Note that the beginner ÔÇśfinesseÔÇÖ of leading the ´é¬Q canÔÇÖt work because East can cover
with the ´é¬K if he has it and you will have nothing left but small cards. This type
of ÔÇśhopeless finesseÔÇÖ has been dubbed a Chinese finesse. I will be referring to this
in later chapters.
The finesses above are not attractive since you gain at best one trick and help
the opponents establish their tricks if the finesse doesnÔÇÖt work, i.e. it is offside. You
should prefer to finesse in suits where you are for sure promoting one or more high
cards, or working toward establishing small cards:
(a) ´éź A Q J (b) ´é¬ A Q 4 3
´éź 4 3 2 ´é¬ 7 6 5
In (a), even if the finesse loses, you will establish a second trick. If the finesse
works, you need to come back to hand to repeat the finesse to get your third trick.
Of course, just because the finesse wins doesnÔÇÖt mean a second finesse will work.
A wily defender might duck his ´éźK the first time to make you waste an entry to
repeat the finesse.
In (b), you have the chance of a finesse and a 3-3 split. Whether the finesse
wins or loses, you can try ducking the next lead, hoping that the ´é¬A will bring
down the remaining cards.
The type of finesse I have been discussing where you lead toward your honor(s),
I will name a lobbing finesse. However, if you have enough strength in a suit you
can profitably lead an honor, which has the advantage of holding the lead for a
repeated finesse.
20 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

(a) ´éź A Q 3 (b) ´é¬ Q 3 2 (c) ´éę A J 10 3
´éź J 10 4 ´é¬ A J 10 9 ´éę Q 9 4
In (a), you have a surplus honor, the ´éź10, so you can lead the ´éźJ. If West has the
king and doesnÔÇÖt cover, you have retained the lead to repeat the finesse. Note that
you have three cards in each hand. When you lead the ´éźJ, if West covers it goes
jack, king, ace, and you spend two honors on the same trick. However, since you
have one more honor (AQJ10) than cards in the suit you will still be able to take all
the tricks when the ´éźK is onside.
In (b), you lead the ´é¬Q from dummy. Note again you have five honors (you
can count the ´é¬9 as an honor here since it touches the other cards you have, the
´é¬QJ10). If East covers you have four tricks. If East doesnÔÇÖt cover, you retain the
lead for a second finesse. If you have no more dummy entries you will take four
tricks if East has the ´é¬K along with no more than two small hearts.
Position (c) is a favorite in bridge books. Now you have the nine in the opposite
hand, and can ensure four tricks without re-entering your hand if you lead that
nine first. When it holds, you next lead the queen then finally low to the jack. If
you lead the queen first, then your nine isnÔÇÖt high enough to hold the lead and you
will have to waste an entry getting back to take a third finesse.
Finesses are often taken by defenders as well. However, since they can see only
the dummy and not partnerÔÇÖs holding, they are playing for a layout of the suit that
is hypothetical.
(a) ´éź 4 3 2 (b) ´é¬ 4 3 2
´éź A Q 5 ´éź J 10 6 ´é¬ A Q 10 9 ´é¬ J 8 5
´éź K 9 8 7 ´é¬ K 7 6
In (a), East leads the ´éźJ to take a defensive finesse, as I call it. Whether South covers
or not, the defenders take three spade tricks. Note, however, that this establishes
the thirteenth spade for declarer, which might or might not matter.
In (b), if East is in (on lead) for the last time, he leads the ´é¬J if he needs three
or more fast tricks in hearts. This time partner has just what is needed, the ´é¬AQ10.
This type of finesse, whether by declarer or defense, I will call a pusher finesse
because you need to lead a card that either holds the trick or is covered, which is
commonly called a pusher.
In most finessing situations you also have the option of ÔÇśplaying for the dropÔÇÖ,
which means playing for the missing card to be offside but with few enough small
cards accompanying it that the card will drop.

(a) ´éź A Q 9 2 (b) ´é¬ A K J 10 8
´éź J 10 3 ´é¬ 9 7 6 5
In (a), you are missing six to the king, so the finesse is a much better chance than
playing for the drop, which requires the ´éźK to be singleton. I will be discussing
basic odds calculations in Chapter 3, but I will tell you that the odds are basically
31:1 against playing for the drop, as opposed to 50-50 for the finesse.
In the suit shown in (b), you are missing only four cards and you have the
´é¬AK, so playing for the drop is a good chance. Most players would recognize that
going for the drop is recommended by the rule ÔÇśeight ever, nine neverÔÇÖ. In any
event, with no other information available, the odds for the drop are slightly better
than for the finesse.
You can often combine playing for the drop with finessing:
(a) ´éź A J 10 4 3 (b) ´é¬ A K J 3
´éź K 5 2 ´é¬ 7 6 5
In (a), cash the ´éźK in case East has the singleton ´éźQ, then finesse the ´éź10. If East

In (b), you can cash the ´é¬A first in case there is stiff ´é¬Q, then finesse the ´é¬J

This type of play, cashing one or more winners in case something important
drops, I have named cash catering. You are catering to a certain short-honor
holding by cashing one or more winners before (presumably) finessing.
When missing the queen, and sometimes the jack, you often have a two-way
finesse if you have higher cards in each hand.
(a) ´éź A J 10 3 (b) ´é¬ A J 10 3 (c) ´éę Q 10 9 3 (d) ´éĘ A J

In (b), you are missing the ´é¬9 and ´é¬8, so while you can take a finesse either

Many positions involve finessing against more than one card.
(a) ´éź K J 2 (b) ´é¬ A Q 10 (c) ´éę 9 4 3
´éź 7 6 5 ´é¬ 8 5 4 3 ´éę A J 10 8
In (a), lead low from hand to the ´éźJ. If the ´éźAQ are onside, you can repeat the
finesse. If the ´éźJ forces the ace, you have developed one trick. If the ´éźJ loses to the
´éźQ, come back to hand and lob toward the ´éźK.
In (b), lead low to the ´é¬10. If that wins, you can cross again to finesse the ´é¬Q.
If the ´é¬10 loses to the king, cash the ´é¬AQ, hoping for a 3-3 split (or ´é¬J9 doubleton)
to make your ´é¬8 good. If the ´é¬10 loses to the ´é¬J, cross to hand to finesse the
queen. In all cases you can hope your ´é¬8 will eventually get set up.
In (c), you have a finesse against touching cards, the ´éęKQ. Easiest is to lead
low the first time to the ´éęJ, probably losing. Then lead the ´éę9 for a pusher finesse
against the other honor.
Sometimes there will be a delayed finesse available.
(a) ´éź A K 9 2 (b) ´é¬ A K 3 (c) ´éę K 3 2
´éź 8 7 6 5 ´é¬ J 9 4 2 ´éę A Q 8 7
In (a), lead up to the ´éźA. If West plays an honor, you can win this trick and then
lead low to your ´éź8, setting up a finesse if he started with ´éźQJ10x and split his
honors (another example of the advantage of leading toward your high cards.) If
West plays low, play the ´éźA. If East plays an honor on this trick, again you should
Some Basic Ideas * 23

---

lead low toward your ´éź8, hoping West has the other two honors to set up a delayed
finesse. In all cases, if the suit is 3-2 you have no need to finesse ÔÇö you just drop
the missing card and cash your ´éź9.
In (b), leading the ´é¬J is attempting a Chinese Finesse. Instead, cash the ´é¬AK; if
nothing falls, lob toward your ´é¬J, hoping for one more trick. If the ´é¬10 falls, your
´é¬J9 are ÔÇśequalsÔÇÖ against the ´é¬Q. If West has singleton or doubleton ´é¬Q, you can
take a delayed finesse against EastÔÇÖs ´é¬10xxx(x). If East has a singleton or doubleton
´é¬Q, your ´é¬J becomes a trick. You should consider not cashing it right away; West
must then keep his ´é¬10xxx(x) protected and you might be able to force him to lead
away from it later in the hand (an endplay).
In (c), cash the ´éęA and then lead low to the ´éęK. You are missing ´éęJ109xxx
originally. If a defender shows out you give up on the suit. If both follow but two
of the big cards (´éęJ109) are still out, you try for a 3-3 split. The interesting case is
when West plays two high cards (say the ´éę10 and ´éę9), making the layout one of
these:
(a) ´éę K 3 2 (b) ´éę K 3 2
´éę 10 9 ´éę J 6 5 4 ´éę J 10 9 ´éę 6 5 4
´éę A Q 8 7 ´éę A Q 8 7
In the first case, you take a delayed finesse by playing a diamond to your eight. In
the second case, you should avoid a delayed finesse. What should you do? It is all
about the percentage play, i.e. the one that works most often. If West has ´éęJ109,
he can play any two cards he wants, but with ´éęJ10, ´éęJ9 or ´éę109 doubleton he must
play the cards he was dealt. If you always finesse, you will win three times for every
once you lose.
Terence Reese called this the Principle of Restricted Choice, i.e. when you
are missing two or more touching cards and you have to guess whether to take
a delayed finesse, assume the opponentÔÇÖs play was restricted, not that they chose
which card to play from equals.
´éź K 4 3 2
´éź A 10 9 8 7
Say you lead the ´éź10. West plays the ´éźQ, and you win with the king. Now when
you lead the ´éź2, East follows with the other remaining small card. Should you
finesse or play for the drop?
The Principle of Restricted Choice says to assume WestÔÇÖs play was forced, i.e.
play him for stiff ´éźQ and finesse. This wins whenever West has a stiff honor (2
cases) and loses to ´éźQJ doubleton (1 case) ÔÇö giving you odds of about 2:1 in your
favor. IÔÇÖm fudging the math a little since the three cases arenÔÇÖt equally likely, but as
it happens, itÔÇÖs not far off here.

There are many possible finessing positions. I suggest reading the excellent
section on suit combinations in the Official Encyclopedia of Bridge.
ruFFIng FInesses
A ruffing finesse uses the power of the trump suit to complete the finesse.
´éź 2
´é¬ 7 6 5
´éź A Q J 10
´é¬ A K Q 3 2
If hearts are trumps, you might try to ruff three spades in dummy, but that would
require a 4-4 spade break and lots of entries to hand. Instead, you could lead the
´éźA and run the ´éźQ, taking a ruffing finesse against West. If West plays the ´éźK, you
ruff; if he does not, you discard from a side suit. Even if the finesse loses, you have
established two more winners. If you think East has the ´éźK, you can lead a spade
from dummy to your queen and then ruff two more in dummy.
Restricted choice can come into play in a ruffing finesse position:
´éź 3 2
´é¬ J 3 2
´éź A K 10 9 4
´é¬ A K Q 10 9 8
Hearts are trumps. You cash the ´éźAK, and East drops the ´éźJ on the second round.
What now?
Your best play now is to cash the ´é¬AK. If both follow, or if West has the last
trump, you lead the ´éź10, planning to take a ruffing finesse. Why not try to ruff
out the ´éźQJx from East? Because the Principle of Restricted Choice says donÔÇÖt play
someone to have chosen from equals, play for their choice to have been restricted.
If East has three trumps, then you have to play for spades to be 3-3 since he can ruff
in if you successfully run the ´éź10.
Some Basic Ideas * 25

---

ruFFIng losers In the hand wIth shorter truMPs
Dummy is normally the hand with shorter trumps, and in this case trumping losers
in the dummy will gain you extra tricks.
(a) ´éź 8 (b) ´éę A 4 3
´é¬ 4 3 2 ´éĘ 4 3 2
´éź A 4 3 ´éę 8
´é¬ A K Q J 10 ´éĘ A K Q J 10
Position (a) is simple. If hearts are trumps, you can trump both spade losers in
dummy and get 5 + 2 = 7 trump tricks. Conversely, in Position (b) with clubs
trumps, it does you no good to trump the losing diamonds in hand. You still get
only five trump tricks, and in fact might lose one if clubs divide 4-1.
Defenders are aware of this possibility and will usually find the trump switch
if they see dummy with (typically) three trumps and a side-suit ruffing value
(doubleton or less). You donÔÇÖt want to help them. In (a) above, if you donÔÇÖt have
side entries to hand, donÔÇÖt lead any trumps. Cash the ´éźA, ruff a spade, then cross
in trumps to ruff the second spade. If you cash a high trump first, then after ruffing
a spade there is only one trump left in dummy, and if you surrender the lead they
will take it out with a trump switch.
truMP control
Establishing side-suit winners wonÔÇÖt necessarily do you any good if there are enemy
trumps out. If the defenders can ruff in and either cash too many tricks or prevent
you from cashing yours, you have lost trump control. A number of the examples
and principles that come later in the book deal with trump control but here IÔÇÖll
discuss a few basic ideas.
1. Unless there is a reason not to (and there often is), draw all the outstanding
trumps.
´éź 5 4 3 2
´é¬ 2
´éę A K 8 7
´éĘ A 6 5 4
´éź A K Q J 10 9 7
´é¬ A 5 4 3
´éę 2
´éĘ 8
26 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

You are in 7´éź. You have eleven top tricks and can ruff two hearts in dummy to get
to thirteen. However, you should not risk any ruffs or overruffs and should draw
the two outstanding trumps before ruffing your hearts in dummy (or, preferably,
claiming).
2. With any suit combination, and your trump suit is a suit combination, study the
position to see how many are out, and exactly what cards are missing (if they
might be important). I call this an asset survey.
On the previous deal, you were missing just two small trumps. I suggest noting
how many total trumps you have, so if you have seven they have six, if you have
eight they have five, etc. Then as you draw each round you can subtract from the
original missing number.
The idea of an asset survey is not restricted to trump contracts, of course.
HereÔÇÖs a deal from the 2011 Spring NABC:

´éĘ A 6 2 1
West leads the ´é¬2, attitude, showing interest in the heart suit. How do you play
from here?
This hand was played by Jeff Meckstroth and typically, he saw the answer
immediately: stick in the ´é¬7. This paid off as West had led from ´é¬Q982. Even so
this gets you up to only seven tricks. Now what?
Since there was nothing great to do in other suits, he cashed the hearts to make
East discard three times. East pitched an encouraging club and two discouraging
spades. Jeff now tried the ´éź10 ÔÇö East covered with the jack and he won with the
ace. Now, reading West for 4-4 in the majors, he led a low club from hand to the
jack. When East won with the king and returned a low diamond, Jeff inserted
another middle card: the nine. That was the end of the hand as the full deal was:

Suppose you are in 6´éź and the lead is the ´é¬Q, which you win with the ace. Cash

return to hand with the ´éĘ10, draw one more round of trumps, and lead high clubs,
throwing diamonds. You will make your slam if the defender who has four trumps
also has at least three clubs.

crossruFF and duMMY reVersal
A crossruff means ruffing back and forth in both hands. This way you might score
all your trumps separately. Crossruffing is most attractive when your trumps are
high enough to stop overruffs and you have enough distribution to keep ruffing in
each hand. You will lose trump control eventually, so you need to cash any side
winners you hope to get early. Lew Stansby made 6´éź on a crossruff against Jeff
Meckstroth and me on this deal:
´éź A Q 10 9
´é¬ A 5 4 3
´éę 3
´éĘ A K Q 4
´éź K J 4 3
´é¬ 8
´éę A K 9 7 6 5
´éĘ 10 3
North opened 1´éĘ, and South responded 1´éę. From there he got to 6´éź and I led a
heart. Reasoning that neither defender was likely to have six hearts and not have
bid at the one-level, he took the ´é¬A and ruffed a heart, led a club to the ace and
ruffed another heart. Then he played a club to the king and a diamond to the ace.
At this point he had scored four side-suit tricks and two heart ruffs, and he had
six remaining high trumps. So he claimed six on a crossruff even though he could
have tried to cash the ´éĘQ (which would have worked) or the ´éęK (which would
not). These plays risked suffering a ruff followed by a trump return which would
kill one of the crossruff trump tricks.
Hands where you hope to run a long suit are not good candidates for a crossruff:
´éź A 8 7 6
´é¬ 5
´éę A 4 3
´éĘ K Q J 9 8
´éź K 5 4 3
´é¬ A 8 7 6
´éę 2
´éĘ A 10 4 3
You are in 4´éź and they lead the ´é¬Q. You could crossruff hearts and diamonds for a
while, but you wouldnÔÇÖt be able to get more than two club tricks without suffering
a ruff. Better to try to maintain trump control. Cash the ´éźAK and then run clubs.
Even if trumps are 4-1 you will be able to take ten tricks.

A dummy reversal is a maneuver where you ruff in the long trump hand. If
you are in a 5-3 fit, you need to ruff at least three times in the long trump hand to
show any benefit from this play. If ruffing even once in dummy isnÔÇÖt practical, a
dummy reversal might be the best approach:
´éź Q 10 9
´é¬ A 3 2
´éę A 5 4 3
´éĘ 8 6 5
´éź A K J 8 7
´é¬ K Q 5 4
´éę 2
´éĘ A K 3
Playing in 6´éź, you have eleven top tricks. Hearts could be 3-3, or you could try to
draw two rounds of trumps, hoping that you can then cash three heart tricks and
ruff the fourth heart in dummy. If the lead is a diamond, you can try to ruff three
diamonds in hand: ´éęA, diamond ruff, ´éź9, diamond ruff with the ´éźK, ´é¬A, diamond
ruff with the ´éźA, then overtake the ´éźJ with the ´éźQ and draw the last trump. This
requires only a 3-2 trump break.
Here is a slightly modified deal from the 2011 Spring NABC in Orlando. The
dummy reversal is easy to miss:
´éź A Q 2
´é¬ 4 3
´éę K 9 7 5
´éĘ K Q 4 3 Dealer West
´éź K 9 ´éź J 4 3 Neither Vul.
´é¬ A Q 10 7 6 ´é¬ K 8 5
´éę Q 4 2 ´éę A J 10 8 6 West North East South
1´é¬ dbl 2´éĘ1 2´éź

´éę 3 1. 9-11, artificial heart raise.
´éĘ A 10 5 2
West leads the ´éę2 to dummyÔÇÖs five and EastÔÇÖs six, and East returns a trump to
WestÔÇÖs nine and dummyÔÇÖs queen. What next?
You are a step behind to ruff a heart in dummy, but with lots of small trumps
in hand, a dummy reversal works perfectly: ruff a diamond, spade to the king and
ace, ruff a diamond, club to the king, ruff the last diamond. Then lead good clubs
ÔÇö they can ruff in whenever they want, but you get 2+0+0+4, and three diamond
ruffs in hand for nine tricks.

BasIc deFensIVe Ideas
Later in the book, IÔÇÖm going to discuss many exceptions to the ÔÇśstandardÔÇÖ rules of
defending. Before we get to the exceptions, though, it is worth going through a
brief review of those standard rules and why they normally apply:
1. second hand low
This gives partner, ÔÇśfourth handÔÇÖ, a chance to win the trick cheaply, or force ÔÇśthird
handÔÇÖ to put up a big card.
(a) ´éź K 9 3 (b) ´é¬ J 5 4
´éź J 8 7 6 ´éź A 10 2 ´é¬ A 9 3 2 ´é¬ Q 10 6
´éź Q 5 4 ´é¬ K 8 7
In (a), when the ´éź3 is led from dummy, play second hand low, the ´éź2. This keeps
your ´éźA10 over dummy. ÔÇśFlyingÔÇÖ with the ace is called ÔÇśbeating airÔÇÖ because instead
of capturing an honor with your ace, you get ÔÇśairÔÇÖ (small cards).
Similarly, in (b), when dummy leads low, play the ´é¬6. If you stick in the ´é¬10,
it goes king, ace. Declarer can then finesse against partnerÔÇÖs nine by running the
eight.
2. cover an honor with an honor
The ÔÇśsecond hand lowÔÇÖ rule is not intended to apply to situations where an honor
is led. In many situations, covering prevents a Chinese Finesse from succeeding.
Generally, when a single honor is led, cover. If an honor is led from touching
cards, wait to cover the last card in the sequence. Of course, all these rules presume
that there is some potential benefit from covering.
(a) ´éź J 3 2 (b) ´é¬ J 10 3
´éź 10 9 4 ´éź Q 6 5 ´é¬ K 9 2 ´é¬ Q 8 7 6
´éź A K 8 7 ´é¬ A 5 4
In (a), when the ´éźJ is led, since there is only one honor in dummy, cover with the
´éźQ. This promotes partnerÔÇÖs ´éź10. DeclarerÔÇÖs lead of the ´éźJ is not a hopeless play:
for example, since he has the ´éź87, he can develop a third trick if East has ´éźQ965.
In (b), when the ´é¬J is led, do not play the ´é¬Q. If you do, then declarer wins
the ace and lobs toward the ´é¬10 for a second trick. When you duck the ´é¬J, partner
wins the ´é¬K. If the ´é¬10 is led next, you do cover that because it is the last honor:
declarer is held to one trick in the suit.
Here is a deal I played in the 2011 Gatlinburg Regional, having got to a
seemingly hopeless 5´éĘ:

Clubs were split 3-2 with the king onside, so I was able to finesse the trumps and
ruff out the diamonds. Making +600!
I always say that covering an honor has a ÔÇśmake a wish foundationÔÇÖ, named
after the charity of the same name. Either you are promoting something in your
hand, or you are hoping to promote something in partnerÔÇÖs hand. However, if
partner canÔÇÖt have any length in the suit, you canÔÇÖt promote anything.

Indeed, it might be disastrous if partner has the stiff king. When you donÔÇÖt cover
the ´éźJ, declarer might decide to play for the drop.

draw trumps, ending in dummy.
3. third hand high
This rule is designed to prevent fourth hand from winning a cheap trick.
´éź 7 5 4
´éź Q 10 6 2 ´éź K 9 3
´éź A J 8
When partner leads a low spade, you must play third hand high, the ´éźK. Eventually
you hope you can trap declarerÔÇÖs jack. If you play a lower card, this is called
finessing against partner.
There are several common exceptions to this rule:
Partner leads an equivalent honor
(a) ´éź 5 4 3 (b) ´é¬ 5 4 2

you should risk covering partnerÔÇÖs ´é¬Q with the ´é¬K as an unblocking play.

4. return partnerÔÇÖs lead
This is primarily a rule for defending against notrump, where in most cases partner
has led a long suit. Against suit contracts, partnerÔÇÖs lead might be a singleton, in
which case returning the lead for a ruff is normally a good idea. In other cases
where it is apparent that there are no more tricks to take in the suit led, then you
can consider a switch:
´éź 4 3
´éę 8 7 6
´éź Q led ´éź A 8 7 6
´éę J 10 9
If you are defending a heart contract and partner leads the ´éźQ, you win the ´éźA.
Declarer is marked with the ´éźK and dummy can trump further spade leads, so
you switch to the ´éęJ, leading around into weakness and taking a defensive finesse.
Partner is likely to have one or more diamond honors.

5. choose between an active and a passive defense
ItÔÇÖs not always necessary to get active on defense ÔÇö indeed, getting active may be
the only way for you to let declarer get home.
The concept of an empty hand versus a full hand is a useful one when you
are trying to decide on an overall plan of defense. An empty hand is one in which
declarer has no prospects for developing the tricks he needs; he can get there only
with defensive help. A full hand is one in which declarer will get his tricks in due
time if given the chance. This has relevance in determining defensive strategy
(active or passive) but here letÔÇÖs just think about it in relation to whether or not to
cash a stranded winner.
´éź 5
´éź A Q 9 6 4 2 ´éź 8 3
´éź K J 10 7
You lead the ´éź6 to the eight and jack. Partner (when next in) returns the three to
the ten and your queen. If the hand is empty, you must abandon the suit (or maybe
duck the trick to retain a major tenace in the suit). If the hand is full, you might
need to cash your trick and find the right switch.
You are probably familiar with the concept of a frozen suit ÔÇö one that no one
can afford to lead without helping the other side.
(a) ´éź K 10 9 (b) ´é¬ 10 4 3
´éź J 8 7 6 ´éź Q 3 2 ´é¬ K 9 8 7 ´é¬ Q 5 2
´éź A 5 4 ´é¬ A J 6
In (a), if declarer leads spades he gets only two tricks. If either defender leads the
suit, his partnerÔÇÖs honor is captured and his own honor can be finessed.
In (b), if the defense leads hearts declarer gets two tricks. If declarer leads
toward the ´é¬10, West plays second hand low and East captures the ´é¬10 with the
´é¬Q. If declarer leads low from dummy, East plays second hand low and West
captures the ´é¬J with the ´é¬K. If dummy leads the ´é¬10, East covers an honor (single
honor) with an honor and declarer still gets only one trick.
A suit that one player, but not his partner, can lead safely I have named a half-
frozen suit. Invariably there is a tenace position that can be led through safely by
one player, but not into by partner.
(a) ´éź A 6 5 (b) ´é¬ K J 3
´éź K 9 8 7 ´éź J 10 3 ´é¬ 10 4 2 ´é¬ Q 9 8 5
´éź Q 4 2 ´é¬ A 7 6
In (a), East, but not West, can lead the suit safely. When East leads the ´éźJ West will
know that declarer has the ´éźQ. In (b) West, but not East, can lead the suit safely.
Note that in both cases the appropriate player is taking a defensive finesse. In the
Some Basic Ideas * 35

---

second example, also, if West leads the two and declarer plays low from dummy,
East must play West for the ten, not the ace.
A suit that can be picked up if you know the location of the cards I call a
guessing suit.
(a) ´éź K J 10 3 (b) ´é¬ K 4 3
´éź 9 8 7 ´éź Q 4 2 ´é¬ 8 7 6 ´é¬ A J 2
´éź A 6 5 ´é¬ Q 10 9 5
East is unlikely to lead either of these suits. In (a) West should be warned by the
presence of the ´éźKJ10 in dummy that ÔÇśbreakingÔÇÖ spades (i.e. leading a suit for the
first time) might solve a guess for declarer. In (b), it is more appealing to try a
defensive finesse, but on this layout it solves a guessing suit for declarer.
The problem for the defense is that it isnÔÇÖt clear in many cases what type of
suit they might be breaking because they canÔÇÖt see declarerÔÇÖs holding in the suit. So
the question becomes, ÔÇśDo I need to risk breaking suits, or can I defend passively?ÔÇÖ
Breaking suits is called an ÔÇśactiveÔÇÖ defense. A lot of times it wonÔÇÖt be clear whether
active or passive defense is called for, but sometimes it is obvious:

Partner leads the ´éźQ to your ace, and declarer plays the ´éź4. What now?
Declarer has the ´éźK, so no more tricks are available there. If a club finesse is
needed it is onside. It is unlikely you can take a trump trick, so declarer will likely
take 1 spade + 5 hearts + 5 clubs = 11 tricks. So even though diamonds is possibly
a frozen suit (say declarer has ´éęK10x), it doesnÔÇÖt matter because declarer can pitch
his diamond losers on the clubs.
Thus, you must try for three diamond tricks by leading the ´éęQ. This is a pusher
finesse, hoping partner has ´éęAJ10(x). I should note that in many bridge problem
books, that would be the end of it. Real-life deals are often more complicated. For
example, it is just possible that declarer has this hand:
´éź K 4 ´é¬ J 9 5 4 3 ´éę A J 2 ´éĘ K 7 3

There is some appeal in a switch to a minor suit, but there is no reason to worry
about losing your tricks. Left to his own devices, declarer must lead from his hand.
Best to continue hearts to clarify that suit, with trumps as a reserve ÔÇśexitÔÇÖ (safe play).

Note that a lead of either minor risks letting him take his tenth trick right away. If
you defend carefully you will always beat 4´éź with passive defense.
So ends Chapter 1, a brief run through many of the basic principles of cardplay.
I shall address exceptions to these principles in later chapters, but for now letÔÇÖs
move on to look at some more advanced maneuvers. Again, you will probably
be familiar with at least some of the ideas, but stay with me ÔÇö I am laying an
important foundation for the good stuff that is coming later.

Chapter 2
the BAsIcs oF AdvAnced cArdPlAy
WeÔÇÖre going to encounter many squeezes and endplays later in the book, so letÔÇÖs
take a look at some of the basics in this chapter.
elIMInatIons and throw-Ins
An endplay, also known as a throw-in or strip, simply means putting an opponent
in and forcing a favorable lead. For this to work, the opponent must be forced to
lead a frozen suit (or a half-frozen suit, when appropriate), or, in a trump contract,
give you a ruff and sluff.
(a) ´éź 6 5 4 (b) ´é¬ 7 5 4
´éź K J 9 ´éź 8 7 2 ´é¬ K 10 3 ´é¬ J 8 2
´éź A Q 10 ´é¬ A Q 9
In (a), only three cards are left to play. Best is to lead from dummy to your ´éź10,
forcing West to lead the half-frozen suit back into your tenace. In (b), leading low
to the ´é¬9 is just as effective. Even if the ´é¬K is onside, thereÔÇÖs no need to put in the
´é¬Q; the endplay will work just as well.
In position (b), East might try putting up the ´é¬J but it wonÔÇÖt help; you cover
with the queen and West must lead from his ´é¬10 into your A-9 tenace. Change the
suit a little, however, and second hand can save partner from an endplay.
(a) ´éź 10 4 3 (b) ´é¬ K 4 2
´éź K 8 7 ´éź J 9 2 ´é¬ Q 10 3 ´é¬ A J 9
´éź A Q 6 ´é¬ 8 6 5
In (a), when dummy leads a low spade, East must insert the ´éź9, otherwise declarer
plays the ´éź6 to endplay West. Instead, if it goes nine, queen, king, then leading the
´éź8 back traps dummyÔÇÖs ´éź10. In (b), when declarer leads the ´é¬5, West must insert
38 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

the ´é¬10 (the ´é¬Q will do also) to prevent a duck into partner. Note that these are
exceptions to ÔÇśsecond hand lowÔÇÖ, plays you wouldnÔÇÖt normally make unless it was
clear an endplay was looming.
Some of these endplays ÔÇśplay themselvesÔÇÖ, but in most cases you have to set up
the position. This means taking out the defendersÔÇÖ safe exits in other suits.

´é¬ A K 3

´éę A Q 9

West leads the ´é¬J against 6´éź and you win the ace. You lead the ´éźK from dummy
and both follow. Now what?
You have eleven top tricks and must get a twelfth from diamonds. Simply
draw trumps and cash the rest of the heart and club winners, ending in dummy.
Then lead a diamond to the nine (if East plays the ´éę10 or ´éęJ, cover with the ´éęQ).
West can win cheaply but must either lead a diamond back into your tenace or give
you a ruff and sluff.
Since success is guaranteed if you time the play correctly, I call this a lock strip:
strip the hand and you are a ÔÇślockÔÇÖ (100%). In many cases, though, the defense will
have a chance to stop an endplay.

If you donÔÇÖt return a diamond, declarer can try for an endplay when trumps are 2-2.
He starts with two top clubs and a club ruff. Now he draws two rounds of trumps
ending in dummy, ruffs the last club, ruffs the spade, and then leads a diamond
to the jack, endplaying partner. Your play at Trick 2 allows partner to win the
diamond at a point where he can exit safely (as it happens, in any other suit).
Declarer can sometimes use ducking or avoidance plays to prevent a critical

last club with your ´é¬A and cross back to dummy with another trump.

that you arranged to have the high trump in dummy ÔÇö you can win this trick and
still try a finesse of the ´éęQ.

Strips can also work in notrump, but since there is no possibility of a ruff and
sluff you have to extract all the possible exit cards. This is sometimes done by
ducking tricks that have to be lost in any event.

squeeze PlaY
A squeeze involves forcing a fatal discard from one or both opponents. There are
many variations but I will discuss here only the most common ones. A squeeze
against only one opponent is called a simple squeeze:

will help us see what the necessary elements are.

squeezes only work when you have all the tricks but one, i.e. you have only one

You lead the ´éęA but West only has to protect spades, so he pitches the ´é¬8. The
only hand getting squeezed is dummy. So if the squeeze is to work, one opponent
must guard both threat suits by himself.
Clyde Love, in his classic Bridge Squeezes Complete1, used the acronym BLUE
to describe the necessary conditions:
B = Both threats must be held by a single defender.
L = Loser count must be rectified, i.e. you must be down to only one loser.
U = U pper hand. At least one threat must lie over the defender to be
squeezed.
E = Entry. In at least one of the threat suits, you must have a winner plus a
threat card in one hand and a small card in the opposite hand.
If you review the ÔÇśsqueeze doesnÔÇÖt workÔÇÖ examples above, you will see that in each
case one of the BLUE conditions isnÔÇÖt met, which is why they fail.
HereÔÇÖs another common squeeze position:
´éź A J
´é¬ 2
´éę ÔÇö
´éĘ ÔÇö
´éź 2
´é¬ K
´éę A
´éĘ ÔÇö
1. Revised and updated second edition (2010) published by Master Point Press.
The Basics of Advanced Cardplay * 45

This time the threats, the ´éźJ and ´é¬K, are split between your two hands. This
position is much more flexible, because when you lead the ´éęA you can discard
the small heart from dummy. If either opponent has sole control of the majors,
he is squeezed. This type of position is called an automatic squeeze, since either
defender can be squeezed. The original position is called a positional squeeze since
only the defender in front of the menaces can be squeezed.
LetÔÇÖs look at a full deal involving a squeeze:
´éź A J 3
´é¬ A K 6 5
´éę K Q 3
´éĘ A K 3
´éź 8 2
´é¬ Q 4 3
´éę A J 10 4
´éĘ Q J 9 8
You arrive in 7NT and West leads the ´éźK. How do you play?
You have twelve top tricks. If hearts are 3-3 you will always make the hand,
but bridge is all about giving yourself extra chances beyond the most obvious.
The only other hope is a squeeze in the majors. It would have to be against West
because both menaces, the ´éźJ and the ´é¬6, are in the dummy. You are well on
your way because presumably West has the ´éźKQ, giving him exclusive control
of spades. You are going to assume that he also has exclusive heart control, since
otherwise it doesnÔÇÖt matter how you play.
LetÔÇÖs check out BLUE. B is taken care of by assumption (West has sole control
of both majors). L is taken care of also, as you have twelve of the thirteen tricks
on top, so just one loser. U is taken care of, as both of your threats lie over WestÔÇÖs
squeeze stoppers. E you have to be careful about, and this is where most who
arenÔÇÖt familiar with squeeze play would go wrong. West has already knocked out
the entry in spades so the heart entry must be preserved.
Best is to win the ´éźA and cash four rounds of a minor (say diamonds), pitching
the low spade from dummy. Now you cash the other minor. If West doesnÔÇÖt pitch
the ´éźQ, you pitch the ´éźJ from dummy and try to run the hearts. Note that you
must have at least one top heart left in dummy after running the minors or you
wonÔÇÖt have an entry to dummy. So you must be patient and try to run hearts only
at the end of the hand.
If you have two losers, you want to try to duck a trick if you can to rectify the
loser count to one. Suppose you are in 6NT with these hands:

Another common squeeze position is the double squeeze, where both
opponents are squeezed:

Here there are four cards left and South has three winners, so L is fine. He cashes

´éĘK becomes the squeeze card. Love termed the club suit the free suit; on this hand
the other three suits are threat suits. Spades are a threat against both opponents.
It isnÔÇÖt necessary to have full comprehension of squeeze play to execute one of
the basic types. Just follow a few rules:
1. Try to rectify the Loser Count to 1.
2. Keep communication in possible threat suits.
3. Be sure to cash the last winner in the free suit before fatally cashing winners
in threat suits.
4. Make a mental note of which cards you need to look for so you know if a
threat has become a winner.
This type of situation is common. Say you are playing 4´éź on this deal in a
matchpoint game, where overtricks matter:
´éź Q J 3
´é¬ A 6 5
´éę A K 4 3
´éĘ 7 6 5
´éź A K 10 9 4
´é¬ 7 4 2
´éę Q 5 2
´éĘ A J

You have no suits you can develop ÔÇö itÔÇÖs always good to check that first
because a squeeze is not the best play on most hands. You have ten top tricks so
you should duck the ´é¬Q. West continues a heart to the ace, and after drawing
trumps you give up the other heart, to get L down to one. East wins and returns
the ´éĘK, giving you hope.
Now, just play off your last two trumps, pitching clubs from dummy. If East
has the ´éĘKQ and four or more diamonds, he is squeezed. All you have to look for
is a discard of the other high club; if you donÔÇÖt see it, try to run diamonds. Either
it works or it doesnÔÇÖt.
Note that you followed my four simple rules: you ducked two hearts to rectify
L, you ran all your spades, you kept the diamond threat suit entries in place, and
you noted that you had to watch for the ´éĘQ. Do those things and the hand plays
itself. Notice that the defense could have prevailed if West had switched to a club
at Trick 2. Then the defenders could ÔÇśkillÔÇÖ the club menace. If you run your tricks
there is no squeeze because you still have two losers. This is not easy defense,
however.
Another common squeeze type is the strip squeeze, so called because it
involves both a squeeze and a strip. These are easier to find when the bidding has

West surely has the ´é¬A and five spade winners, and likely has the ´éĘK also.

Love called this type of squeeze a surplus winner squeeze because you squeeze
the defender out of the extra winners you canÔÇÖt afford to let him cash. Note that for
squeezes where you are going to lose later trick(s), the usual L rules do not apply.
On this hand you had five losers less one for the Trick 1 duck, or a total of four
losers when the squeeze operated. In fact, you need at least two losers for strip
squeezes, since you plan to lose a trick later.
This squeeze is also called a triple squeeze or three-suit squeeze because West
has assets in three suits (his spade winners are crucial as well as his ´é¬A and ´éĘK).

That spade suit is called an extended menace or a two-trick menace because
giving up the squeeze stopper in that suit gives you two extra tricks. If you only
had one potential extra spade trick (say dummy started with ´éźAK9 alone), West
could get out of the second trick gain by giving up his spade stopper. The second
squeeze would fail because both minor-suit menaces lie under West.
Again, this chapter has been a brief look at some of the basic principles of how
squeezes and endplays operate. It should help readers to understand many of the
more complex positions described later in this book.

Chapter 3
tools For AnAlyzIng A hAnd
Beginners are taught that the most important thing to do as declarer is to make a
plan. Yet they have difficulty doing so, even with the limited number of tactics
available to them. The more advanced player must juggle many more possibilities,
as weÔÇÖve begun to see in the first two chapters. How, then, does the expert player
approach the task of choosing a line of play from the many weapons in his arsenal?
LetÔÇÖs again look at the key elements, in this case the analytical tools that you have
to work with.
countIng losers
The most important thing to me is simple: count both winners and losers. This way
you will know how close you are to making a contract (winners) or being set
(losers). Say West leads the ´é¬Q against your 4´éź contract on this layout:
´éź K 4 3 2
´é¬ A 7 6
´éę K Q J
´éĘ 8 6 5
´éź Q J 10 9 6
´é¬ K 8 3
´éę 7 6
´éĘ K Q J
By promoting high cards, you can get four trump tricks, two hearts, two diamonds
and two clubs for ten tricks. So if you only went that far you might decide just to
knock out the ace of trumps. However, you are in danger of losing a heart trick as
well as the three aces you must lose. A count of four potential losers implies you
must do something to get rid of one of them, so you must establish a diamond for
a heart pitch. You need to win the heart in hand to save the dummy entry (the
defenders might hold up the ´éęA one round) and lead a diamond to knock out the
ace.
Is this plan foolproof? Certainly not. The unlikely dangers are a 6-1 split in
hearts or clubs, but in that case you might go down even if you led trumps right
Tools for Analyzing a Hand * 53

---

away. More likely problems are 6-2 diamonds, where they kill your winner by
ruffing, or a switch to clubs to secure a ruff when they are 5-2. However, this must
all be risked.
Suppose instead West leads a trump, which is ducked. Now you have the
timing to continue trumps and guarantee ten tricks (barring a very unlikely ruff).
Sometimes you have fewer losers than the number you can afford:
´éź A 7 5
´é¬ K Q 9 3
´éę A K 6 4
´éĘ Q 2
´éź 9 2
´é¬ A J 10 8
´éę Q 5 3
´éĘ A K 7 6
Partner eschews the nearly laydown 6´é¬ and puts you in 6NT. West leads the ´é¬6.
How do you play?
You have eleven winners, and no losers (by that I mean no particular trick
that has to be lost). For example, you could play off the ace, king and queen in
both minors, neither suit splitting, and then lose two minor-suit tricks, or you
could lose them in spades, etc. This is a bit confusing because the same term is
used for squeeze play, where you would say the loser count is two (you have all
but two tricks on top), versus what you might call ÔÇśspecific unavoidable losersÔÇÖ in a
particular suit (of which you have none).
The term ÔÇśloserÔÇÖ is so embedded in bridge literature for both situations that I
propose calling each type of loser something slightly different:
squeeze losers = number of cards left minus the number of top tricks.
hand losers = unavoidable losers in particular suit(s) for the purpose of
selecting a line of play.
In the 6NT hand, you have 2 SL (squeeze losers) but 0 HL (hand losers). Some
time ago I coined the term +L to signify the number of tricks you can afford to give
up at any particular point in the hand. You contracted for twelve tricks so you can
afford to lose one trick, yet you have no HL (hand losers). Thus I would say you
are +1L ÔÇö you have one surplus hand loser.
In the 6NT contract, once you note that you have no suit you can develop (i.e.
promote extra winner(s) by conceding a trick or tricks), counting HL or SL will get
you to the same place: duck a safe trick, which must be in spades since ducking a
minor kills that menace, hoping for a squeeze (or a 3-3 break in a minor).
54 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

It is very important to note +L situations because they open up a whole family
of plays where you can afford to lose the lead for squeezes, endplays, establishing
suits, keeping the dangerous opponent off lead, etc. Here is a 3NT contract from a
match I played the day before writing this chapter:
´éź A 5 4 3
´é¬ A J 3
´éę 8 7 2
´éĘ 9 4 3
´éź J 9 2 ´éź 10 8 6
´é¬ 10 6 2 ´é¬ Q 9 8 Dealer South
Neither Vul.
´éę J 10 6 ´éę K 9 5
´éĘ A 8 7 5 ´éĘ Q J 10 2

1NT
´é¬ K 7 5 4
pass 3NT all pass
´éę A Q 4 3
´éĘ K 6
At the other table, West selected the ´éź2 for his lead, and the first trick continued
low, ten, queen. Declarer now led a heart to the jack and EastÔÇÖs queen. East
thought West had started with ´éźKJxx so she led back a spade. Now the contract
was made. Jeff Meckstroth made a nice falsecard, if you can call it that, by winning
the first spade with the queen when many would win the king.
Against the same contract at the other table, I chose to lead the ´é¬6, so declarer
tried the jack from dummy, which went to EastÔÇÖs queen and declarerÔÇÖs king. Now
he played four rounds of spades, each defender pitching an encouraging club (and
declarer a heart). At this point he has 4 spades, 2 hearts and 1 diamond = 7
winners. He has only 1 HL, the ´éĘA. Thus he is +3L, i.e. he can duck or concede
three more tricks (at least potentially ÔÇö that doesnÔÇÖt mean it is necessarily right to
do so).
The obvious trick source was diamonds so he passed the ´éę8 to my ten. I got
out with the ´é¬10 to dummyÔÇÖs ace. Next he played a diamond to the queen and I
falsecarded with the ´éęJ. He was now up to eight winners. It was time to restock
and recount HL (hand losers). Each trick you duck reduces ÔÇś+LÔÇÖ by 1. Thus, by virtue
of having ducked a diamond, declarer was down to +2L.
Believing that I had led from a four-card heart suit, he decided clubs were 4-4
and placed me with 3=4=2=4 shape. As he was still +2L he was prepared to have
me take two hearts (the ´éĘA was already counted as a top loser). Accordingly, he
exited with a heart. Unfortunately for declarer, partner won this and switched to
the ´éĘQ and down he went.
Why didnÔÇÖt he test the diamonds by cashing the ace? He was so sure I had only
two diamonds that he ÔÇśknewÔÇÖ exiting a heart was a lock strip. If he cashed the ´éęA
first and then played a heart, he would go down if my partner had the ´éĘA because
Tools for Analyzing a Hand * 55

---

she would have a diamond trick to cash at the end (we would make two hearts, two
diamonds, and one club).
Declarer missed several clues here, but it makes a point: the hardest part is
figuring out what to play for. That is, as long as you can do basic analysis. As an
aside, note that neither player on opening lead chose a club, which gives the ninth
trick away immediately.
control units and the establishment race
Another key concept in counting W (winners) and HL is what I call a control
unit. A control unit is very much like a stopper, but it can also be the ability
to trump. Going back to the 4´éź example right at the beginning of this chapter,
West led a heart, leaving you with only 1 control unit in hearts. Leading trumps
then diamonds would mean conceding 2 control units (for the opponents), so the
defenders would win what has been called the ÔÇśestablishment raceÔÇÖ ÔÇö their crucial
trick(s) would be established before yours.
A number of play concepts can be simplified into arithmetic. If you have the
lead and you have 1 control unit (in a crucial suit) and have only 1 control unit to
knock out, you win the establishment race. If they are on lead in this situation, they
win the race if they lead the correct suit.
ÔÇó If you have the lead, you win if control units are equal.
ÔÇó If they have the lead, you need one more control unit than them.
LetÔÇÖs look at control units (CU), along with W and HL, in a 3NT contract.
´éź A 3
´é¬ A Q J 4
´éę Q 10 8 7
´éĘ 9 7 6
´éź K 2
´é¬ 7 6 3
´éę J 9 4 2
´éĘ A K Q J
You are in 3NT and West leads the ´éźQ. How do you play?
You have seven top tricks and can establish two more in diamonds. However,
you have only 1 CU left in spades after winning the opening lead and you have
2 CU to knock out in diamonds. So, you will lose the establishment race if you
work on diamonds. Counting losers, you would lose at least three spades and two
diamonds, too many.
You need two extra winners, and you have a good chance to get them in hearts.
Win the spade in hand and take a heart finesse, making your contract if the heart
56 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

finesse works or hearts are 3-3 (or both!). You might lead the ´é¬A first, catering
to RHOÔÇÖs singleton ´é¬K. This gives up an overtrick if hearts are 3-3 with the king
onside. If you take this ÔÇśsafety playÔÇÖ, you need to save the ´éźA in dummy as a late
entry.
If instead West leads a heart, you can guarantee the contract by winning the
ace, then working on diamonds. Taking a heart finesse is a good play to try to make
overtricks, but it risks going down if East wins the ´é¬K and switches to a spade. The
whole deal might be:
´éź A 3
´é¬ A Q J 4
´éę Q 10 8 7
´éĘ 9 7 6
´éź Q 10 4 ´éź J 9 8 7 6 5
´é¬ 10 8 5 2 ´é¬ K 9
´éę A 5 3 ´éę K 6
´éĘ 10 8 5 ´éĘ 4 3 2
´éź K 2
´é¬ 7 6 3
´éę J 9 4 2
´éĘ A K Q J
Think of it like this: counting W and HL, with the aid of CU, gives you a map, as
I call it, of your position. There are many things you can think about on a bridge
hand, especially on many ÔÇśrealÔÇÖ hands, which are more complex than the carefully
selected deals you find in many books. With this map, you can narrow your focus
of attention, being able to eliminate choices that wonÔÇÖt (or are unlikely to) help and
look for something better.
The concepts of control units and the establishment race need to be understood
in the broader context of the entire deal. Another 3NT contract:
´éź K 3 2
´é¬ Q J 3
Dealer South
´éę A Q J
Neither Vul.
´éĘ J 9 4 3
West North East South
´éź A 7 6 1´éĘ
´é¬ K 9 4 1´éź 2´éź* pass 2NT
´éę 7 6 pass 3NT all pass
´éĘ K Q 10 8 5
West leads the ´éź5 to EastÔÇÖs jack. How do you plan the play?
Tools for Analyzing a Hand * 57

---

If you just casually say, ÔÇśI will lose three spades and two aces if I work on clubs
and heartsÔÇÖ, you might conclude you need the diamond finesse. However, when
you can sever the opponentsÔÇÖ communication you can often stop the running of a
suit. Duck the ´éźJ and then win the second spade. Now knock out the ´éĘA to come
to 2+0+1+4=7 sure tricks. If East has the ´éĘA, you are home because he has no
more spades to lead (you set up the other two tricks you need in hearts).
Should West win the ´éĘA and set up his spades, you will have to guess what
to do.
You can try the diamond finesse, giving up an extra undertrick if it loses and
West has the ´é¬A. If the diamond finesse wins, you can run your tricks and then
decide whether the ´éęK is really onside, or whether you should try to knock out the
´é¬A. If East has both the ´é¬A and the ´éęK and finds the smooth duck of his ´éęK, you
might go down in a makeable hand, but thatÔÇÖs life.
If the hand is a bit different, you can find a lock line of play:
´éź K 5 2
´é¬ A Q 9 3 Dealer South
´éę A 5 Neither Vul.
´éĘ 10 9 5 3

This time the lead is the ´éźQ. You could duck this, but it is simpler to win, planning
to knock out the ´éĘA, getting you up to 2+1+2+3 = 8 tricks. The ´éĘA is WestÔÇÖs
only possible entry and you can take the heart finesse later. Just be sure to duck
the next spade lead before winning the ´éźK, running clubs, and taking the heart
finesse. Once spades are established, West becomes the dangerous opponent and
East becomes the safe opponent. You are simply planning to lose any late trick to
the safe opponent.
countIng wInners
LetÔÇÖs take a closer look at how to count winners:
ÔÇó First count only your top, guaranteed (or virtually guaranteed) tricks.
ÔÇó If you need more tricks, look at the various suits to see where you might
get the needed tricks.
ÔÇó Bear in mind that you need to consider the various problems associated
with your ÔÇśnot on topÔÇÖ tricks, such as:
ÔÇó If you are in a trump contract, you need trump control to take (at least
some of) these tricks.
ÔÇó You need entries to tricks you develop.
58 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

ÔÇó You need to consider whether you might lose the establishment race.
ÔÇó Some of the potential tricks may not be at all certain, depending on the
location of missing honors and/or how the suit splits.
Ideally, you want to find a lock (100% certain) line of play. If one isnÔÇÖt available, you
want the percentage line, the line most likely to succeed. Many times combining
chances in two suits is best, even if you donÔÇÖt know exactly how to calculate the
odds:
´éź K 7
´é¬ A J 8 3
´éę A Q 4 2
´éĘ J 7 6
´éź A 2
´é¬ K 4 2
´éę K 6 5
´éĘ K 8 5 3 2
West leads the ´éźJ against 3NT. Your plan?
You have 2+2+3+0 = 7 top tricks. You will have only 1 CU left in spades, thus
3 HL in spades (at least 3HL, and thatÔÇÖs if spades split 5-4) should you lose the lead
twice. Thus trying to develop clubs is very unlikely to work (you need ace-queen
doubleton onside, and failing that you will have 3+2 = 5 HL, one too many).
If diamonds are 3-3, that will get you up to eight tricks but you still need a
ninth trick from hearts or clubs. If diamonds are not splitting you need two more
tricks and your best shot is to try a club first, hoping the king wins (you revert to
hearts) or that East plays the ace and the queen later falls. If the club play fails you
will need four heart tricks.
The key here is to realize that you need to know how many diamond tricks
you have before deciding whether, or how, to play on the other suits. The hand
is actually tougher if diamonds do split, since you have more options in that case.
Say you play three rounds of diamonds, ending in dummy, and they do split
3-3. These are the cards you have left:
´éź K
´é¬ A J 8 3
´éę 4
´éĘ J 7 6
´éź 2
´é¬ K 4 2
´éę ÔÇö
´éĘ K 8 5 3 2
Tools for Analyzing a Hand * 59

---

The first thing you should do is lead the thirteenth diamond, making them
discard, since it wonÔÇÖt hurt you to pitch a club. Say East throws a spade and West
a club. What now?
You have two basic choices: work on clubs and if that doesnÔÇÖt work try hearts,
or work on hearts only. These are sensible options because you have the possibility
of developing dummyÔÇÖs fourth heart into a trick. If the hearts were AJx in dummy
facing Kxx in your hand, the choice would be clear: lead a club toward your king,
with the heart finesse as your fallback. The issue becomes: is it better to try two
different suits, or to give yourself better odds in one suit?
Leading a club now will work when the ´éĘA is onside or when East has a
singleton ´éĘQ, about 52% in all. (I will discuss odds calculations in more detail
later in the chapter.) However, even if the club finesse loses, you can win the spade
return in dummy, and you can then try the heart finesse. So your overall odds are
about 76%.
If you work on hearts first, you will make if the heart finesse works or the suit
splits 3-3. If the heart finesse loses, a spade comes back and it will be too late to
work on clubs. So, which line is better? We can figure it out the simple way, by
taking out what works on both lines, i.e. the heart finesse. Thus we can compare
the club finesse (52%) with a 3-3 heart split and the heart finesse losing. Even if
we donÔÇÖt know how to calculate the heart suit odds exactly, we know that a 3-3
split is about a one in three chance, so it is worse than the club finesse which here
is a little better than 50%.
Based on the above analysis, you should play clubs first. However, you may
have noticed that there is another possibility: you can cash the ´é¬AK, possibly
dropping a doubleton ´é¬Q, then lead a heart toward the jack. This picks up a
singleton or doubleton queen offside, any position with the queen onside and any
3-3 break. IÔÇÖll return to this hand and finish the discussion later in the chapter.
countIng the hand
Fourth suit forcing is a popular bidding convention, but I use the same term to
help players learn to count the hand. There are many references to hand (and suit)
distributions in this book. To follow the narrative, and be able to visualize the
possibilities yourself, you need to be able to count an opponentÔÇÖs hand.
One of the great symmetries of bridge is that the distribution of each playerÔÇÖs
hand, among the four suits, is four numbers that add up to 13. The same is
true of the distribution of one suit among the four hands. Here are the possible
distributions, either of a hand or of one suit, if no holding is longer than six cards:
4333 4432 4441 5332 5422 5431 5440 5521
5530 6322 6331 6421 6430 6511 6520 6610
60 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

Many times you will be playing or defending and get a count on three of the four
suits or three of the four hands in one suit. As an example:
´éź 8 7 6 4
Dealer South
´é¬ A 9 3
Neither Vul.
´éę 6 4 3
´éĘ A K 4 West North East South
´éź 9 3 1´éź
´é¬ K 2 2´éę 3´éę* pass 4´éź
´éę A K Q 5 2 all pass
´éĘ Q 10 7 6
You cash the ´éęAK (declarer dropping the jack) and continue with the queen,
declarer ruffing. Whenever one of the unseen hands shows out in a suit, you
should know the entire layout of that suit. Stop to assimilate this information.
You know that declarer started with two diamonds. You can also tell yourself
that the original diamond layout was 5-3-3-2 (going clockwise around the table,
starting with your own holding).
Next declarer cashes the ´éźAK, partner discarding a club on the second spade.
Assimilate again: 2-4-1-?, so the fourth number (declarerÔÇÖs spade length) must be
6. The four numbers must add to 13, so the fourth suit (or length in the same suit,
as here) has to be 6. This is what I mean by fourth suit forcing.
So, declarer is 6=?=2=?. Now declarer cashes the ´éĘAK, discarding a heart
from hand on the second club. Assimilate again: declarer is 6=?=2=1, so fourth
suit forcing means he must have 4 hearts! He ruffs dummyÔÇÖs last club in his hand.
What are your thoughts at this point?
IÔÇÖve talked about counting winners and losers. The other type of counting
in bridge involves high card points, distribution and tricks. This enables you to
decide which of the possible layouts is realistic given the bidding. For example, if
someone opens 1NT showing 15-17, you want to play them to be at least close to
that range (say 14-17).
On our example deal, you can count nine tricks for declarer: 6+1+0+2. You
have no possibility of taking any more tricks in any suit but hearts, so you need
two tricks from hearts to set the contract. Declarer has eliminated all the cards in
diamonds and clubs, and his next move is to play ace and another heart. You win
with the ´é¬K to arrive at this position:

´éź 8 7

Declarer has another heart loser, but you are forced to lead a minor suit, giving him
a ruff-and-sluff (or ruff and discard). Declarer ruffs in one hand, discards the heart
loser out of the other, and claims. Do you see what went wrong?
You needed to unblock the ´é¬K by playing it under the ace. Then when a second
heart is led, partner can cash the ´é¬Q and ´é¬J to set the hand. What if declarer has
stronger hearts, say Q10xx? Then your unblocking play will let him make an extra
trick, eleven tricks instead of ten. This doesnÔÇÖt matter much at IMPs, where you are
mainly concerned with setting the contract. At matchpoints, though, it could be
costly and you would have to guess which layout is more likely.
Note how the basic tools of counting and assimilating enable you to do the
real work of an expert, which is visualization. You need to be able to visualize the
following:
ÔÇó either of the unseen hands.
ÔÇó a layout in any particular suit or suits that you need to assume exists for
you to be successful.
The key is to find a solution to your particular problem in the middle of play with
the minimum amount of work (i.e. calculation and visualization). On our example
deal, if you visualize a heart layout where you can get two more tricks, you realize
that you need partner to have the ´é¬QJ due to the ruff-sluff danger if you hang on
to your ´é¬K.
We looked in Chapter 1 at promoting high cards in suits such as Kx facing
QJxx, and now you are visualizing this holding as a defender. When declaring you
played ÔÇśhigh card from the short sideÔÇÖ to unblock the suit (king first, in this case)
and this is what you are doing as a defender, except you donÔÇÖt see what partner
actually has, only what you visualize (assume) he must have for success. If you
realize this then you donÔÇÖt need to go further, i.e. to visualize both unseen hands.
Some deals are harder, of course, and to get the best answer you do need to do
more work. There are many fine books and computer programs that will help you

practice counting and visualizing, and of course you want to try to do it as much
as you can when you play.

´éź K Q 3

You lead the ´éźJ to the queen in dummy and partner plays the two (standard signals).
Declarer runs the ´éęQ to your ace. What do you play next?
When declarer has made a limited bid, such as opening 1NT or 2NT, it is
particularly useful to start by counting his HCP and dummyÔÇÖs, to get an idea of
how many HCP partner can have. I suggest starting with declarerÔÇÖs minimum to
give you partnerÔÇÖs maximum HCP. Here 15+11 (in dummy) = 26, add your 9 HCP:
26+9=35. So partner has at most 5 HCP.
The next part is to ask yourself, ÔÇśWhat can my partner (reasonably) have that
will allow me to set the hand?ÔÇÖ Declarer is marked with the ´éźA (partner would win
Trick 1 and return your suit if he had the ´éźA). If partner has the ´éĘA, that is 4 HCP.
That means declarer has the other important cards: the ´éęK, ´é¬AQ and ´éĘQ. Now,
count his tricks: 3+1+4, and he can develop a ninth trick in clubs. If you want you
can visualize declarerÔÇÖs hand (with respect to his high cards, since you donÔÇÖt have a
count of his length in any suit yet). His hand would look something like:
´éź A x x 3 spade tricks (AKQ)
´é¬ A Q x 1 heart trick (A)
´éę K x x x 4 diamond tricks (5 in dummy missing the ace)
´éĘ Q x x 1 club trick (K or Q)
= 9
DeclarerÔÇÖs club trick is ÔÇśslowÔÇÖ, i.e. he will have to knock out the ´éĘA. However, he
has two spade stoppers left and you canÔÇÖt lead hearts successfully either, so he will
win the establishment race whatever suit you lead next. Thus partner having the
´éĘA is called a losing case. You have to look at other possibilities.
What if partner has the ´éęK? Then he might have the ´é¬Q or ´éĘQ but nothing
else (K+Q = 5 HCP, his maximum). The ´éĘQ wonÔÇÖt help because declarer will have
3+1+3+2 = 9 tricks, but you can perhaps succeed if partner has the ´éęK and the ´é¬Q,
if partner has four hearts. Consider these possible declarer hands:
