Once you get considerable practice, your thinking might go like this: ÔÇś26+9=35,
max of 5. Club ace no good, but ´é¬Qxxx + ´éęK, or ´é¬Axx(x), beats the handÔÇÖ. And
you switch to a heart. So you can learn to be more efficient with your analysis.
On other deals you wonÔÇÖt be able to determine the correct play quite so readily;
in many of these youÔÇÖll just have to make your best guess because you have no solid
information. Even so, these techniques are invaluable, often later in the deal when
you get more information and still have a chance to make a winning play.
assuMe the Best ÔÇö or the worst
Analyzing a hand is easier if you assume it matters what you play. Thus if you can
find a necessary assumption, your task is made a bit easier. Suppose you are in 6´é¬
on either of the deals below:
(a) ´éź A K 3 2 (b) ´éź A 3
´é¬ K 4 2 ´é¬ K 8 7 6
´éę K 3 ´éę A Q 3
´éĘ A Q 10 4 ´éĘ A K 4 3
´éź 8 7 6 ´éź K 5
´é¬ A 8 7 6 3 ´é¬ A J 9 4 3
´éę A J 4 2 ´éę K 9 4
´éĘ K ´éĘ Q 5 2
64 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

In (a), West leads the ´éźQ. You canÔÇÖt make the hand unless trumps split 3-2. Thus
a 3-2 trump split is a necessary assumption, so you can count 2+4+2+3=11 top
tricks. It is easy to get a twelfth trick by ruffing a diamond in dummy. This is
completely safe if you cash the ace and king of trumps first. Once both opponents
follow, cash the ´éĘK, cross to the ´éęK, throw a spade and a diamond on the high
clubs, and you ruff your last diamond in dummy, not caring if they ever ruff in.
I have named this type of line-of-play cash and thrash: cash one or more top
trumps, then thrash, i.e. lead your other winners. Calling it cash and thrash implies
there will be at least one trump out but that you donÔÇÖt mind.
In (b), the lead is the ´éźJ. Again you have to assume only one trump loser but
here the hearts represent a suit combination, a term I will use to mean a suit that
has some possible interest in the way it is played. You have 2+4+3+3=12 tricks.
So, you assume not only that you have at most one trump loser, but also that it
matters how you play to lose only one trump trick. This is easy if trumps are 2-2
or 3-1 so again, assume they are 4-0. Can you cater to either 4-0 trump break?
The answer is yes, using a safety play. Doing an asset survey, you are missing
´é¬Q1052 (realizing that the ´é¬5 is not important, you can say ´é¬Q10xx). By leaving
the ´é¬K87 in one hand and the ´é¬J9xx in the other, you can pick up Q10xx on either
side for one loser, so lay down the ´é¬A first. If trumps are 4-0, one defender will
show out and you play accordingly.
a short Math Break
Once you understand the various concepts we have been discussing, it is good to
know how to put them together. There are some techniques for calculating the
odds that help you do this. If this scares you, feel free to skip this section on first
reading ÔÇö but eventually you are going to have face up to most of it.
LetÔÇÖs start with something simple, such as the odds of a finesse winning.
(a) ´éź A Q 3 (b) ´é¬ A J 10
´éź 6 5 4 ´é¬ 8 6 5
In (a), the odds are 50-50 that the finesse is onside. In (b), assuming you can be
in your hand twice to finesse twice, you just need at least one of the heart honors
to be onside. There are four possibilities: West has no honor, the king, the queen,
or both. So the chances of success are about 75%1 (only West having no honor
is a losing case). The footnote gives slightly more accurate numbers, which take
into account the principle of vacant spaces, explained later in the chapter. ItÔÇÖs
worth noting here, though, that finesse odds can change considerably if you have
information about the layout. For now weÔÇÖll go with the simple odds, which apply
when you have no such information.
1. In fact, West having no honors is a 24% chance, as is the chance that he has both the king and
queen. West will have one specific honor 26% of the time for each (queen or king). So the
double finesse is actually a 76% shot.
Tools for Analyzing a Hand * 65

---

It is good to know the approximate percentages for basic splits. Most of the
time, you are missing 4, 5, 6 or 7 cards in a suit combination.
You have They have Split Approx. Frequency
9 cards 4 cards 2-2 40%
3-1 50%
4-0 10%
8 cards 5 cards 3-2 68%
4-1 28%
5-0 4%
Notice that when you are missing an even number of cards, 4, the probability that
they split as evenly as possible, 2-2, is less than 50%. The opposite is true when
you are missing an odd number of cards: the 3-2 split of 5 cards will happen about
two-thirds of the time.
Why? When you are missing an odd number of cards, a 3-2 split means that
either LHO has 3 and RHO has 2, or the other way around. When an even number
cards are missing, there is no ÔÇśdouble countingÔÇÖ since each opponent has the same
number.
LetÔÇÖs show this specifically:
´éź A 10 9 7 6
´éź K 8 5 4
You are missing: QJ32
LHO RHO
Q J 3 2 ÔÇö Two 4-0 splits.
ÔÇö Q J 3 2
Q J 3 2 Eight 3-1 splits (four 3-1, four 1-3).
Q J 2 3
Q 3 2 J
J 3 2 Q
Q J 3 2
J Q 3 2
3 Q J 2
2 Q J 3
Q J 3 2 Six 2-2 splits.
Q 3 J 2
Q 2 J 3

---

There are eight 3-1 splits (including 1-3 and 3-1) versus only six 2-2 splits. This
is why a 3-1 split is more likely. Note also that there are 2+8+6 = 16 possible
distributions of the opponentsÔÇÖ cards when you are missing 4 cards. LetÔÇÖs look at 5
cards missing: this time you are missing QJ432:
LHO RHO
Q J 4 3 2 ÔÇö Two 5-0 splits
ÔÇö Q J 4 3 2
Q J 4 3 2 Ten 4-1 splits. The five where LHO has 4
Q J 4 2 3 cards are listed: just reverse the holdings
Q J 3 2 4 so that RHO has 4 cards to get to ten.
Q 4 3 2 J
J 4 3 2 Q
Q J 4 3 2 Twenty 3-2 splits. The other ten are
Q J 3 4 2 the same but reversed.
Q J 2 4 3
Q 4 3 J 2

J 4 2 Q 3
J 3 2 Q 4
4 3 2 Q J
That gives us 20+10+2=32 possible splits, twice as many as when 4 cards are
missing. In fact this is the pattern:
1 card missing: 2 cases
2 cards missing: 4 cases
3 cards missing: 8 cases
4 cards missing: 16 cases
5 cards missing: 32 cases
6 cards missing: 64 cases
etc.
2n (read ÔÇś2 to the nth powerÔÇÖ) = the number of possible layouts when n cards are missing.
So, you can get a rough estimate of the odds of a particular split by enumerating the
cases that work, and dividing that into the total number of possible layouts. For
example, the probability that LHO has all 4 missing cards is 1 in 16 ÔÇö there are 16
layouts, and only 1 of them counts. The probability that LHO has exactly 2 cards
when 5 are missing is 10 (1/2 of the 3-2 splits) in 32.

Counting Combinations
Is there a way, other than by counting each specific case, to count relevant holdings?
Suppose you have this trump suit, and need to play it for no losers (!):
´é¬ A K 7 4 2
´é¬ 10 9 3
You need to ask yourself two questions:
ÔÇó Which of the possible splits matter for your play?
ÔÇó How many of the cases win for each play option?
This is a fairly simple position, so itÔÇÖs not hard to figure out. You can play for QJx
onside with 8x offside (run the ten and then the nine), or you can play for either
opponent to have doubleton QJ (cash the ace and king). Those are your only
winning layouts.
Clearly there are two cases of doubleton QJ, one for each opponent. If East
is to have ´é¬8x, we can choose his smaller spot card one of two ways, so there are
two cases here too. In this instance, the math gives us no guidance ÔÇö both lines
of play (cashing the ace, king or finessing) have equal (and pretty small) chances
of success.
LetÔÇÖs look at a more complex example.
´éź K 2
´éź A 10 9 4 3
You need four tricks from this suit, and have a side entry to the South hand. You
play a spade to the king and another back toward hand, seeing all small cards. How
do you play?
Since some cards in the suit have already been played, here you have one more
question to ask yourself:
ÔÇó Which original splits are no longer possible?
At this point, you have these cards left:
´éź ÔÇö
´éź A 10 9 4
68 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

LHO has played a small spade, RHO has played two small spades. So we can
eliminate 6-0 splits and we know LHO does not have five spades (RHO would
show out the second time). We can also eliminate either opponent having QJ
doubleton, and RHO having a doubleton honor or QJx. LHOÔÇÖs stiff honor is also
eliminated. So these initial holdings are still possible:
RHO has two small.
RHO has three small.
RHO has Qxx or Jxx.
RHO has Qxxx or Jxxx.
RHO has QJxx.
RHO has QJxxx.
The next question is, ÔÇśWhich holdings matter?ÔÇÖ RHO having two small doesnÔÇÖt
matter; LHO has two winners regardless. Any 3-3 split doesnÔÇÖt matter either.
RHOÔÇÖs QJxxx doesnÔÇÖt matter because we need four tricks, not three. So we are left
with RHO holding either honor-fourth or QJxx. In the first case you need to rise
with the ace to drop LHOÔÇÖs doubleton honor; in the second case you need to stick
in the ´éź10.
We can enumerate each specific case:
RHO: Q876 Q875 Q865 Q765 J876 J875 J865 J765
RHO: QJ87 QJ86 QJ85 QJ76 QJ75 QJ65
This means the odds are 8 to 6 in favor of going up with the ace.
This is quite an onerous task, so letÔÇÖs seek a shortcut. Note that the spades
could be considered in two groups: the important cards (the Q and J) and the
unimportant cards (the xÔÇÖs). There are four xÔÇÖs, 8765. So we want to count Qxxx
and Jxxx, versus QJxx.
The mathematical technique we need is called combinations2. You have four xÔÇÖs,
so we need to count the combinations of xÔÇÖs for each relevant holding. If a player
has Qxxx, you need to count the number of ways he could have three of the four
xÔÇÖs. Looking at it the easy way, if RHO has three xÔÇÖs, LHO has only one x. It should
be clear that there are four ways for this to happen as LHO can have each specific
missing x. Take a look at the specific cases for Qxxx and confirm that this is true.
If there are four ways to have Qxxx (or Jx on the other side), then there are also four
ways to have Jxxx opposite Qx. Thus there are 4+4 = 8 ways that RHO can have
honor fourth opposite honor doubleton on the left.
If RHO has QJxx, then LHO has xx. This time we need to count the ways that
you can have two of the four xÔÇÖs. If you study the specific layouts above, you will
see that there are six of those. This gives us the 8:6 odds I spoke of.
2. The mathematical formula for combinations is nCr = n!/r!*(nÔÇôr)! In the case of QJxx facing xx,
it is 4C2, or 4*3*2/2*2, or 6 ÔÇö the number of ways to choose 2 items from 4.
Tools for Analyzing a Hand * 69

---

LetÔÇÖs look at this in the context of a full deal. This one came up in a knockout
match I played shortly before writing this section:
´éź K 3 Dealer South

´é¬ J 10 8 4
´éę Q J 9 4

´é¬ A 6 5 3

´éĘ A 5
The lead was the ´éĘ6. With two aces missing I needed to lose only one trump trick,
so I rose ´éĘK and led the ´é¬J. This went king, ace, deuce and now West played the
´é¬7 when I led a second heart from hand. What should I do?
Originally, missing five cards in hearts, there were 32 possible layouts, but
most of them have been eliminated. One that remains, East having ´é¬KQ9, is not
relevant since there is no winning play for me. The holdings that matter are where
West started with ´é¬972, ´é¬Q72 or ´é¬Q972.
The relevant heart holdings East could have are:
´é¬ K Q
´é¬ K
´é¬ K 9
With the first two, playing the ´é¬8 is right; with the last one, playing the ´é¬10 is
right. Note that there are no insignificant cards left, so this is not a combinations
counting exercise per se like the ones we have been considering. It would appear
that the mathematics (two cases to one) tell me to play dummyÔÇÖs ´é¬8; in fact, I
played the ´é¬10, dropping EastÔÇÖs ´é¬9, to make the hand (and win the match by 5
IMPs). Why?
First of all, the 4-1 split, where East has the stiff king, is a little less likely than
either of the others, so the two cases to one donÔÇÖt exactly translate into 2:1 odds.
However, there is a more practical consideration ÔÇö as there so often is. Much of
the time when West has four trumps, I canÔÇÖt make the hand. LetÔÇÖs say the ´é¬8 wins
and East discards a club. If I cross in clubs and lead another trump, West wins and
clears the hearts and I lose trump control. Better is to lead the ´éźK, but a club return
puts me in a similar bind. I would need to knock out the ´éęA and hope both that
there is no diamond ruff and that the defense canÔÇÖt keep leading clubs to advantage.
So I decided to give up on 4-1 trumps and focus on the 3-2 breaks: I would play
East for either K9 or KQ.
70 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

At first sight, it seems that there is nothing to choose between these cases.
However, the ´é¬K and ´é¬Q are touching cards so the Principle of Restricted Choice
comes into play. If East had KQ doubleton she could have played either card, so it
is half as likely that she played the king from that holding. I went with the 2:1 odds
in playing her for K9 rather than KQ, and this time, virtue was rewarded.
Vacant Spaces
I said earlier that the odds of a finesse can vary substantially depending on what
information you have about the hand, for example:
´éź A Q 3
´éź 8 7 6
If you know that West has 5 spades and East 2, then the odds are 5:2 in favor of the
´éźK being onside. The odds that West has any particular spade are 5:2. Similarly,
if East has 4 spades, the odds are 4:3 that the ´éźK is offside.
The rule ÔÇśnine neverÔÇÖ means donÔÇÖt finesse missing four to the queen (unless
someone shows out to mark the finesse):
´éź K 9 4 3
´éź A J 10 8 7
You lead the ´éźA from hand and lead toward dummy, seeing the three small spades.
What are the odds of the finesse versus the drop? You have seen two cards from
West, so he has 11 unknown cards or ÔÇśvacant spacesÔÇÖ; you have seen one card from
East so he has 12 ÔÇśvacant spacesÔÇÖ. Thus the odds are 12:11 in favor of playing for
the drop.
However, if you have other information, the odds can change considerably.
´éź K 9 4 3
´é¬ A 6 5
´éę J 8 7
´éĘ Q 9 3
West North East South
´éź A J 10 8 7
3´éę 3´éź
pass 4´éź all pass
´é¬ 10 7 3
´éę A 2
´éĘ A K 4
Tools for Analyzing a Hand * 71

---

West leads the ´éę3, surely a singleton. You win EastÔÇÖs ´éę9 with the ace and cash the
´éźA, all following low. You lead the ´éźJ and see a low spade again. What are the
odds?
Ignoring the 3´éę opening, the odds are 12:11 in favor of playing for the drop.
Once we know East has 7 diamonds and West one, West has 10 vacant spaces (13
minus 2 spades minus 1 diamond), while East has 5 vacant spaces (13 minus 1
spade minus 7 diamonds). Thus the revised odds are 10:5 (2:1) in favor of taking
the finesse.
Combining Chances
You usually improve your odds when you can combine more than one chance of
success, but not always. The key is how to figure it out. Returning to our 3NT
contract from earlier in the chapter:
´éź K 7
´é¬ A J 8 3
´éę A Q 4 2
´éĘ J 7 6
´éź A 2
´é¬ K 4 2
´éę K 6 5
´éĘ K 8 5 3 2
West led the ´éźQ and you won in hand to save a late entry to possible red-suit tricks,
then tested diamonds and found them 3-3, getting you up to eight tricks. You
cashed the last diamond and paused to calculate the odds of trying the club finesse
then the heart finesse, versus trying for three heart tricks by cashing the ´é¬AK and
leading a third heart up to the jack.
The ÔÇśclubs-then-heartsÔÇÖ line works right away 52% of the time (when clubs
work). Only if the club finesse loses do we then worry about hearts, so we make
when the club finesse loses and the heart finesse wins. So, we are looking at the 48%
of the time a club play doesnÔÇÖt work. In that subset, the heart finesse works half the
time for a total of 24%. Once you get the total for each ÔÇśpieceÔÇÖ, add them together:
52% + 24% = 76%. Here it is in another form to make it easier to understand:
52% clubs work
+ 24% 48% (clubs fail) x 50% (heart finesse wins)
______
= 76%
Note that if you donÔÇÖt take out the 52% where the club play works, when counting
your chances of the fallback plan of leading hearts, you will be double counting
72 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

some winning cases and inflating the chance of success beyond what it really is.
This is the wrong way:
52% clubs work
+ 50% heart finesse wins
______
= 102%
This adds up to more than 100%, so you know it is wrong!
LetÔÇÖs compare the ÔÇśclubs-then-heartsÔÇÖ line with the ÔÇśhearts onlyÔÇÖ line. If you play
hearts to best advantage, ´é¬AK and a third heart up to the jack, you succeed when
West has the ´é¬Q, when hearts are 3-3, or when East has ´é¬Qx. Here are the correct
odds, with no double-counting:
50% West has ´é¬Q
+ 18% 50% East has ´é¬Q x 36% (chance of 3-3 split)
+ 8% 48% (chance of all 4-2 splits) x 50% (East has 2 cards) x 33%
(East has doubleton ´é¬Q when 6 cards are missing)
______
= 76%
Note that there is no double-counting with the doubleton queen offside, since that
case features neither the finesse winning, nor a 3-3 split.
So here is a case where the odds of the two alternative lines of play seem almost
identical! If you got this far, is there anything nitpicky that can tip the scales? Two
things make the heart play a bit better: (1) dummy has the ´é¬8, meaning that if
West has ´é¬109 doubleton you will get three heart tricks, adding about 1%, and (2)
if West has a stiff heart, you will be in the wrong hand but you can at least lead a
club out of your hand, trying for a miracle (stiff ace now flops). That might add
another 1%.
As you see, odds calculations can get quite involved even when the hand itself
isnÔÇÖt that complicated. Still, it is useful to know what the tools are if you want to
try to work it out, even approximately.
On many hands the possibility of dropping an honor, especially in trumps, is a
mirage, in the sense that it wonÔÇÖt be necessary with correct technique. Look at this
next hand from a recent Regional:

a second heart to your king (West plays the ten and the nine). How do you play

You have lost two tricks so you are +1L. You could ruff a club in dummy, then
hope to drop the ´éźQ. If it doesnÔÇÖt drop, however, you will take 4+1+2 (no ´éę10
dropping) + 1 + 1 club ruff = only nine tricks. If you find it easier to look at your
hand (the long trump hand) as the ÔÇśmaster handÔÇÖ, you have four club losers and this
line disposes of only three of them, leaving you with the ´éźQ and the ´éĘQ as losers.
Better is to try to ruff twice in dummy: it seems very unlikely that West has six
clubs, and you have strong spade spots. So ruff a club, cash two diamonds pitching
a club, then ruff a heart high. If West can overruff and return a trump, you play
your ´éź3 under dummyÔÇÖs king and crossruff the rest of the tricks.
The drop mirage arises because dummy has the ´éźAK. If dummy had ´éźKQx
you would play correctly automatically as you would have no shot at dropping the
ace of trumps!
What if you had only ´éźJ10932? Now the crossruff option is risky, but you can
try to set up hearts: ruff a club, cash two diamonds, then ruff a heart with the jack.
If West pitches, you try a spade to the king and ruff another heart high. Once this
lives, cash and thrash (see p. 65) by playing a trump to dummyÔÇÖs ace and pitching
your last club on dummyÔÇÖs good heart.

East follows so there are two trumps out. If WestÔÇÖs ÔÇśodd number of diamondsÔÇÖ is 3,
then he can overruff either red suit. So you have to guess: are trumps now 1-1, in
which case you ruff a heart high to set up the fifth heart then cross to the ´éźA? Or
is West out of trumps, in which case you should try to ruff something (may as well
try a diamond) with the ´éź3 to ruff your last club?
West doesnÔÇÖt have six clubs (East would have overruffed), so if he started with
2-2 in the majors and an odd number of diamonds, he must be 2=2=5=4. If he has
both remaining spades he started with 4=2=3=4 and you canÔÇÖt make the hand. If
he started with three spades he is either 3=2=3=5 or 3=2=5=3.
There are more layouts where ruffing a heart high and trying to split the trumps
works so I think this is the percentage line.
Sorry for the lengthy analysis, but there is much to be learned by comparing
your line of play with slight (but material) variations in the layout.
trIck Packages
This is the final concept of this chapter. I coined the term trick packages to mean
groups of extra tricks from one or more suit(s) that you might combine (if there is
more than one suit) to get to the required number of winners.
Consider this 3NT contract:
´éź K 7 6
´é¬ K Q 10 3
´éę A Q 10 4
´éĘ J 2
´éź A J 8
´é¬ 9 8 4
´éę J 8 3
´éĘ K Q 9 4
West leads the ´éź4 to EastÔÇÖs ´éź10 and your jack. How do you plan to play?
You have 4W (top tricks) and 2HL (top losers) so you are +2L. You also need
to develop five more winners. There are lots of possibilities for the trick packages
that might add up to five tricks:
ÔÇó 3 hearts and 2 clubs.
ÔÇó 2 hearts, 2 diamonds, 1 club.
ÔÇó 1 heart, 2 diamonds, 2 clubs.
ÔÇó 3 diamonds and 2 clubs.
This could get confusing fast. The easiest way is to see what tricks you can get in
each suit for sure, given time (i.e. surviving the establishment race) and see if you
Tools for Analyzing a Hand * 75

---

can make it work. You can develop 1 heart and 2 diamonds and 2 clubs for sure,
so you just have to check for snags.
If you lead a heart to the king and it holds, you then lead the ´éĘJ and, if that
holds, another club to the king. If that holds, you lead the ´éęJ and take at least nine
tricks. If you can see all this quickly, it pays to play fast as the defenders might give
away the position of the high cards by their tempo. Also, if East has the ´é¬Axx he
might well duck, trying to give you a guess if you are missing the jack or to make
communication difficult if you do have the jack. On this hand East does best to
win the ´é¬A and return a spade.
You win the spade in dummy and lead the ´éĘJ, which West, the danger hand
(because of his spade suit) does well to duck. Now if you lead a club to the king
and ace and a third spade comes, you have a problem: if you donÔÇÖt cash the club
you might lose it, but if you do cash the ´éĘK and East has ´éĘ10xxxx and the ´éęK, you
will go down after you finesse the diamond. However, you only lose if West has
ducked the doubleton ´éĘA; if West has more clubs than that, East can have only
one club trick to cash and since you were +2L you can afford to lose the ´éęK and
the ´éĘ10.
If the ´é¬K won the trick and you drove out the ´éĘA in two leads, losing to West
who returned a spade, you have a lock if you win in dummy and lead a diamond
toward your jack. You no longer have to worry about cashing the ´éĘQ or losing it.
Fortunately, on complex hands like this you donÔÇÖt have to foresee all the
possible variations at Trick 1. Just work on the suits in the logical order. You want
to finesse the diamond last, into the safe opponent, knocking out aces first or taking
your tricks there if they duck their ace(s). You hope you need only one heart trick,
and you want to lead toward your high cards, so while in hand it is natural to play
a heart first.
Note that the principle of trick packages has validity even if we modify the
hand to make it less of a sure thing, for example by giving dummy the ´éę9 instead
of the ´éę10. You still hope to take two extra diamond tricks but it is far from certain
missing the ´éę10. You can start the same way, though, leading a heart toward
dummy and seeing what happens. Sometimes East will win the ace and return a
club from A10xxx for example. If the play does go the same awkward way (East
wins the ´é¬A and returns a spade; West ducks one club then wins the second and
clears his five-card spade suit), then you are left with these cards:
´éź ÔÇö
´é¬ Q 10 3
´éę A Q 9 4
´éĘ ÔÇö
´éź ÔÇö
´é¬ 9 8
´éę J 8 3
´éĘ Q 9
76 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

Restocking, you still are +2L but West has established two spade tricks so you canÔÇÖt
let him have the lead. You have developed three of the five tricks you need so you
can do a restocking trick package, if you will. You could hope to get the two extra
tricks you need from:
ÔÇó 2 heart tricks.
ÔÇó 2 diamond tricks.
ÔÇó 1 club trick and 1 red trick (both would be assured with finesses into the
safe opponent). The ´éĘ10 would need to drop to get another club trick.
ÔÇó 1 heart and 1 diamond trick.
To try to get two heart tricks, cash the ´éĘQ, throwing a diamond, and run the ´é¬9.
This will work for sure if the finesse wins. If the finesse loses and East cashes the
´éĘ10, you will almost certainly go down if he has a heart to exit with. Dummy will
be endplayed, and youÔÇÖll need to find a stiff ´éęK (you have lost four tricks already).
To try to get two diamond tricks, cash the ´éĘQ throwing a heart from dummy,
then lead the ´éęJ. If West has the ´éęK you want to stop it from taking a trick. If
you do an asset survey you see that you have the ´éę9 and ´éę8, so once the jack is
covered you can drive out the ´éę10 and get the two extra diamond tricks you seek.
The problem will arise if West has ´éęK10x(x) as he is the dangerous opponent. Of
course if East has the ´éęK, you need to drop the ´éę10 later.
To try for one extra heart trick, you could lead to dummyÔÇÖs ´é¬10, with the idea
that if it loses you have the ´é¬9 to get back to hand to try the diamonds. Possibly
losing a trick to the ´é¬J is a problem because if East can get the ´éĘ10 and the ´é¬J, you
need the diamond finesse.
My instinct would be to take the ´éĘQ, pitching a heart, and lead the ´éęJ. Most of
the time West wonÔÇÖt duck a doubleton ´éĘA. Here are the possible diamond layouts:

Of the 56 (out of 64, which is 26) cases that are at all likely (forget the two 6-0 splits,

´éęK10xx, not all is lost. The original deal might be:

for 9+ tricks). If East pitches a diamond, then you can set up a third diamond

and ´éĘ10.
I know this ordinary-looking hand got quite involved, but that is what many
ÔÇśrealÔÇÖ bridge deals are like. At least you are now armed with some of the basic

Chapter 4
mAkIng your PlAn
the BasIcs
We are now ready to face that moment when the opening lead has been made, the
dummy hits the table, and you begin to make a plan for the play. There are four
things you should do once the opening lead has been made and dummy has been
revealed.
1. read the position
Your first step is to make a preliminary analysis. Study the lead, and see what
information it gives you. The possibilities are too many and varied to try to list
them all here, but some obvious examples are:
ÔÇó If the opening lead against notrump is from a four-card suit, LHO almost
certainly has nothing longer.
ÔÇó If you are missing the ace and king of a side suit in a trump contract, and
neither is led, then the opening leader probably does not have them both.
ÔÇó An opponent who preempts and then leads a trump is unlikely to have
shortness in one of the other side suits.
Likewise, you can take inferences from the bidding about the probable distribution
and placement of high cards ÔÇö remembering that actions not taken are sometimes
as telling as calls that were actually made.
Later in the hand, too, it may be appropriate to go through this process again,
in light of the play to date.
Making Your Plan * 79

---

Take a look at this deal from the final of the 2004 U.S. Team Trials:

Agreeing clubs after a transfer sequence, Rosenberg and Zia found themselves off

took the ´éęA and returned the ´éę8, which declarer won with the ´éęK. Postponing
the play of the critical club suit, declarer now cashed his spade tricks and on the
third round of the suit West pitched the ´é¬5, following that with the ´éę6 on the next
round. How should declarer tackle clubs?
WestÔÇÖs opening lead (ignoring his partnerÔÇÖs double) suggested that he must be
short in hearts and long in diamonds, and he was known to have only two spades.
That appeared to mark him with length in clubs and even if he held only three he
was still a favorite to hold the jack. Declarer cashed the ´éĘQ and then played a club
to the ten. When he opened his eyes, he discovered the ten had held the trick.
On Board 95 out of 96 in the final of the 2001 Venice Cup in Paris, Daniela von
Arnim took a similar inference to secure a dramatic win for Germany.

Perhaps not surprisingly, West failed to find the killing club lead, tracking a
normal-looking ´éź7. DummyÔÇÖs ´éźK held, and East played the ten. Declarer tested
diamonds, and on the third round East pitched the ´é¬2, a very suggestive card.
With a good seven-card suit, East would surely have bid clubs or even doubled the
final contract. With five or six clubs she might well have pitched one rather than a
heart, especially looking at four hearts in the dummy. So at this stage West looks
to have started with six spades, three diamonds and probably two or three clubs.
She thus has two unknown cards to EastÔÇÖs five (East started with two spades, two
diamonds, and at least four clubs). Declarer therefore cashed the last diamond
and played a heart to the nine. That was worth 10 IMPs (the contract in the other
room was 3´éę by South, making four). Board 96 was a push, and Germany won the
match by 2 IMPs.
Technically, once East discards on the third diamond there is a case for cashing
a top heart, just in case West has a singleton jack. The fourth diamond is the entry
for the last heart after the winning finesse.

1. Keycard ask in hearts.
´éĘ Q 10 7 6
Gavin Wolpert led the ´éźJ. Jeff won with dummyÔÇÖs ace and cashed the ´é¬A, North
following with the jack. The Principle of Restricted Choice suggested that declarer
should come to hand and finesse in hearts, but there were two additional factors
to consider. First, North was known to have a significant number of spades, but
had not taken any action: that argued against him holding a singleton heart. By the
same token, South was unlikely to be long in hearts given his known spade length.
Accordingly, Jeff cashed the other top heart and felled NorthÔÇÖs queen. Now he had
a lock strip: he could simply play four rounds of diamonds, putting North on play
and forcing either a lead into the club tenace or a ruff and discard.
Making Your Plan * 81

---

2. Make a map
Step 2, mapping, involves considering your basic position using the tools described
in Chapter 3. How many winners and losers do you have? Can you afford to
lose more tricks of one type or another (and thus consider ducking something)?
Mentally list your potential trick packages. Think about the control unit situation.
And so forth.
The higher the contract, the simpler this calculation becomes, as in this
example from the 1997 Bermuda Bowl:
´éź 9 8 6
´é¬ 7
´éę A Q J 6 5 3
´éĘ 10 6 3 Dealer West
Neither Vul.
´éź A K ´éź Q J 7 2
´é¬ A K Q 6 4 ´é¬ J
West North East South
´éę 8 2 ´éę K 10 7 4
Meckstroth Helness Rodwell Helgemo
´éĘ A J 8 7 ´éĘ Q 5 4 2 1´éĘ1 3´éę 3NT pass
´éź 10 5 4 3 6NT all pass
´é¬ 10 9 8 5 3 2
1. Precision.
´éę 9
´éĘ K 9
South led the ´éę9 and North took the ace and returned the queen, South discarding
a heart. It was easy to see that I had four tricks in spades, four or five in hearts, one
in diamonds and one in clubs. So, regardless of what happened in the heart suit, I
was going to need the club finesse.
That decided, I won the ´éęK, and played a club to the jack, noticing that South
had played the ´éĘ9. When that held, I unblocked the top spades and returned to
hand with a heart to cash two more spade winners. On the fourth round South
was still following, and I was at a decision point. If the hearts were breaking, I
needed to pitch a club from dummy, and if they were not, I was going to have to
play South for ´éĘK9.
SouthÔÇÖs early discard of a heart persuaded me to play for the suit to be 6-1, so
I guessed right. Next time I might choose to defend 3´éę doubled, which goes for
at least 1100.
3. go over the basic options
On many deals declarer will have to consider three things: his offensive potential
in each suit, the various threats available to the defense (such as ruffing, leading
trumps to cut down ruffs, leading through tenaces and high cards, knocking out
entries or simply cashing enough winners to defeat the contract) and the possibility
82 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

of adopting a passive ÔÇśwait and seeÔÇÖ strategy. One way to remember these factors is
to use the acronym ODP: O ÔÇö Offensive Potential, D ÔÇö Defensive Threats, P ÔÇö
Passive Strategy.
This deal from the 2009 Bermuda Bowl illustrates the merits of a passive
approach by declarer:

West led the ´éźA and continued the suit. With the spade suit already eliminated, Jeff
was happy to postpone playing either minor and at Trick 3 he played the ´é¬Q from

dummy with a spade, ruffed a diamond and exited with a club. Sooner or later

no longer have the entries to eliminate the diamonds and you have just turned a
100% proposition into a 50-50 one.
4. analyze
Although many deals simply depend upon the play in a single suit, declarer will
frequently have to envisage the various combinations of extra tricks that might be
available from two or even three suits in ÔÇśtrick packagesÔÇÖ. This will of necessity
have to include keeping an eye open for possible snags and pitfalls before coming
up with the best idea.
On this deal from the 2010 NEC Cup, declarer had to consider how best to
combine his chances in two suits:
´éź Q 9
´é¬ A Q 10 8 7
´éę A K 6 5 4

West led a trump and dummyÔÇÖs nine was allowed to win. When declarer played a
club, East went up with the ace, cashed the ´éźA and went back to clubs. Declarer
won in hand and drew the outstanding trump. Perhaps deciding that West must
have something for his raise, declarer now took the heart finesse. East won and the
defenders could cash a club trick for one down.
Clearly declarer does much better to play a heart to the ace and then ruff a
heart. He crosses to dummy with a diamond and ruffs a third heart. When the
king falls he can claim, but even if it does not he still has chances in diamonds.

deFoggIng ÔÇö More on the thought Process
With luck, a ÔÇśsure-trickÔÇÖ answer will occur to you without having to go through all
these various stages. They are designed to allow you to see a solution early, if one
is available, without missing something important. I consider this to be a proper
and acceptable sequence in which to consider things. However, life isnÔÇÖt always
that straightforward.
Before I move on to discuss technical plays, the subject of much of the rest of
this book, I want to suggest a number of questions that you can ask yourself to help
with any unresolved issues in your analysis. While we are still primarily thinking
about the start of play here, you will find many of these questions useful in the
middle of the hand too.
Making Your Plan * 85

---

1. What are my options and what are the implications of each one?
Many mistakes are made because a certain option was never considered. By
considering the implications of each, you hope to unlock the secret of why one
should be selected over another. For example, a player might reason, ÔÇśI could duck
a round of trumps in order to maintain control, but only if the opening lead was
not a singletonÔÇÖ, etc.
Before embarking on a finesse, you always want to ask yourself, ÔÇśWhat will
happen if it loses?ÔÇÖ If the answer is something catastrophic, then you should spend
some time trying to find alternative options. Look at this deal:
´éź A
´é¬ A 7 6 4
´éę A Q 9 8
´éĘ K Q 9 5
´éź K 10 9 8 4 3 2 ´éź Q J 6 5 Dealer North
EW Vul.
´é¬ J ´é¬ Q 10 9 8
´éę 7 6 4 3 ´éę K J 5 2
West North East South
´éĘ 2 ´éĘ 8 1´éę pass 2´éĘ
´éź 7 3´éź 4´éź* pass 5´éĘ
´é¬ K 5 3 2 pass 6´éĘ all pass
´éę 10
´éĘ A J 10 7 6 4 3
West led the ´é¬J, and when declarer won with dummyÔÇÖs ace, East followed with the
´é¬Q. Declarer played a club to the jack and could see that provided the hearts were
3-2 there would be no problem. He cashed the ´é¬K, but West discarded a spade.
One possibility now was to play a diamond to the queen, hoping to discard a heart
on the ´éęA. If that failed, however, that would be that.
Declarer looked a little more deeply into the position and realized he did not
need to risk the diamond finesse. Instead he played a spade to the ace to eliminate
the suit, cashed the ´éęA and followed that with the ´éęQ. If East had followed with a
low card, declarer was going to discard a heart. If West won the trick he would have
to return a diamond, ensuring a diamond trick for dummyÔÇÖs ´éę98, or surrender a
ruff and discard. When East covered the ´éęQ, declarer ruffed, went back to dummy
with a trump and played the ´éę9, again intending to discard if East played a small
card. East covered, declarer ruffed and returned to dummy one last time to discard
a heart on the established ´éę8.
2. Does the Control Unit situation dictate any particular line?
A control unit is a card that protects you and allows you to win the lead. (This
idea was discussed in Chapter 3.) For example, if you are declaring, you have (one
hopes) a high-card stopper in the suit they attack. If you are in a trump contract,
86 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

your remaining trumps also serve as control units. For the defenders, control units
are the cards that declarer must knock out. So, if declarer has two control units and
the defenders have two control units, the defenders will win the establishment race
since they have the lead (assuming they lead the correct suit).
So, an analysis of control units can lead declarer to take evasive measures (a
holdup play, avoidance, knocking out the entry in the danger hand first, etc.) when
needed. If declarer is ahead in the control units game, he is fine as long as he
doesnÔÇÖt squander them.
´éź J 10 9 8 3
´é¬ J 10 7
´éę K Q 10 4 3
´éĘ ÔÇö Dealer East
NS Vul.
´éź K ´éź A Q 7 6 5 4
´é¬ 6 5 4 ´é¬ 8 3
West North East South
´éę A 9 7 6 5 2 ´éę J 1´éź1 2´éź2
´éĘ 9 5 4 ´éĘ A J 3 2 pass 2NT* 3´éź 4´é¬
´éź 2 all pass
´é¬ A K Q 9 2
1. Precision, 11-15.
´éę 8
2. Four hearts and a longer minor.
´éĘ K Q 10 8 7 6
West leads the ´éźK and switches to a trump. What are your prospects?
You can only get seven trump tricks by ruffing losers in dummy, so you must
establish a minor suit. It is tempting to lead a diamond but unless West (a very
strong player) takes the ace, the hand will be difficult to play (you are sure to need
a winning guess in the club suit to get home). The sounder plan is to try to set
up the clubs. This will require a 3-2 trump break and probably a favorable club
layout. After winning the trump switch in hand, you ruff a club and draw trumps.
If hearts turn out to be 4-1 you will have to play the ´éĘK and hope for the best.
When they prove to be 3-2, you still have two control units and can afford to play
a diamond before going on with the club suit.
If West ducks, you win in dummy and can now take on East. A spade from
dummy forces a cover and you ruff and play a top club. One way or another you
must come to ten tricks. If West goes in with the ´éęA, the fall of EastÔÇÖs jack simplifies
matters. If East instead were to follow with a low diamond, West would continue
with a diamond, forcing you to guess what to do in the suit. It might be right to
put in the ten, playing West to have started with ´éęAJxxxx (when cashing the ´éęA at
Trick 2 before switching to a trump is the winning defense) but keep in mind that
the defenders will often go wrong in this type of situation.

play may vary when he has only one control unit in a suit:

Here the ´é¬A represents declarerÔÇÖs only control unit in the heart suit, and if West
leads a heart then declarer will have to win, draw trumps, eliminate the clubs and
then exit with a heart, hoping to negotiate the diamond suit for no loser.
When West leads a trump, which is what happened here, there is no immediate
threat of a heart loser. Declarer can simply draw trumps and play three rounds of
clubs. Then he cashes the ´éęK and plays a diamond to the ten. Now he has enough
time for two diamond finesses, this line giving him an almost 78% chance of a third
diamond trick.
3. Do I have to do this right now?
Asking yourself this question alerts you to the notion that you might be committing
yourself to the key play prematurely, without testing other options first, or before
getting enough information in non-critical suits to enable you to make your best
guess in the critical suit.
HereÔÇÖs an example from a recent Regional:
´éź A Q J 8 3
´é¬ A Q J Dealer South
´éę 9 7 3 Neither Vul.
´éĘ J 10
West North East South
´éź K 4
1´éę
pass 1´éź pass 2NT
´é¬ K 10 3
pass 6NT all pass
´éę A J 8 4 2
´éĘ A K 3
West leads the ´é¬6 to the jack, and you run four spades, pitching two diamonds
from hand. West pitches a discouraging club, East pitches a heart. What now?
88 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

You could believe the discard and finesse the club, but you have no chance to
make if it loses. Try instead playing a combination diamond finesse, by leading low
to the ´éę8. If this loses to the ten, you can still finesse the club.
If West wins the diamond with the king or queen, then the odds are about 2 to
1 in favor of the next diamond finesse. I (West) actually had ´éęKx and ´éĘQxxxx so
the suggested line would have worked.
Of course, it is just possible that a judicious falsecard could do you in, if I were
to win the ´éę8 with the ´éęQ or ´éęK when holding ´éęKQ10 and no ´éĘQ, but that is just
too bad.
4. When does it matter what I do?
Asking yourself this question will help you sort out the irrelevant from the relevant/
important. Everyone knows that Trick 1 is an important moment for the defense,
but if you have retained the lead then Trick 2 can be equally critical.

5. Is this a tightrope situation?
A tightrope is a situation where the plays are forced, up to a certain point.
Recognizing this kind of position simplifies the analysis; follow the play tightrope
until someone has to make a choice and go from there.
Take a look at this deal from the 2010 WomenÔÇÖs European Championship:
´éź Q 7 6 5 4
´é¬ J 9 6 5
´éę Q 9 7
Dealer West
´éĘ J
NS Vul.
´éź A 10 3 2 ´éź K 9
´é¬ A 10 4 3 ´é¬ K 8 7 West North East South
´éę K ´éę A J 10 3 2 Sivelind Senior Rimstedt Dhondy
´éĘ A K 8 7 ´éĘ Q 5 3 1´éĘ pass 1´éź1 pass
´éź J 8 3NT pass 6NT all pass
´é¬ Q 2
1. Diamonds.
´éę 8 6 5 4
´éĘ 10 9 6 4 2
You want to be in 6NT here, which obviously has excellent chances assuming a
reasonable diamond break. North led the ´éź5 and declarer took SouthÔÇÖs jack with
the ace. Clearly the diamonds have to be tested, so you might as well get on with
it, and see what information you can gather along the way.
Declarer cashed the ´éęK and a top club and crossed to the ´éĘQ, North discarding
a spade. Declarer now played the ´éęA and ´éęJ, discarding a heart and a spade from
hand to arrive at this position, with North on lead:

So far, all the moves have been just about forced, as a chess player would say. Now
there are two variations.
90 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

If North returns a spade declarer wins in dummy, cashes the ´éę10 (discarding a
heart) and then plays the last diamond. When South discards a club, declarer does
the same and now plays a club to the king. North, down to the ´éźQ and ´é¬J96, is
squeezed in the majors.
If North plays a heart instead, declarer has more than one way to get home.
Say she wins the heart in hand, taking SouthÔÇÖs queen with the ace. She then crosses
to the ´éźK and cashes two diamonds, pitching a heart and a club from hand. North
is now down to the ´éźQ and ´é¬J9 and once again a club to the king executes the
squeeze. If declarer wins the heart switch with dummyÔÇÖs king she can make the
hand on a criss-cross squeeze, cashing two diamonds, pitching a heart and a club
from hand, then crossing to the ´éĘK to squeeze North.

end position can be important when you are considering the possibility of an

Prospects arenÔÇÖt great. If you can run diamonds you are fine, otherwise you will

the ´éę6; West played low, and when I played the nine from dummy, East pitched a

West is likely 5-6 in the red suits, with an unknown black card (6-6 is possible
but I think unlikely; he would have overcalled 1´é¬ not 2´é¬). My plan was to force a
split in diamonds, eliminate his black card, then engineer a red-suit endplay. So I

´éź 3

How should I play?

and king. After some thought West returned the ´é¬9. Should I cover with dummyÔÇÖs

a diamond lead. So I ducked in dummy, playing East for doubleton ´é¬Q. When
EastÔÇÖs queen did indeed appear, I won with the ´é¬A, cashed the ´é¬10, and now the
´éę2 endplayed West for +600.
Many players will be familiar with endplaying an opponent who holds a
doubleton honor in a suit, the idea being to force them to give you a ruff and
discard. This is a frequent ploy when declarer is one trick short and appears to
have one inescapable loser.
If a suit is distributed like this:
´éź 10 8 5 3
´éź Q 9 4 2 ´éź K J
´éź A 7 6
then declarer can simply play the ´éźA and exit with a spade.

---

However, if the suit looks like this:
´éź 10 8 5 3
´éź Q J 9 4 ´éź K 2
´éź A 7 6
East can avoid the endplay by dropping the king under the ace.
´éź 10 8 5 3
´éź K J 9 2 ´éź Q 4
´éź A 7 6
When the suit looks like this East will be endplayed if he wins the second trick, but
West can come to the rescue by going up with the king ÔÇö the so called ÔÇśCrocodile
CoupÔÇÖ in which WestÔÇÖs ´éźK swallows his partnerÔÇÖs card.
When declarer is going to attempt this type of endplay it is usually a good idea
to cash the ace early in the play, as it may not always be obvious to a defender that
an unblock is necessary. However, even late in the hand a defender may not always
get it right, as on this deal from the 2001 Vanderbilt final:

cashed the ´éźA East did not unblock, so declarer played three rounds of diamonds,

This concept of visualizing the end position can help even on a complex deal

West leads the ´é¬2 (fourth best) to the ten and ace. What is your plan?

in hand and also cash your long diamond, as there will still be trumps outstanding.
It is very difficult to see the possibility, but what you have to do is threaten
a crossruff with a long diamond in tow. You must draw some trumps also. You
lead a diamond from hand, West plays the ´éę2 (standard signals), and East wins

a diamond in dummy and lead a club ÔÇö the queen. You are hoping East has ´éĘK8
doubleton, so that he canÔÇÖt overruff a late red card.

---

How do you plan the play? You need to visualize the end position you are angling
for. You will have ruffed two diamonds in dummy and two spades in hand. You
need to ruff another diamond and then ruff a spade (and not get overruffed), so that
you can lead your good diamond. So, this is the ending you want to reach:
´éź 10
´é¬ 5
´éę ÔÇö
´éĘ 9 6
´éź J ´éź 9
´é¬ Q 9 ´é¬ 10
´éę ÔÇö ´éę A
´éĘ J ´éĘ 8
´éź ÔÇö
´é¬ 6
´éę J 8
´éĘ 7
You have lost a diamond and must lose the ´éĘJ, so you need three of the last four
tricks. When you lead the ´éę8, West canÔÇÖt ruff lest you pitch the heart loser. Also,
he canÔÇÖt pitch the ´éźJ or you would ruff in dummy and cash the ´éź10 to discard your
heart loser from hand. So, he pitches a heart and you ruff, ruff the ´éź10, and lead
the fifth diamond, scoring the ´éĘ9 en passant.
Note that this ending wonÔÇÖt work if West has two spades left since the ´éź10
is not then a threat. So, after playing a diamond to the queen and a second heart
return to your king, you know you need to ruff two spades and two diamonds
ending in hand, and that means you need to start by ruffing diamonds: diamond
ruff, ´éĘQ covered by the king and ace, spade to the ace, spade ruff, diamond ruff,
´éźQ covered by the king and ruffed, leading to the desired end position.
This is a very difficult hand indeed but this is the technique that you would
need to employ to get it (or an easier hand) right.

8. What plays might anyone (including partner) make that would be abnormal, and
what would be the implications of those various actions and plays?
I call these abnormal events triggers; that is, they are supposed to trigger a noticing
and thoughtful reconsideration from oneself.
This deal is from the 1999 Bermuda Bowl:
Dealer East
Both Vul.
´éź 9 7 5 4
´é¬ K J 5 4
´éę A K 5
´éĘ J 5
´éź K Q J 8 6 ´éź 10 3
´é¬ A 3 ´é¬ 6 2
´éę ÔÇö ´éę J 8 7 6 4 3 2
´éĘ Q 10 8 7 4 3 ´éĘ A 9
´éź A 2
´é¬ Q 10 9 8 7
´éę Q 10 9
´éĘ K 6 2
Several East-West pairs found themselves defending 4´é¬ by South after a Michaels
overcall from West. In order to defeat it the defenders have to secure a diamond
ruff, but not everyone managed that.
The play generally started with the ´éźK, which declarer won with the ace to play
a heart. West went in with the ace, cashed a spade and switched to a club. East
took the ace but did not always find the diamond return.
You might argue that given the auction, East should be alert to the possibility
that his partner has very few diamonds, and that WestÔÇÖs switch to the ´éĘ3 may well
be screaming for a diamond ruff. However, maybe West can make it even clearer?
Since the jack is on view in dummy, maybe the best card for West to return is
the queen of clubs in an attempt to trigger East.

9. What the heck is the position?
It can be quite easy to overlook the necessity of making a preliminary analysis once
the opening lead has been made and dummy comes down. If you forgot to do this
first, it is very helpful to go back and do it before you get bogged down in some
obscure analysis.
´éź A 7
´é¬ 8 6 3 2 Dealer South
´éę K Q 9 8 5 Neither Vul.
´éĘ K 9
´éź ÔÇö ´éź Q 10 8 6 3 West North East South
1´éź
´é¬ A K J 9 ´é¬ Q 10 7 5
dbl redbl pass pass
´éę J 7 2 ´éę 10 6
2´éĘ 2´éę pass 2´éź
´éĘ J 8 5 4 3 2 ´éĘ 10 7
pass 4´éź pass 4NT*
´éź K J 9 5 4 2
pass 5´éĘ* pass 5´éę*
´é¬ 4 pass 5´é¬* pass 5´éź
´éę A 4 3 pass pass dbl all pass
´éĘ A Q 6
When West leads the ´é¬A it is clear that EastÔÇÖs double can only be based on a
significant trump holding ÔÇö probably all the missing spades. To arrive at eleven
tricks you will need to take either six trump tricks and five in the minors, or seven
trumps and four outside. EastÔÇÖs failure to bid 2´é¬ suggests that he will hold no more
than four cards in the suit and if hearts are 4-4 then it will probably only be possible
to score four tricks in the minors.
East follows to the first heart with the ten and when West continues with the
king he plays the five. You ruff and test the trumps by crossing to the ´éęK and
playing the ´éź7. When East covers with the eight you play the nine and West
discards the ´éĘ2. By now you should have a very good picture of the layout, and
there is more than one way to get home. With East holding all five spades and
probably four hearts you cannot take five minor-suit tricks. One way to get home
now is to play a diamond to dummy and ruff a heart. A club to the king is followed
by another heart ruff. Now you cash the ´éĘA, ruff a club with the ´éźA and exit with
a diamond. East has to ruff and lead into your ´éźKJ.

10. Is there one card I can place in one of the unseen hands?
Once you can place an important card in one of the unseen hands, it will reduce
the number of possible hands that they can have considerably.
Failing to place a high card can have fatal consequences, as on this deal from
the final of the 1982 World Pairs Championship:
´éź K 9 7 2
´é¬ 4 2
´éę 9 7 6
´éĘ A K 10 9
Dealer West
´éź A 10 6 3 ´éź Q J 8
NS Vul.
´é¬ Q 9 6 ´é¬ K 10 8 7 5
´éę Q J 10 3 ´éę A K 8 4 2 West North East South
´éĘ J 6 ´éĘ ÔÇö Kantar Lawrence
´éź 5 4 pass pass 1´é¬ pass
´é¬ A J 3 1´éź pass 2´éę pass
´éę 5 3´é¬ pass 4´é¬ all pass
´éĘ Q 8 7 5 4 3 2
Lawrence led the ´éĘ5 and declarer ruffed KantarÔÇÖs king. Next he ran the ´éźQ, North
winning with the king and playing the ´éĘA, which declarer ruffed. At this point
it is clear that South has the ´é¬A, so declarer should continue with the ´é¬K. South
can win and try another club, but declarer can ruff and is in no danger. However,
declarer played a heart to the queen and, when that held, he belatedly realized that
another heart might enable South to draw trumps. So he cashed his spade winners,
pitching a diamond. It would not help South to ruff, so he discarded a diamond
and a club. When declarer now played on diamonds, South continued to pitch
clubs until North was able to ruff in, leaving South to take the last two tricks with
the ´é¬AJ.
Here is another example from the same event; this time it was the absence of a
bid that enabled declarer to figure out the position:

I led the ´éęQ and declarer won in hand and played a spade to the ten. When that
held, he placed me with the ´éźAQ and the ´éęQJ, in which case it was clear I could
not have the ´éĘK. So declarer played a low club from the dummy and when Jeff
played low his ten held the trick. I took the next trick with the ´éźA and returned a
club. Declarer won with the ace, felling EastÔÇÖs king, cashed two trumps ending in
hand, ruffed a diamond and played on clubs, happy to give up two trump tricks.
Often on defense you have to assume partner has the card or cards you need
him to hold. HereÔÇÖs a deal from the 2011 Spring NABC:
´éź J 3

Partner leads the ´éź7 (third best in partnerÔÇÖs suit), and it goes to the jack, king and
ace. Declarer now leads the ´é¬8 to dummyÔÇÖs queen and your king. What do you
do now?
Is it possible partner has ´éź Q10x(x)? Almost certainly not: declarer is an expert
and would have bid the hand more slowly in that case, either to avoid 3NT or to
let his partner play it if that is the final contract. So he has ´éźAQ10x. What about
the hearts? Partner must have the ´é¬A; holding ´é¬Ax declarer would almost certainly
lead ace and another in case you have a stiff king. Declarer likely has another heart
so if he gets in he will establish the suit. A hand with ´éźAQ10x and four heart tricks
means he has nine tricks if he has the ´éęA. So, you must lead a diamond now,
hoping not only that partner has both red aces but that he can establish another
diamond trick.
Should you lead the ´éę9 (middling, see p. 169)? This will get you four fast
diamond tricks if partner has ´éęAJ8x. However, you only need three diamond tricks
so donÔÇÖt risk confusing partner by leading the ´éę9: lead low. He has the ´é¬A anyway
as an entry.

11. Is there an error I can play for?
Even when things look hopeless, it may be possible to induce your opponent(s) to
assist you. Look at this deal, from the 2011 Spring Nationals:
´éź 9 3
´é¬ K 3 2
´éę K 8 4 2
´éĘ A 6 4 3
´éź A 10 5 2
´é¬ A J 10 6 4
´éę 3
´éĘ J 9 2
With East-West vulnerable, I opened 1´é¬ as South and partner raised me to 4´é¬ (yes,
this was the real bidding). West led the ´é¬5, and I got a shock when East pitched
an encouraging club! Winning the ´é¬6, I led a diamond; West ducked and I won
the ´éęK. What next?
I have 1+4+1+1 = 7 tricks, so I need to ruff in hand twice (to get up to 5 trump
tricks) and dummy twice. West seems bent on leading trumps so I want to lose a
spade trick to East. So I led the ´éź9, hoping for a cover. East duly covered with the
king and I ducked. He assisted me with another diamond lead and I was able to
ruff, play a club to the king and ace, a spade to the ace, a spade covered by WestÔÇÖs
queen and ruffed, diamond ruff, spade ruff.
102 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

---

Now I am down to ´é¬AJ and two club losers and West has all four trumps, so I
merely lead a club. She has to ruff and lead back into my ´é¬AJ: +420!
Later in the book weÔÇÖll be discussing situations where it is right to play second
hand high, but this was not the time for East to do so. If he ducked the spade, West
could win and lead another trump and set the hand. Leading the ´éź9 off dummy
made it more tempting for him to cover.
Years ago, there was an Italian Blue Team hand where declarer had this spade
combination:
´éź 9 x x
´éź A K 10
He needed to squeeze West, and East rated to have three spades. So he crossed to
dummy and led the ´éź9, and East covered from Qxx, perhaps worried that declarer
had K10x. The effect of the cover was to isolate the spade guard with West and the
squeeze now operated.
If the opponents, especially strong players, know a lot about the deal, you are
much less likely to make a hand with trickery than if they are in the dark. I played
this one at the 2011 Spring NABC:
Dealer South

´é¬ 5 3 2 1. Precision 1´éĘ, 16+ any shape.
2. 6-7 any shape, or 8+ with no good bid.
´éę K 6 5
3. 6-7, five-card spade suit.
´éĘ 9 3
West, Lew Stansby, leads the ´éęQ. What are your prospects?
If diamonds are 3-3 the cause is hopeless unless I duck the diamond in both
hands and they continue the suit. However, East will surely discourage a diamond
continuation so that is unlikely to work.
If diamonds are 4-2, I need to knock out WestÔÇÖs only entry. If West has either
club honor, I expect that East will rise with his honor (even the king) when I lead
from dummy so this wonÔÇÖt work. My only chance would be to concede a heart trick
first and hope that East has the ´éĘAK.
If diamonds are 5-1 I am in much better shape, as long as West has only one
side entry, but I must draw trumps. As this appeared to be my best chance, I
Making Your Plan * 103

---

ducked the diamond in dummy, East played the eight (discouraging) and I won
with the king. I played the ´éźQ, then a spade to the ace, on which East pitched a
heart. Now what?
West still has the ´éź10, so I canÔÇÖt draw the third trump (they can lead hearts and
get two heart tricks). I can try a heart now, hoping for East to have the ´éĘAK, but
all they need to do is keep leading hearts and, when East gets in with the last club,
lead another heart for a trump promotion. I think playing for this mistake was my
best shot, but I tried a low club from dummy. East won with the ace, led another
diamond, and West got in with the ´é¬A to cash the diamond, down one.
At the other table a trump was led so making ten tricks was easy. Did I have
a legitimate play for it by leading a club? Diamonds would have to be 5-1, East
would have to have something like
´éź x ´é¬ A 10 9 x x x x ´éę x ´éĘ A K x x
or
´éź x ´é¬ A K 10 x x x x ´éę x ´éĘ K x x x
both hands where West has only one entry. Most players would bid more than 2´é¬
with these hands and I feel sure that Chip Martel would have also.
So I was likely dead either way. I can make the hand if I leave trumps alone,
play for diamonds 4-2 and lead a heart first, but I think 5-1 diamonds with West
having any one side entry is the better percentage chance. I can also make via the
double dummy play of running the ´éź8! Then when I play a second trump I can
concede a heart and avoid any trump promotion.
12. Control/Alt/Delete
This is the sequence of keys for re-booting a computer that is caught in an infinite
loop or has had an unrecoverable system error. It applies equally well if you find
yourself stuck on a lot of speculations and tangents. Go back to basics:
Read the position; Map your basic position (winners, losers, entries); do Basic
Options Analysis (check offensive prospects suit-by-suit, defensive threats, and
merits of passive strategy); and finally do Full Analysis (trick packages that would
give you the extra tricks needed to succeed ÔÇö analyzing them all if necessary).
104 * Part 1 ÔÇö The Building Blocks

PArt 2 mAneuvers

cArdPlAy

Chapter 5
declArer PlAy
In Part 1, I discussed two broad topics: first, some of the basic cardplay techniques
that are available to you, and second, how you go about analyzing a hand and
making a plan for the play. Now itÔÇÖs time to move on to more advanced maneuvers.
Some of these ideas may be familiar to you, but I suspect many will not be. For the
most part they are not complicated, but are things that are hard to visualize until
someone has shown them to you. Once seen, they are generally not difficult to
apply when the occasion arises. I have, as always, given them names, for purposes
of reference and as an aide-memoire.
the entrY or k.o. FlY
I mentioned earlier my credo that ÔÇśentries are the lifeblood of bridgeÔÇÖ. So letÔÇÖs start
with some maneuvers that revolve around creating or preserving entries. The first
I have named the Entry Fly or K.O. Fly.
Here, LHO leads RHOÔÇÖs suit (presumably with a doubleton) and you have
some holding where RHO can duck to preserve communication. You canÔÇÖt prevent
this, but at least you can go up (fly) in dummy to get a free entry there, since RHO
does not want to win this trick.
This is the type of position you should be on the lookout for:
´éź 10 x x
´éź A J x
When West leads the ´éź9 you can go up with dummyÔÇÖs ten.
´é¬ Q x x
´é¬ J x x
Here you are in notrump, East has bid hearts, and West leads the ´é¬8. Since East
will want to duck this trick, you can go up with the queen and gain an entry to
dummy.
LetÔÇÖs see this tactic at work in a full deal:

---

´éź J 5 4
´é¬ 8 6 5
Dealer East
´éę Q J 10 8
Neither Vul.
´éĘ A 4 3
West North East South
´éź A 10 7 1´éź 1NT
´é¬ A Q J pass 2NT pass 3NT
´éę 9 5 4 all pass
´éĘ K Q J 8
West leads the ´éź9. What is your plan?
To retain the option of taking a heart finesse, play the ´éźJ from dummy. If East
has
´éź K Q 8 6 3 ´é¬ K 7 3 ´éę A 2 ´éĘ 10 7 5
he may duck the ´éźJ on the theory that if you have ´éźA107x you have three stoppers
anyway. That being the case, he might as well hope that his partner has a doubleton
spade and the ´éęK.
Once the ´éźJ holds the trick your only hope is the heart finesse, but playing the
´éźJ gives you the extra entry to take it twice. If East covers the ´éźJ, duck hoping for
split diamond honors.
Here is an example where declarer makes the K.O. fly play in order to preserve
an entry to hand. It comes from the 2005 World Transnational Open Teams.

needs only to guess the heart suit to arrive at nine tricks.

the entrY oVertake
In any situation where you are short of entries you may be able to execute an
overtaking play, even if this gives up a trick. For example, with
´éę A J 10 9 x
´éę K
you can overtake the king with the ace and then force out the queen to set up the
suit. You need only one entry outside diamonds to pull this off.
Here is a full deal that illustrates the principle:
´éź 8
´é¬ 9 6 3
´éę A 8 4
´éĘ A K 10 9 8 6
Dealer North
´éź Q J 10 7 ´éź 9 5 4 3
NS Vul.
´é¬ K 10 5 4 ´é¬ Q 7
´éę K 9 2 ´éę J 10 5 West North East South
´éĘ 4 2 ´éĘ J 7 5 3 1´éĘ pass 1´é¬
´éź A K 6 2 pass 2´éĘ pass 3NT
´é¬ A J 8 2 all pass
´éę Q 7 6 3
´éĘ Q
West leads the ´éźQ and declarer wins. With only one outside entry to dummy, he
overtakes the ´éĘQ with the king, cashes the ace and then forces out the jack. The
´éęA is the only side entry required.
When entries are at a premium, declarer should always be alive to the possibility
of an overtaking play. This combination:
´éź A Q 10 8
´éź K J 9 7
can yield no fewer than four entries to the North hand ÔÇö overtake the king with
the ace, the jack with the queen, etc.
108 * Part 2 ÔÇö Advanced Cardplay Maneuvers

---

the exPosIng oVertake
In this play, declarer, with a wealth of high cards (usually in the trump suit), makes
an overtaking unblock to create the possibility of a subsequent finesse should one
be needed. I can show the basic idea with this deal I played many years ago in the
Grand National Pairs, partnering Jim Reiman:
´éź J 8 4 2
´é¬ K Q 10 7 3
´éę A K 5
Dealer East
´éĘ Q
Neither Vul.
´éź 7 5 ´éź A K Q 10 9 3
´é¬ 9 5 4 2 ´é¬ ÔÇö West North East South
´éę 4 2 ´éę J 7 5 3 Reiman Rodwell

1. Diamond control, slam try in hearts.
´éĘ K 9 2
The defenders started with two rounds of spades, and I ruffed with the ´é¬8 (spades

the seven allowed me to claim +480.
Geoff Hampson, that famous ex-Canadian, came up with an even safer variation
ÔÇö ruff the second spade with the ´é¬A, then lead the ´é¬8 to the king (seeing the
void), and ruff the next spade with the ´é¬J. This rules out any chance of an overruff
even if East did have a seven-card weak two-bid. As chessmaster Bobby Fischer
once said, ÔÇśWhen you see a good move, look for a better one!ÔÇÖ

West leads the ´éźA, forcing dummy to ruff. If you ruff with the two of hearts the 4-1
trump break spells defeat, as there is no way to get to hand and draw the last trump.
However, if you ruff high, you can then cash dummyÔÇÖs two remaining top hearts,
draw trumps via a finesse against EastÔÇÖs ten and then enjoy the clubs.
Here is a deal from a recent Regional where I used this technique (playing
IMPs) to have a free shot at an overtrick in 3NT.

West led the ´éźQ to dummyÔÇÖs ace. I have nine certain tricks and a vulnerable
diamond holding, so I should only take the heart finesse for an overtrick if IÔÇÖm
100% safe. The first key is to see the exposing overtake in clubs: I can lead the ´éĘQ
and if East follows, I can overtake, being able to finesse the ´éĘ8 later if East has all
five clubs. This is necessary if I want to finesse the heart early (in fact I can run six
club tricks safely, pitching two spades and two hearts, and have another heart to
pitch should they take three diamond tricks, but this was faster and easier).
The problem is a possible diamond switch if the heart finesse loses, and an
asset survey shows I am missing ´éęAQJ10xxx. If East leads a medium diamond
honor, I am safe as long as I donÔÇÖt cover the first diamond honor, but do cover the
second. Look at this potential diamond layout:
´éę 9 2

´éę K 8 4 3
I am missing four diamond honors, and if I cover one of them they spend two on
one trick, so as long as they canÔÇÖt communicate to trap my eight, IÔÇÖm safe. I call this
handling of the diamond suit delayed cover protection.
So I led the ´éĘQ to the ace and finessed in hearts, to make the overtrick.

Other Variations
(a) ´éź A K J 9 7 4 2
´éź Q 3

(b) ´é¬ A K J 10 7

´éę K J 3 2
Leading the king to the ace, then later the jack to the queen, produces no fewer

either opponent starts with the singleton ´éę9.
the re-entrY unBlock
This is a similar type of play where unblocking a sequence of three or more cards
will preserve entry options later. Suppose West leads a diamond (trumps) when
you have this combined holding:
´éę 10 9 8 6
´éę A K Q 7
Play the ´éę10 and, provided you donÔÇÖt think West has ´éęJxxx (and you donÔÇÖt have
some other reason to let the ten hold), overtake it with the ace. This gives you
maximum flexibility later. Note that this play is most advantageous if you think
West will lead the next trump.
the chInese hIgh card ProMotIon
You are trying to establish a suit (normally a 5-3 fit) and can afford two losers in
it. If you need to get it going without creating entry problems, you can consider
leading high from a holding of honor-third, as in this situation:

cards in dummy ÔÇö hence the name.
Chinese High Card Promotion can also be used in situations where you are just
trying to promote two tricks in a 4-3 fit without letting them build a second trick
for themselves, as in this example:
´éź Q J 8 3

queen first will inevitably establish a second trick for him as you develop your own

finessed him.

PlaYIng hIgh as declarer
IÔÇÖve already discussed some positions, and the Chinese High Card Promotion is a
good example, where it is necessary for declarer to lead or play a high card rather
than a small one. Here are some other situations where the same ploy is useful.
a) the Promotion smother Play
Sometimes declarer can prevent a trump promotion by preserving a high trump to
overruff with. Suppose this is your trump suit, and you are worried about West
scoring a ruff.
´é¬ Q x x
´é¬ A 10 9 or ´é¬ A 9
´é¬ K J x x x
If East is presumed to have the trump ace, lead low from dummy. When East
ducks and your king holds, rather than guess on the way back (no re-entry to
dummy), lead the jack now. This is clearly only going to be effective when both
dummy and declarer are also void in the same suit as West.
This might be the full deal:

and king, and now itÔÇÖs important for declarer to lead the ´é¬J, for the promotion

Note that if I lead the ´éĘK from dummy at Trick 2, East winning and playing another

very awkward).
Real life is usually more complicated than just taking finesses into the safe
hand, however. In the finesse through safety, declarer leads through the safe
opponent, planning to duck if they go up but to go up if they duck. Sometimes
a very favorable assumption as to the layout of the cards must be made (such as
finding the safe opponent with Kx or KQx in the ducking suit). This play can be
used during suit establishment, or for stripping the hand of exit cards and rectifying
the count for a strip-squeeze.
Here is a typical case:
´éź 7 5
Dealer South
´é¬ A 8 3 2
Neither Vul.
´éę 7 5 4
´éĘ A K 7 6 West North East South
1´éę
´éź A 6 3 1´éź dbl* pass 1NT
´é¬ K 4 pass 2NT pass 3NT
´éę A K 10 3 2 all pass
´éĘ 5 4 3
West leads the ´éźK and you duck, winning the third round with the ace (East
follows, dummy pitches a heart). What now?
You need the diamond suit and you need to keep West off lead. If you lead the
´éęA, it is curtains, as East unblocks a doubleton queen. You need to cross to dummy
(a club to the ace) and lead a low diamond as a finesse through safety. If East plays
the ´éęQ, duck. If East plays low, win the ace.
If West follows to your ace with a diamond honor, you can cash the ´éęK,
catering to ´éęQJ tight, and still have an entry in dummy to lead toward your ´éę10xx
should West have a stiff honor.
If West plays low on the ´éęA, cross back to dummy (in hearts this time) and try
a diamond up again. You duck if the ´éęQ appears; if you donÔÇÖt see the queen, win
the ´éęK, hoping East has three diamonds. This is the full layout:

Sometimes these avoidance positions are complicated, not to mention harder to

´éź A 9 6 4
´é¬ A 6 5

´éź K Q 5 ´éź J 10 8 3

´éĘ 8 7 ´éĘ 6 3 2 1´éę dbl 1´é¬ 1NT
´éź 7 2 2´éę 3NT all pass
´é¬ K 7 3
´éę K 10 6 5 4
´éĘ Q 9 4
West leads the ´é¬J and East plays the ´é¬Q. You want to prevent a lead through your
´éęK10xxx, hoping for an endplay late in the day. If you just run your tricks, West
pitches diamonds (and East hearts) and you must lose five tricks as long as West
unblocks spades at the right time.
The solution is to lead spades yourself, but you need to finesse through safety
by leading through West. Win the ´é¬K and lead a spade. If West doesnÔÇÖt split,
you win the ace, cash your winners, and exit a spade hoping for the above layout.
When West does split, you duck. He leads a second heart to your ace, so you cross
to the ´éĘ9 and lead another spade, giving West the same choice. You still have the
´éĘQ as an entry so West canÔÇÖt get out of it by cashing the ´éęA and exiting with a
spade.
What if instead your clubs were Qxx? Would all be hopeless (against best
defense)? This time you need to lead a spade from dummy at Trick 2, so win the
´é¬A at Trick 1 and lead a low spade. East can stick in the ´éź8 and return a diamond,

---

but West is left with king-queen tight in spades. He returns the ´éźK to your ace,
leaving this position:
´éź 9 6
´é¬ 6 5
´éę ÔÇö
´éĘ A K J 10 5
´éź ÔÇö
´é¬ K 7
´éę K 10 6 5
´éĘ Q 4 2
West has the stiff ´éźQ left, ´éęAQJ98 and three small cards between hearts and
clubs. If he is 3=2=6=2 originally, cash the ´é¬K, ´éĘJ and ´éĘA, then lead a spade to
force a diamond play. Running clubs wonÔÇÖt work because West will throw three
diamonds ÔÇö then if you try exiting with the spade from dummy, West has a heart
to get out with.
However, if West started with 3=1=6=3 you have to run your clubs to take out
WestÔÇÖs exit cards. You also need to retain the ´é¬K as a late hand entry for a diamond
play. If you play an early heart to the king, West keeps three clubs and forces you
either to abandon club winners in dummy or to allow him to jettison his ´éźQ on
the long clubs, or to leave him with a club exit card if you donÔÇÖt cash at least three
club winners.
You have to guess which layout is more likely. Mathematically, West having
3=2=6=2 is more likely. (West having any two clubs is just as likely as him having
any three clubs. The difference is that there are five cases of ´é¬Jx and only one stiff
´é¬J.) If you think you know enough about their style to be sure that East would
not bid with
´éź J 10 8 x ´é¬ Q 10 x x x ´éę x ´éĘ x x x
then that might tip the scales.
the FInesse through danger
The finesse through danger is closely related to #7, the finesse through safety. As
we have just seen, that one involves leading through the safe hand, ducking if he
plays a high card. You do just the opposite when leading through the danger hand
ÔÇö going up when he goes up, and ducking when he ducks.
122 * Part 2 ÔÇö Advanced Cardplay Maneuvers

---

´éź 7 6 5
´éź A K 10 4 3
If East is the danger hand, lead a spade from dummy, covering EastÔÇÖs card. You can
even succeed if East has ´éźQJ92 or ´éźQJ82, as long as you need only three tricks (run
the ´éź5, then play a spade to the ten).
In many cases you are just hoping to slip past the danger hand, without being
put to an awkward guess.
´é¬ 3 2
´é¬ A Q 10 6 5 4
If East is dangerous, you finesse the ´é¬10 first and then later the ´é¬Q. But what if
East plays the ´é¬K on the first round and you have no sure usable re-entry to hand?
You are cooked if he has ´é¬Kx (if you win, your suit is dead; if you duck, then the
danger hand is in). If he has ´é¬KJx, you need to finesse again; if he has ´é¬Kxx you
must play for the drop of the offside jack doubleton. Basically, you just have to
guess what to do. It is for this reason that it is often right to play danger hand high
on defense ÔÇö an idea weÔÇÖll get to in Chapter 10.
the crossoVer stoPPer
While weÔÇÖre on the topic of situations where one defender and not the other can
cause you a problem, let me introduce the concept of the crossover stopper. I use
this phrase to describe a card that doesnÔÇÖt stop the run of a suit on power, but does
require them to cross over an extra time to play the suit.
´éź K Q 3 2
´éź A J 10 8
´éź 9 5
East leads the ´éźJ around to the king. Your nine is a crossover stopper ÔÇö West will
have to lead the suit twice to run it. Another way to look at it is that you have the
potential pusher card they need to lead through strength ÔÇö the one that would
either hold the lead (to lead through again) or allow them to run the suit if it were
covered. In the example, if West held the ´éź9 he would only have to get in one more
time to set up spades.
It can be very valuable to realize that you have a crossover stopper. In that
situation, it is not attractive for you to lead the suit yourself in an attempt to break
the defendersÔÇÖ communications, unless you think that West has only one card left
in the suit.

the rodwellIan IntraFInesse
LetÔÇÖs move on now to some situations where the way you handle a specific suit can
make all the difference in the number of tricks you take. You may be surprised at
how much value you can extract even from quite small spot cards.
The intrafinesse, one of the few modern discoveries in cardplay technique, was
popularized by the Brazilian Gabriel Chagas. The standard intrafinesse position is:
´éź A 9 x
´éź K 8 x x
You play low to the ´éź9, then cash the ace and finesse the ´éź8 to win when West
started with a doubleton honor.
Here are a couple of examples of the Rodwellian version, in which declarer
makes the play while missing a major honor:
´é¬ J 3
´é¬ A Q 9 8 5
If you think LHO has four hearts, run the nine (if you can handle everything,
including the post-mortem when East has a singleton or doubleton ten).
´éź K 4
´éź Q J 9 8 3
Once again you can consider running the nine when you think the length is on
your left.
Here are a few more variations of the normal intrafinesse:
´éĘ A 9 6 3
´éĘ Q 8 4 2
The standard way to tackle this combination is by playing low to the ace and then
low toward the queen, but if you are pretty sure West has the ´éĘK, thatÔÇÖs not going
to work. To take an intrafinesse, you start by playing low to the eight and then run
the queen on the next round, hoping to find RHO with ´éĘJx or ´éĘ10x.

´éę A 9 5
´éę Q 8 7 3
As in the previous example, the classical way to play this suit is by cashing the ace
and then playing the nine, putting up the queen if East plays only small cards. If
you know West has the king, you might try starting with low to the ace and then
ducking the next round. However, that will not provide a third trick if West has
´éęKx. Instead, you can begin by leading low to the nine. If East has ´éęJ10xx, ´éęJxxx
or ´éę10xxx, you will lose only one trick.
´éź A 10 4
´éź 9 7 6 3
In this case, low to the ten caters to West having honor doubleton (or ´éźKQJx), but
loses to KQ, QJ or KJ doubleton or a singleton honor on the right. You will score
two tricks almost 68% of the time.

the Intrasquash
The intrasquash is a type of intrafinesse safety play. The idea is that a specific
intermediate singleton can be squashed; otherwise all is a wash (if a certain
minimum number of losers can be afforded).
Here are a couple of examples:
´é¬ A 4
´é¬ K 10 8 7 5 3
With this combination your only hope of six tricks is to find someone with the
doubleton ´é¬QJ. However, with spots this good it costs nothing (assuming no 5-0
break) to run the ´é¬10 ÔÇö which gives you slightly more than a 79% chance of five
tricks. If West has a singleton queen, jack or nine, you will win in dummy and lead
to your spot on the way back. If East has the singleton queen or jack, your only
chance is an erroneous cover by West. However, if East has a stiff nine then you
legitimately lose only one trick.
You may think this is an exercise in esoteric theory, but Patrick Jourdain of
Wales brought this play off in a Camrose match against England in 1989, Graham
Kirby as East accompanying the play of his singleton nine with the comment, ÔÇśI think
this is what you are looking forÔÇÖ. At the other table David Burn was the declarer
who didnÔÇÖt find the safety play, playing, he claimed, for a trump coup. David Price
was dummy and asked about his heart holding. When Burn mentioned the ten but
not the next one down Price asked again and was told about the eight. He asked
again and was now told about the seven. Then Price pointed out the safety play.
The Vugraph commentator didnÔÇÖt understand JourdainÔÇÖs play until a member of the
audience pointed out that it was the textbook line.
´éĘ K 4
´éĘ Q 9 7 6 5 2
The idea is the same in this case; run the ´éĘ9 in case East has the stiff ´éĘ8. You take
four tricks nearly 82% of the time.

which you tackle a suit may be affected by how you place the missing honors. Look
at this deal from the 2005 Venice Cup Final:

the skY Marshal FInesse
This one got its name because you have to lead a high card for maximum tricks
against certain distributions of the opposing honors ÔÇö the bad guys.
´éź A 2
´éź Q 10 9 8 6
For years everyone thought this combination should be tackled by cashing the
ace then playing low to the queen. This works when the jack is singly or doubly
guarded on your left and is clearly better than playing the ace followed by low to
the ten, which only gains when there is Kxx on your left. The chance of making
four tricks is around 39%.
However, the best chance (a priori) to win the most tricks in the suit is to start
by leading the queen. You pick up Kx in front of the A2 (the gaining case), and
otherwise it is a wash with the play of ace and low to the queen (assuming requisite
entries). This improvement gets you up to a near 43% chance of four tricks.

´éę A 2
´éę J 9 8 7 5
