doubleton KQ, as would playing low to the ace and then back to the jack. It gains
when ´éęKx or ´éęQx is in front of the ´éęA2, and also when you get an erroneous cover
from ´éęKQxx, ´éęK10xx, or ´éęQ10xx. You only lose when there is a singleton honor.
Altogether this gives you almost a 72% chance of three tricks.
´éĘ K 2
´éĘ J 9 8 7 5
You might tackle this combination in the same way by starting with the jack,
although the computer reckons leading low is best ÔÇö delivering three tricks more
than 47% of the time (you put up the king then play back to the jack).
the MartellIan squash
The name for this play comes from a hand I played long ago against Chip Martel,
who was on my left. I had this combination in the spade suit:
´éź J 5 4 3
´éź A Q 8
I needed three spade tricks, and I needed to keep LHO off lead. I knew at that

stiff ten or nine I led dummyÔÇÖs jack. East played the king, and Chip played the ´éź6
under my ace.
I crossed back to dummy to lead the ´éź3, and East produced the ´éź2 ÔÇö and
now, as I didnÔÇÖt want to go down an extra 100, I went up with the ´éźQ. In fact
Chip had ´éź76 doubleton, so I had had a blind spot. I could have stuck in the
´éź8, completely ÔÇśsquashingÔÇÖ his ´éź76 doubleton. It wouldnÔÇÖt have helped RHO Lew
Stansby to split, since he didnÔÇÖt have the winners to cash.
I subsequently named this the Martellian squash because Chip had the exact
holding I could squash and I missed it. Some examples of other suit combinations:
(a) ´é¬ J 9 2 (b) ´éę A K 10 6
´é¬ A 7 5 3 ´éę J 2

The Martellian squash is obviously closely related to the Rodwellian intrafinesse.
´éę J 5 ´éĘ J 3
´éę A 8 7 3 2 ´éĘ A 8 7 6 2
Suppose with the diamond combination, which I had at the 2011 NABC in
Louisville, I need three tricks. A 3-3 split is fine, and if West has KQ64 I am also
good: I lead toward the jack, and later, when in dummy, run the jack. This also
picks up K10, Q10, K9 or Q9 on my left, a form of Martellian squash.
Change the suit ever so slightly to the club combination, and I can now take a
Rodwellian intrafinesse, leading the six from hand and planning to run it if West
plays low. Then when I lead the jack from dummy I pick up WestÔÇÖs 10x or 9x, as
well as king or queen plus ten or nine doubleton. Of course, West can give me
a guess by playing the 10 or 9 with all two-card holdings when he has it (from a
holding of 10x or 9x, playing high would be an intrapop, p. 259) giving me a guess
whether to lead back to my eight or try to drop honor doubleton. In practice it is
very hard for West to duck honor-ten or honor-nine doubleton. I can also pick up
109 doubleton, which is what I would play for if West plays the nine on my six, or
if the trick goes six, ten, jack, queen.
Note that if West has KQxx, he can duck. Now if I run the six I lose three
tricks, when if I had put up the jack I would lose only two ÔÇö but there are more
holdings to pick up by running the six.
the sPeed oF lIghtnIng PlaY
Now letÔÇÖs move on to some general tactical ideas ÔÇö some of them are legitimate,
in the sense that the opposition can do nothing to counter them, while others
depend on inducing an (often slight) error. The speed of lightning play is one of
my favorites. As any fan of the band Queen will anticipate, it can be very, very
frightening.
If RHO is the dangerous opponent, you can often lead away from a holding
like AJx in dummy toward holdings in the closed hand headed by the ten, on the
theory that RHO wonÔÇÖt go up with Qx(x). A common variation is where you lead
low from KJx toward 109xxx in hand. Of course, you must be able to afford to lose
a trick to RHO later on. This play is most valuable when you have something like
Qx opposite AJx in hand in the suit they led (dummyÔÇÖs queen having won Trick 1),
where East canÔÇÖt hurt you later, only now.
This play is so named because East will play low at the speed of lightning, as
a matter of habit. In fact, if he knows that he is the dangerous opponent, there
is every reason for him to play the queen (danger hand high!) since if he ducks,
declarer will doubtless try some coverage ducking play.

the notruMP Fork
The expression MortonÔÇÖs Fork originates from a policy of tax collection devised in
1487 by John Morton, Lord Chancellor of England under Henry VII. His approach
was that if the subject lived in luxury, he obviously had sufficient income to spare
for the king. Alternatively, if the subject lived frugally, he must have substantial
savings and could therefore afford to give some to the king. These arguments were
the two prongs of the fork: regardless of whether the subject was rich or poor, he
did not have a favorable choice.
Most readers will be familiar with the bridge version, the MortonÔÇÖs Fork Coup.
In this coup declarer leads through a defender toward a high card. The defender
can choose to duck, after which declarerÔÇÖs remaining loser in the suit is discarded
elsewhere, or go up and have his winner ÔÇśbeat airÔÇÖ, setting up an extra trick in the
suit. The notrump fork is a more general version of this ÔÇö if the defender rises, it
sets up one or more winners for declarer, while ducking allows declarer to steal a
trick and possibly turn his attention elsewhere. There are two basic types.
Declarer Play * 135

---

sure trick
The play is guaranteed to be successful, given enough entries.
´éĘ Q J 7 5 4
´éĘ K 6 3 2
Lead low from hand. The suit runs unless it breaks 4-0. However, if West has
four of them, you can force him to duck (twice, if you cross back to your hand and
repeat the procedure) since going up is immediately fatal. If you can then build
your tricks in another suit, you have successfully completed a notrump fork.
conditional
You need either a mistake or a favorable (or not too unfavorable) lie of the suit.
´é¬ Q 7 4
´é¬ K 8 6 5
Here, if you need three tricks from this suit (or to steal one trick), the move is
legitimate only if you happen to lead through ´é¬Axx.
´éę Q 9 8 3
´éę K 6 4 2
In this case, the lead is successful unless a defender can rise with AJ10x(x)
(presuming you need three tricks, or to steal just one).
Such forking suits can be straight two-way guesses, like this:
´éź Q x x
´éź K x x x
or one play may be favored ÔÇö for example, with
´éĘ Q J x x
´éĘ K 9 x x
lead toward dummy, being able to pick up A10xx in RHOÔÇÖs hand but not LHOÔÇÖs.
136 * Part 2 ÔÇö Advanced Cardplay Maneuvers

---

If a guess is required, lead through the opponent who would be dangerous
if he were to capture one of your honors; otherwise (depending on the rest of the
hand) take your best guess.
Suppose the Qxx opposite Kxxx scenario occurs when you believe that West
has at least a six-card suit that he has led and that you have double-stopped. You
have already used one of your stoppers, and East is known to be unable to return
the suit (having started with an initial stiff or void). You have a finesse into West
that yields only eight winners if it loses but nine if it wins.
If three tricks from Qxx opposite Kxxx would see you home, then you can
legitimately lead through West and make your contract when he has Axx in that
suit, as West canÔÇÖt afford to go up with the ace. This is just giving you an extra
chance ÔÇö you claim if West ducks. If West wins, you can cash the king and queen
to see if the suit splits 3-3; if not then you can fall back on the finesse. You may get
other chances too ÔÇö you may be able to cater to a stiff king offside (you can afford
a loser but only to East), or if West has four-card length in the fork suit, then he
can be strip-squeezed.

has at most a singleton diamond. East has a good heart and probably a good club,
but that is all, so you can afford to play ace and a diamond, waiting for East to give
you a diamond at the end.
If West started with two or three diamonds, then if he keeps at least two
diamonds you exit with a spade for a diamond return. If West keeps one diamond

cash and thrash
This type of play arises when you have solid outside values but a modest trump
holding: say, Axxx opposite xxxx.
Suppose the defenders start by attacking a suit in which you hold Axx facing a
singleton. You win, cash the ace of trumps and then thrash by playing your side-
suit winners. The point of this play is that by cashing the ace of trumps you avoid
the risk of a player ruffing in with a singleton trump. You also avoid the danger
of playing a second round of trumps, which might give them the chance to draw
trumps and then run their side suit. The play is equally valid if you start by simply
ducking a round of trumps, planning to cash the ace on the next round.
138 * Part 2 ÔÇö Advanced Cardplay Maneuvers

This scenario can arise with different trump holdings, for example AKxx facing
xxxx, or AKxxx facing xx when you are forced to ruff in the long trump hand at an

Declarer can make things much tougher for the defenders by retaining the ace

he ruffs a club and plays a diamond, and the defense prevails.

Block duckIng
Block ducking is a ducking play that preserves communications in the suit itself. It
is easier to demonstrate than to define:
´éź K 10 3 Dealer North
´é¬ K Q 5 4 3 Neither Vul.
´éę 2

West leads the ´éź9 against your Moysian (4-3 fit) slam. How do you play from here?
You have ten top tricks. Against this lead, ruffing two diamonds in dummy
and getting back to hand to draw trumps is a problem. However, one diamond ruff
will do if you can get a fourth heart trick. Since you are +1L, you can try ducking
a heart immediately! This is expedient not only to avoid the defense playing a
third round of hearts, but also for trump control and re-entry purposes. You are
blocking the suit, which is why I call this block ducking.
They play a second trump, which you win and then you play the ´éęA and ruff
a diamond. Now you lead a heart to the ace and draw trumps, claiming if neither
major splits worse than 4-2.

the 322/1/6 suPer duck
The 322/1/6 super duck is (obviously) another ducking play (donÔÇÖt worry about the
name ÔÇö IÔÇÖll get to it in a moment). This is the standard position:
´éź K 7 5
´é¬ 5 3
´éę A Q J 5 4
´éĘ 8 5 3
Dealer South
´éź J 10 9 8 ´éź 6 4 2
Neither Vul.
´é¬ K J 4 ´é¬ Q 7 6 2
´éę 9 2 ´éę K 10 7 6 West North East South
´éĘ J 9 7 2 ´éĘ Q 10 1NT
´éź A Q 3 pass 3NT all pass
´é¬ A 10 9 8
´éę 8 3
´éĘ A K 6 4
You win the spade lead in hand and take a diamond finesse; East ducks. You will
probably duck a club now (you might instead lead a heart to the ten and jack).
They win and return another spade. You win in hand, repeat the diamond finesse,
and down you go.
The winning play is to duck the first round of diamonds completely! This is
such a counter-intuitive play with this combination that I call it a super duck. The
numbers 322 describe the number of stoppers you have in each suit. You need 3
in the suit they led (spades), because ducking then finessing (in diamonds) means
conceding two control units. However, you also need 2 stoppers in any suit they
could switch to, for the same reason.
Declarer Play * 145

---

The 1 in the name means you have exactly one entry to the long suit. If you
had two entries you wouldnÔÇÖt need to bother with the duck. The 6 means you have
six winners on the side, which means you need three tricks from the crucial suit
(here, diamonds). Hence the name: 322/1/6 super duck.
The play is equally valid with ´éęKQJ54 opposite ´éę83.
This is an example of why you have to guard against the possibility of a defender
making an astute duck with a doubleton king:
´éź J 6 2
´é¬ 8 4
´éę A Q J 5 3

´éź K 7 ´éź Q 10 8 4 Dealer South
´é¬ Q 10 7 6 2 ´é¬ 9 5 3 NS Vul.
´éę 10 9 7 2 ´éę K 8
´éĘ Q 2 ´éĘ J 10 9 6 West North East South
1NT
´éź A 9 5 3
pass 3NT all pass
´é¬ A K J
´éę 6 4
´éĘ A 8 5 3
West leads the ´é¬6 and declarer wins with the jack. When he now plays a diamond
to the queen, East should duck smoothly. If declarer returns to hand with the ´éĘA
and plays a diamond to the jack, East wins and simply returns a heart. Now, with
neither minor suit behaving, eight tricks are the limit1.
Instead, declarer can afford to super duck the first round of diamonds, allowing
East to win with the eight. He takes the heart return and plays a diamond to the
queen. East wins, but now declarer has three winning diamonds and nine tricks
in total. It is worth noting that this play will be a spectacular failure if West has
´éęK109xx, but East having ´éęK10xx or ´éęKx is much more likely.

shortshake
While weÔÇÖre on the subject of avoiding defensive ruffs, overruffs and promotions,
I should mention the shortshake. A shortshake involves discarding a non-loser so
that you can take a ruff in this suit earlier in the play and (you hope) avoid getting
overruffed.

You arrive in 5´éĘ as South, after opening a strong 1´éĘ. North has shown both

which you win with your ace. How do you play from here?
You need to assume only two trump losers. Then, you need either to run
diamonds (you could try to ruff one in hand, hoping the opponent with two
diamonds has exactly three clubs) or to pitch a diamond, if necessary, on spades
(via either a finesse or ruffing out the suit). Which do you favor?
I decided to work on spades, hoping for a 3-3 split or ´éźKx. To reduce the risk
of ruffs, I led the ´é¬K at Trick 2 and pitched a spade, the shortshake. I then ruffed
a spade and led a club to the ace. If East had played an honor I would have had to
consider now playing another trump, but both played low. I ruffed a second spade,
West playing the jack. I then exited with a low club to WestÔÇÖs queen. It appeared
that my line of play had failed, as the full deal was:

the runnIng suIt eradIcatIon endPlaY

´éĘ J 8 3
West leads the ´éę8, fourth best, and East plays the ´éę2 (upside-down count). After
winning the ´éęJ, you lead a club and West pitches the ´é¬8. How do you continue?
It appears that West has ´éęAQ98xx so you canÔÇÖt let East have the lead. West
is at least 6-5, so spades arenÔÇÖt splitting and you therefore have only seven tricks.
You canÔÇÖt endplay West in diamonds because he will cash five diamond tricks.
However, if you can put him in with a heart, he will be forced to lead a diamond,
which eradicates the threat of him running this suit. So win the ´éĘA, cash the ´éźAK
and play three rounds of hearts. West scores two heart tricks and the ´éęA, but the
diamond suit is now dead. You then set up your ninth trick in clubs (and thus
must not create a fifth defensive winner for East by cashing the third high spade).
Can anything go wrong? What if West unblocks his hearts? Remember you
have only seven tricks and nowhere to go for a ninth without letting East in for
a diamond through if he does unblock. To help protect against this possibility,
unblock your ´é¬10 and ´é¬9 under the ace and king. If West was not dealt the ´é¬3,
he canÔÇÖt avoid the endplay.
The full layout is:

the Freeze-PassIng endPlaY
Sometimes you need tricks from a frozen or half-frozen suit. If thatÔÇÖs the case, it
may be possible to play the suit at a time when the opponent will be endplayed,
thereby passing the freeze. Take a look at this deal:
Dealer East
´éź K 8 6 5

´éę Q 9 8 7 6 5
1. Promising four trumps.
´éĘ K 4
West leads the ´éĘQ. What is your plan?
You need to negotiate the spade suit for one loser. In an effort to get more
information, you win the ´éĘK, cross to the ´éęA and lead a low heart. East plays the
ace, king and queen of hearts and you ruff. You play a diamond to the king, the
suit splitting 2-2 (East has jack doubleton). Now what?
The opponents play a 15-17 notrump, and it seems that East has the ´éźA, so he
has 14 HCP known. Thus, West has the ´éźJ. You play a club to the ace and ruff a
club, West playing low (not the best play to conceal the club layout). At this point
you know East is ?=4=2=3+, so 3=4=2=4 seems most likely. Is there anything you
can do about it?
If East has ´éźA9x, you can lead the ´éź10. West must cover with the jack and
you play the king. East wins the ´éźA but must either give you a ruff-sluff or return
a spade, which you duck around to dummyÔÇÖs ´éź8. You passed the freeze in spades
at a point when East would be endplayed, hence the name.

the lunar Menace access squeeze
In their classic Adventures in Cardplay, Ottlik and Kelsey described a squeeze threat
to which there is no entry as being ÔÇśon the moonÔÇÖ ÔÇö hence the name ÔÇślunar menaceÔÇÖ.
The idea is to set up a squeeze or endplay that forces an opponent to give you access
to the lunar menace. It sounds far-fetched, but here is a deal from recent play:
´éź 4 3
´é¬ Q 4 3
Dealer South
´éę 8 7 5 4 3
EW Vul.
´éĘ A Q 3
West North East South
´éź A 2 1NT
´é¬ A 10 9 7 all pass
´éę J 10 9 2
´éĘ K J 8
West leads the ´éźQ; you win the ace and try the ´éęJ. West looks it over and plays
low, allowing EastÔÇÖs king to win the trick. They run four more spade tricks. What
cards do you come down to?
You have to make three discards from each hand. It would be dangerous to
discard down to 2-2 in hearts, or to stiff either honor, so you can afford one heart
from each hand. You want to keep your three club tricks, so you can part with one
club from dummy. You want to try to establish diamond trick(s), so you pitch two
diamonds from hand and one from dummy. In other words, dummy throws one
card from each suit, while you throw a heart and two diamonds from hand.
Declarer Play * 151

---

East has the long spade (on which West discards a club), and he returns a
club after the spades run out. Naturally you keep the position flexible by winning
the ace and then you lead a diamond, but East pitches a club on this trick. West
returns another club. The cards left are as follows:
´éź ÔÇö
´é¬ Q 4
´éę 8 7
´éĘ Q
´éź ÔÇö
´é¬ A 10 9
´éę ÔÇö
´éĘ K J
You have three of the five remaining tricks (restock) and would like a fourth. West
still has one top diamond so you canÔÇÖt surrender any tricks to him, except perhaps
in diamonds. What are your options at this point?
1. You could win with the ´éĘQ and lead the ´é¬Q. This only works if East has
the ´é¬KJ. Worse, if this trick goes queen, king, ace and West then wins
your ´é¬9 with the jack, you will have to guess which winner to pitch at
Trick 12 when West cashes his diamond.
2. You could win with the ´éĘQ and lead low to the ´é¬9, winning if East has
the ´é¬KJ. At least this way if you are wrong you will still take three tricks.
3. You could win with the ´éĘQ and exit with a diamond, hoping West has no
more clubs; you would then have a guess on his forced heart return. West
would have to be 4=3=3=3, not 4=2=3=4.
4. You could overtake with the ´éĘK in hand and cash the ´éĘJ. Your plan is
to play West for the ´é¬J and East for the ´é¬K. The problem is that West
might have ´é¬Jxx and you would be left with a diamond loser in dummy.
However, in this four-card ending:
´éź ÔÇö
´é¬ Q 4
´éę 8 7
´éĘ ÔÇö
´éź ÔÇö ´éź ÔÇö
´é¬ J x x ´é¬ K x x
´éę A ´éę ÔÇö
´éĘ ÔÇö ´éĘ x
´éź ÔÇö
´é¬ A 10 9
´éę ÔÇö
´éĘ J

when you lead the ´éĘJ, West is squeezed. If he discards a heart, you lead the ´é¬9,
planning to run it and overtake the heart return to drop the jack. If West pitches
his ´éęA, establishing the ÔÇślunar menaceÔÇÖ in dummy, then you run the ´é¬9 and let
dummy take the last two tricks.
Which line is best? I think the last one. East very likely has the ´é¬K since he
didnÔÇÖt switch to a heart. If he has the ´é¬K he is less likely to have the ´é¬J, both from
raw probability and the fact that West might have discarded a discouraging heart
with only low cards in the suit.
Intent to deceIVe (the laIr PlaY)
I havenÔÇÖt talked much yet about falsecards, and I shall indeed get to them in
Chapter 8. However, before we end our discussion of tactical ideas for declarer, I
want to mention one specific situation where falsecards can be useful.
Count signals help a lot on defense, but without help from the auction, an
opponent may not be able to narrow down his partnerÔÇÖs exact holding right away.
It might be difficult to determine whether an even count signal is from a two- or
a four-card holding, for example. Declarer can sometimes take advantage of this
with a well-timed falsecard. I executed this one against Mark Lair (the Texan expert
and professional player, on occasion my teammate).
´éź A K 2

´éę J 5

took care of a losing club.

Chapter 6
deFense
I want to turn now to tactics that are available to the defenders. Some of them, as
you might expect, are related to the declarer tactics I described in Chapter 5; others
are quite distinct. As I did in the previous chapter, IÔÇÖll start with some ideas that
revolve around entries ÔÇö often a weakness for declarer that the defense can exploit.
gougIng
Gouging involves leading or following suit with a high card which, although unable
to win the trick or promote anything, nevertheless pins declarer in the wrong hand.
This type of play can be made for a variety of reasons.
´éź Q 10 7 4 2
´éź A J 6 ´éź 8 3
´éź K 9 5
When declarer leads low to the king, you can win and if necessary return the jack
to pin declarer in the dummy. This gouging play will be successful when partner
has a trump and declarer canÔÇÖt get off the dummy, or when you have a finessable
trump position and declarer canÔÇÖt get to hand in time for the finesse.
Here is a full deal example of gouging:
´éź K 3
Dealer South
´é¬ Q 10 7 6 5

´éę 7 5 4

and plays a spade. Not wanting to use up your fourth diamond exit card, you win
the ´éźA and return the ´éźQ, putting declarer in dummy. He leads a heart (partner
contributes the ´é¬8, playing standard signals) and you win declarerÔÇÖs king with your
ace. What now?

daYs oF thunder
In the Days of Thunder play, the defenders lead declarerÔÇÖs solid suit to create
impossible entry problems. This device takes its name from a Tom Cruise action
movie about NASCAR racing ÔÇö I felt it expressed the potentially dramatic effect
since several tricks may be gained.
This is the type of situation I have in mind:
´éź K Q J 8 5
´é¬ ÔÇö
´éę ÔÇö
´éĘ A K
´éź A 3
´é¬ ÔÇö
´éę ÔÇö
´éĘ Q J 8 7 4
If the defenders play a spade at this point, declarer has to guess which suit is
breaking. If he gets it wrong, declarer cannot take all the tricks.

Rather than return a club, Garozzo played back a diamond, a Days of Thunder
play. Passell won the diamond in dummy and his best shot seemed to be to cash
the ´éźJ, the potential ninth trick. (In fact, running the diamond winners works as
long as declarer reads NorthÔÇÖs discards correctly.2) However, this set up two good
spades for South. When declarer continued with the ´éĘJ, North won the ´éĘA and
led a heart to the ´é¬A to allow South to take the setting tricks.
An alternative for declarer after the diamond return is to strand the good spade
and lead the ´é¬J, but South can foil that attempt by rising with the ace and playing
another heart, setting up two tricks for North.
Declarer did nothing terrible here. The diamond play was so effective because
it compelled Passell to choose from several unappetizing alternatives:
ÔÇó blocking diamonds by winning in hand
ÔÇó stranding the spade winner
ÔÇó cashing the ´éźJ while both round-suit aces were still outstanding
The possible effect of the diamond play could be inferred from declarerÔÇÖs work on
spades, a scrappy 5-1 fit, which made it quite evident that he thought nine tricks
were there for the taking on the expected club return. Declarer couldnÔÇÖt be weak in
hearts, so all that was left was to attempt to screw up his communications.

The club play works because the heart suit is blocked. If you return a diamond or

suIt suFFocatIon PlaY3
The idea of this play is that a defender leads away from his honor into a long semi-
solid suit in the dummy, playing declarer for a singleton in the suit. The thinking
behind it is that partner will have a losing trump left to ruff with when declarer
continues with a ruffing finesse. It works well when the suit is 2-6-4-1 round the
table, with you sitting over the dummy holding Kxxx or Axxx.
Other conditions of the play are:
ÔÇó Declarer can draw partnerÔÇÖs trump (the suit suffocator) if you donÔÇÖt lead
dummyÔÇÖs long suit.
ÔÇó Declarer needs to establish more than two tricks in dummyÔÇÖs long suit.
ÔÇó Dummy lacks the two entries declarer needs to establish the rest of the suit
and then get back to dummy after trumps are drawn.
Here is an example:
Dealer North
´éź A Q J 10 9 5
Both Vul.
´é¬ Q 7 3
´éę ÔÇö West North East South
´éĘ K 7 4 3 1´éź pass 2´é¬
´éź K 7 6 2 pass 4´é¬ pass 4NT*
´é¬ K 10 5 pass 6´é¬* all pass
´éę A J 8 4 2
´éĘ 10
Partner leads the ´éę6 (an unreadable spot card), ruffed in dummy. When declarer
plays dummyÔÇÖs ´é¬Q, you cover with the king and it holds the trick! Now what?
Declarer would be unlikely to duck holding ´é¬AJxxxx, so it is reasonable to
place him with ´é¬AJxxx. He didnÔÇÖt know dummy was going to turn up with a
diamond void, so he canÔÇÖt be missing the ´éĘA. Partner would likely have led a
spade with a singleton, so place declarer with the stiff. If you lead back anything
but a spade, declarer will have little choice other than to draw trumps and try the
(successful) ruffing finesse in spades, with the ´éĘK as a re-entry.
This is the layout you are hoping for:
3. I confess that my original name for this play was inappropriate for a family audience, and I was
persuaded that some revision was in order. E.R.

The suit suffocation play works quite well. On a spade shift declarer can win the
´éź9 and then run the ´éź10. Now, though, he has to draw trumps and he canÔÇÖt do
that ending in dummy. The best he can do is to take three spades, five trumps
(including the diamond ruff) and three clubs for eleven tricks.
True, declarer might make, double dummy, by taking your ´é¬K, drawing a
second round of trumps and then playing a spade to the ace and the ´éźQ (which
would be a losing line with spades 3-3). But this does not detract from the beauty
of the spade suit suffocation play.
The play is so named because you are reaching into their good AQJ10 suit and
smothering the life out of it. The suit canÔÇÖt live if you donÔÇÖt give it room, and time
enough, to breathe.
ProtectIng a MInor tenace
When you think that partner has led a singleton, it is usually right to reject what
might otherwise be an automatic cover. If declarer is short of entries, you can
create real problems for him.
Suppose clubs are a side suit that declarer has bid and partner leads the ´éĘ2 in
this situation:
´éĘ 10 8 4 3
´éĘ 2 ´éĘ Q J 6 5
´éĘ A K 9 7
DonÔÇÖt cover if declarer plays the eight or ten from dummy. The idea is that declarer
canÔÇÖt repeat the finesse now (lest partner ruff), and if he is short on dummy re-
entries, this could let you take a trick eventually. He will need to draw trumps,
then re-enter dummy twice to pick up the suit.

---

HereÔÇÖs an example in a full deal:

´é¬ 5 4 3
´éę J 9 8 7
´éĘ K 3 2

´éź K 10 9 8 ´éź A J 6 5 3

´é¬ K 9 8 6 2 ´é¬ Q J 10 7

´éĘ 9 8 7 ´éĘ ÔÇö 1´éĘ

Partner leads the ´éę2, and declarer plays the nine from dummy. Duck this trick ÔÇö

the leFt JaB
The left jab is a very common tactic. It occurs when you play an unsupported
honor to disrupt the timing of an opponentÔÇÖs attempt to set up his suit. ItÔÇÖs a form
of Chinese High Card Promotion, but in a suit that the opponent holds strongly.
Look at this situation:
´éę A J 10 6
´éę K 8 4 or
´éę Q 8 4
When declarer leads this suit, it can be effective to go up with your honor if declarer
has two small. One of several good things might happen. Declarer might play you
for ´éęKQx, and waste a re-entry to hand to lead this suit again. Alternatively he
might duck, or he might not have enough dummy entries to develop the tricks he
needs.
DonÔÇÖt use the left jab from honor third versus a 6-2 fit; count on partner to
duck with honor doubleton offside. If partner knows you would always go up with
honor doubleton, he can duck with honor doubleton if you fail to rise, knowing
you have honor third. Of course, you have to be willing to let them take two fast
tricks as a trade-off for killing the suit.
162 * Part 2 ÔÇö Advanced Cardplay Maneuvers

---

The left jab can also be effective if declarer is trying to take a combination
finesse against two cards of unequal rank in your hand, as in this example:
´éĘ A J 9 3
´éĘ K 10 6 or
´éĘ Q 10 6
Now when you rise with an honor, declarer has to guess whether to finesse the nine
next round or play you for the KQ.

I could have made life much harder by going up with the ´é¬Q when declarer

and plays a spade, and I win and switch to my singleton club, setting up a ruff when
partner gets in with the ´é¬K. This is hardly clear-cut at single-dummy, however.
In basic terms, you should go up with your honor when they lead toward
dummy if you think declarer has two or three small. They must win (or be
Chinesed) and lead again toward dummy from hand, which they may not be able
to do.
I said at the start of this chapter that often similar ploys are available to both

is easier to spot as declarer where you know your sideÔÇÖs combined assets, as in this

164 * Part 2 ÔÇö Advanced Cardplay Maneuvers

If declarer ducks, he suffers the Chinese Promotion when you play a second
heart, and suddenly has five losers. If he wins, he must cash the diamonds or lose
them forever. If he leaves any diamonds in dummy, you will get two hearts (via a
duck from partner), two spades and a club. If he runs the diamonds, he can pitch
a club and a heart from his hand, but then what?
If declarer sees this coming, he may realize that the club play at Trick 5 is a
mistake. A spade instead is the winning line, although the way home is far from
straightforward.
This is the full deal:
´éź 7 4
´é¬ A 10 5
´éę A K Q J 6 4
´éĘ Q 3

´éź Q J 8 6 3

´éĘ K J 10 7
the 007 PlaY ÔÇö lIcense to kIll
In the 007 play you continue playing a suit even though that establishes a winner
for the opponents, knowing that you can keep leading it and let partner ruff the
winner with a worthless trump. It is most commonly used as a defense against a
potential squeeze (on yourself, later), but could equally well be used just to kill a
winner, as in this example:
´éź Q 6
´é¬ J 10 9 5
´éź A 4
´é¬ A K Q 5
Defending against a spade contract you lead the ´é¬K and partner plays the two,
promising an odd number. You can continue with your remaining top hearts,
setting up dummyÔÇÖs jack, knowing that you can win the first round of trumps
with the ace and play your last heart: partner will ruff and kill dummyÔÇÖs winner.
Of course, if declarer has advertised a long spade suit you run the risk of partner
having either a singleton trump, or doubleton king in trumps, so this play is not
automatically correct.

a fourth heart, and West scores the ´éźJ.

ruFFIng declarerÔÇÖs loser
Part 4 of this book discusses many situations on defense where it is necessary to do
something counter-intuitive. HereÔÇÖs a glimpse of whatÔÇÖs to come there: even when
declarer leads a card from dummy that you are sure your partner can win, it might
still be right to ruff. For example, you may want to be on lead
ÔÇó to continue with a forcing defense.
ÔÇó to return a trump in order to kill ruff(s) or break communications.
ÔÇó to play a side suit from your side.
HereÔÇÖs an example:

´éź A 10 3
´é¬ 5 4
´éę A 7 6 5
´éĘ J 6 3 2

´éź 9 ´éź 7 6 5 4

´é¬ Q 10 9 8 7 ´é¬ 3 2

´é¬ A K J 6 all pass
´éę Q 3
´éĘ Q 5
With no attractive lead, partner (West) punts with his singleton trump. Declarer
wins in hand, plays the two top hearts, and ruffs a heart in dummy with the ´éźA.
How do you defend?
Partner has shown odd heart count, so declarer still needs to ruff another heart
in the dummy. There is no chance if declarer has a winner, or a hand entry, in
either minor, so prepare yourself to get in by discarding a diamond on the third
round of hearts. Then, when declarer plays ace and another diamond, you ruff and
return a spade to remove dummyÔÇÖs last trump.
Declarer can do better as the cards lie by playing the ´é¬A, the ´éęA and the ´é¬K
and then exiting with the ´éęQ to the king. Then the way for the heart ruffs is clear.
unBlockIng For a Pusher
WeÔÇÖve looked as some special suit combinations from declarerÔÇÖs point of view, but
there are many situations where a defender has to unblock or be alert to a potential
problem.
For example, with KJ108 in front of A5 in dummy, it canÔÇÖt really cost to stick
in the ten when partner returns the suit. If partner has say 973, your unblock has
made room for his ÔÇśpusherÔÇÖ (the nine) to be effective in retaining the lead to trap
declarerÔÇÖs queen. This is the position you are catering for:
´é¬ A 5
´é¬ K J 10 8 ´é¬ 9 7 3
´é¬ Q 6 4 2
The play goes seven, two, ten, ace, and you run the suit if East gets in again to lead
the ´é¬9, his pusher. This play is also effective if West has AJ108 in front of Kx or
Qx.

the unBlockIng concessIon
In this play you are conceding a trick and unblocking at the same time (thus,
leading a middle card). The idea is to retain communication with partner, and/or
not to rectify the loser count.
´é¬ J 5 4 3
´é¬ K Q 10 6
When you lead the king or queen, declarer wins the ace, partner playing the ´é¬8.
Next time you are in, lead the ´é¬10, and either steal a cheap trick or set up your long
card while maintaining flexibility.
MIddlIng
Middling is a play where the middle card of a holding is led, usually by a defender,
in order to be able to run a suit. Middling is an unblocking play and should be
distinguished from a surrounding play, which works like this:
´éź Q 7 4
´éź K J 9 6 ´éź A 8 2
´éź 10 5 3
Here West leads the ´éźJ, enabling the defense to run four tricks in the suit. If
dummy covers, East wins the ´éźA and WestÔÇÖs ´éźK9 surrounds the ´éź10.
By contrast, here are some examples of middling:
´éź Q 7 3 2
´éź K 9 4 ´éź A J 8 6
´éź 10 5
If West starts with the king and then follows with the nine, declarer ducks and East
has to win with the jack (note that declarerÔÇÖs ´éź10 is a crossover stopper). If four
tricks are needed from this suit, West will need another entry to lead it. However,
if West leads the nine first time and declarer plays low from dummy, East can win
with the jack and return the six. Now when West wins with the king the defenders
can cash two more tricks at once.
´éę J 4
´éę A Q 8 3 ´éę K 9 6
´éę 10 7 5 2
Here, East must lead the nine to secure four fast tricks.
Defense * 169

---

´é¬ J 6
´é¬ A Q 8 3 ´é¬ K 10 5
´é¬ 9 7 4 2
To ensure four tricks can be taken on the go, East must start by leading the ten.
West wins with the queen and returns the three.
´éĘ J 8 6 2
´éĘ K 10 3 ´éĘ A Q 7 4
´éĘ 9 5
Once again it is vital for West to start with the ten if the defenders want to get four
tricks immediately.
These plays can be hard to recognize because the middle spot looks like top
of nothing when it is led (this is especially true on the opening lead). The context,
i.e. the need to run this suit, should serve to alert partner to what you are doing.
the reese PlaY
This is related to middling, but now it is the player with the four-card length who
has to do the hard work, leading high to preserve communication with partner,
who has honor third. The idea is to establish the suit so that it can be run with only
one subsequent lead from the honor third hand, not two.
HereÔÇÖs the basic situation (the position was first described by Terence Reese in
his classic Reese on Play):
´éź J 9
´éź Q 10 7 5 ´éź K 6 3
´éź A 8 4 2
Suppose West starts with the five. Declarer takes EastÔÇÖs king with the ace, and
now in order to run the suit East will need at least one and possibly two entries. If
West leads the queen instead, the best declarer can do is duck the first round (if he
wins the ace, the suit is ready to roll and either player can lead it when they get in).
When the queen holds, West leads the ´éź5 to EastÔÇÖs king; declarer must either win
and hope to keep East off lead, or duck and make West the danger hand.

If you lead a low heart to the jack and king, you are ripe for an endplay (declarer

defense is to cash the ´éęK and get out with a club. As you might have noticed,
declarer initially had a number of alternative lines of play that allowed him to make
his contract.
the MortonÔÇÖs Fork concessIon
As I reminded you in the previous chapter, MortonÔÇÖs Fork is a coup that forces an
opponent to choose between two evils: letting declarer establish one or more extra
tricks in the suit led, and losing the opportunity to win any trick in that suit.
The MortonÔÇÖs Fork concession arises when a defender concedes an opponentÔÇÖs
winner before that winner can be used as a MortonÔÇÖs Fork threat, and/or before the
requisite position for the Fork has been established.
This is a typical example:
´éź 9 7 5 2
´é¬ 8 4
´éę A 8 5 3
´éĘ 9 7 4
´éź K Q 10 8 4 3 ´éź 6 Dealer East
´é¬ J 10 6 3 ´é¬ 7 Neither Vul.
´éę J ´éę K Q 10 9 6 4 2
´éĘ K 3 ´éĘ Q 6 5 2 West North East South
´éź A J 3´éę 4´é¬
´é¬ A K Q 9 5 2 all pass
´éę 7
´éĘ A J 10 8

When you lead the ´éęJ, declarer wins with dummyÔÇÖs ace and plays four rounds of
hearts. After winning the ´é¬J, you exit with the ´éźK. Declarer takes the ace and
returns the jack. When you win this trick you have reached this position:
´éź 9 7
´é¬ ÔÇö
´éę 8
´éĘ 9 7 4
´éź 10 8 4 3 ´éź ÔÇö
´é¬ ÔÇö ´é¬ ÔÇö
´éę ÔÇö ´éę Q 10
´éĘ K 3 ´éĘ Q 6 5 2
´éź ÔÇö
´é¬ 9 5
´éę ÔÇö
´éĘ A J 10 8
If you think you know the club position (see empty signal ÔÇö the next section),
now is the time to lead a low spade. That will leave declarer in the dummy when
it wonÔÇÖt do him any good (if he takes a club finesse, you win and exit with a high
spade).
If instead you exit with the natural-looking ´éź10, declarer (who has a count on
the hand) can ruff and lead the ´éĘJ from hand to leave you with a choice of evils.
First, you can duck and have your ´éĘK fall under the ace next time. Second, you
can win and allow declarer to repeat the club finesse (either you have to play a
club yourself or you have to give him access to dummy with the ´éź9). Note that by
playing the ´éĘJ, declarer was unblocking for a pusher.

´éź 7 4 3

´é¬ A Q

Partner leads the ´éę2 (playing third- and fifth-best leads) and declarer plays the ´éę10
under your ace. What now?
It looks like trumps are 5-5 between declarer and partner, since partner
doubled. Declarer should have most of the missing high cards to bid 4´éź on a five-

probably the ´éęK10. Partner would likely have led a club if he had a singleton or
doubleton, so he is probably void. Thus partner is likely 5=5=3=0.

´éź 7 4 3

´éę 5 4 3

´éĘ 6 2
East did play another diamond, so I won and played a heart to the queen. Now I

if he leads a heart I score my other small trump), and if West doesnÔÇÖt ruff, the ´éĘA

the ´é¬10, but I have no way to score any small trumps. I could prevail in various
ways if only I had the ´éź8.
Returning the ´éĘK is indeed a MortonÔÇÖs Fork Concession in that it executes the
MortonÔÇÖs Fork before IÔÇÖm ready, at a point when West has a winning defense, the
diamond discard.
the eMPtY sIgnal
It hardly needs stating that signaling is a critical part of defending. LetÔÇÖs look at
some situations where you may not have thought about the possibility of conveying
a message to partner.
If you have honor fourth in a suit, with very low spots, such that they can
never be promoted, it could be helpful to partner to pitch one to say ÔÇśmy holding is
174 * Part 2 ÔÇö Advanced Cardplay Maneuvers

emptyÔÇÖ i.e. ÔÇśI have no useful intermediatesÔÇÖ. If partner has a count on the hand, he
will realize that this is an abnormal play, especially when declarer might have four
cards in the suit. This can help him find the right defense, as in my first example
of a MortonÔÇÖs Fork Concession (see p. 172).
This was the position with West to play:
´éź 9 7
´é¬ ÔÇö
´éę 8
´éĘ 9 7 4
´éź 10 8 4 3 ´éź ÔÇö
´é¬ ÔÇö ´é¬ ÔÇö
´éę ÔÇö ´éę Q 10
´éĘ K 3 ´éĘ Q 6 5 2
´éź ÔÇö
´é¬ 9 5
´éę ÔÇö
´éĘ A J 10 8
At this point partner is marked with a 1=1=7=4 distribution. If declarer has
´éĘAQxx, you must exit with the ´éĘK (or the ´éź10, planning to unblock the ´éĘK under
the ace), since declarer will then have just nine tricks. However, if declarer has
´éĘAJ108, then you need to execute the MortonÔÇÖs Fork Concession and give him his
spade trick now.
How can you tell which holding declarer has?
If partner has ´éĘJ108x (his holding when declarer has ´éĘAQxx), he clearly canÔÇÖt
afford to throw one. However, with ´éĘQ6xx, he knows that keeping four clubs
canÔÇÖt be necessary (declarer, provided he has the ´éĘAK, will ruff the high spade
return and play ace, king and a third club, setting up whatever club spot he has).
If declarer is 3=6=1=3, then a club pitch is okay, and if West has the ´éĘKx then it
is right (and necessary) to alert him to the chance to execute the MortonÔÇÖs Fork
Concession.
cashIng the IMPuted truMP ace to ask For a ruFF
When you switch in the middle of a hand, partner may have difficulty guessing
whether you have a singleton or doubleton, and therefore what his correct play
should be. However, in some situations there is a way to help him. If you are
known to have the trump ace (usually partner assumes this, because there is no way
of defeating the contract without it), and are playing for ruffs, cash the trump ace
before leading a singleton. If you fail to cash your ÔÇśknownÔÇÖ trump ace first, partner
should, and will, play you for a doubleton.
Defense * 175

---

HereÔÇÖs an example of the situation you are catering for. Defending 4´éź, partner
leads a heart and you see this dummy:
´éź K Q J 6
´é¬ 7
´éę A K Q 5 3
´éĘ K Q 8
´éź A 7 4
´é¬ A 8 6 5 2
´éę 9 6 4
´éĘ J 4
Since trumps are 3-2, your only chance for three more tricks is two black aces and
a ruff in one of the minors.
You take the ´é¬A and return a club. Partner will duck this; then, when you get
in with the ´éźA, you can play your remaining club. If partner has the ´éĘA, you will
score a ruff on the third round of the suit. Just as you assume that partner has the
´éĘA, without which there is no chance for you to beat 4´éź, partner must assume that
you have the ´éźA. Given that, when you donÔÇÖt cash the ´éźA partner can infer that
your club is a doubleton. Instead, if your hand is
´éź A 7 4 ´é¬ A 8 6 5 2 ´éę 9 7 6 4 ´éĘ J
then after winning with the ´é¬A you should cash the ace of trumps first, and only
then lead a club. Now partner will know you have a singleton and return a club
for you to ruff.

clarIFYIng the lead oF an unsuPPorted honor
IÔÇÖll finish this chapter with three quite general ideas that you should add to your
arsenal.
One of the awkward areas for the defenders is when they have to attack a suit
where dummy has a modest holding such as three small. Sitting over the dummy
it can be helpful to lead the king from, say, K75 when partner has something along
the lines of AQxx or AJxx. You might also consider leading the queen from Qxx,
particularly if you need to run the suit. If dummy has 10xx and you find partner
with AKxx, this would save him from having to guess whether to underlead back
to you or to try to drop declarerÔÇÖs Qx. This play would also expose declarerÔÇÖs duck
from Kxx.
If dummy has three small, then a common agreement is to lead a low card from
suits headed by the ace or king and second highest from other combinations unless
you have a worthwhile sequence.
Another reason to consider an eccentric lead arises after a lead-directing
double, which may persuade you to try something unusual. For example, imagine
the layout looks like this (an approximate reconstruction of a deal from the 1993
Camrose Trials):

BaIt-and-swItch
The bait-and-switch play derives its name from the idea that before setting up
your long card(s) ÔÇö usually in a 4-4 fit ÔÇö you switch to another suit. A common
example is one in which you lead through declarerÔÇÖs ace when he canÔÇÖt afford to win
the trick (lest he open up that suit), with the idea that partner will win and switch
back to your 4-4 fit.
This is a typical situation:
´éź J 7 4
´é¬ 7 5
Dealer South
´éę K J 10 5
NS Vul.
´éĘ K J 10 4
´éź K 9 8 3
West North East South
´é¬ J 10 6 3
1NT
´éę A 6 pass 3NT all pass
´éĘ A 7 3
Partner leads the ´é¬2, which goes to the five, ten and ace. It looks like declarer has
´é¬AKx, but two hearts and two aces still comes to only four tricks for the defense.
When a minor is led, win and return a spade, the bait-and-switch play. If declarer
canÔÇÖt afford to go up, you are hoping partner will win and work it out.
This is a possible layout:
´éź J 7 4
´é¬ 7 5
´éę K J 10 5
´éĘ K J 10 4
´éź Q 6 2 ´éź K 9 8 3
´é¬ Q 9 8 2 ´é¬ J 10 6 3

´éĘ 6 2 ´éĘ A 7 3
´éź A 10 5
´é¬ A K 4
´éę Q 7 3
´éĘ Q 9 8 5
Unless declarer is playing a very deep game, partner should realize it must be right
to go back to hearts after winning with the ´éźQ.
One way to help partner make the right decision if you are trying for a bait-
and-switch is to lead a higher card than necessary (for example, the ´éź9 here), to let
partner know not to keep leading the suit.

oPtIon-reMoVIng Pseudo-squeeze
We have seen that a good declarer tries to combine as many chances as possible,
rather than putting all his eggs in one basket. Your job as a defender is to force him
to commit as early as you can, preferably steering him toward a losing line of play.
For example, when an opponent has several options that he hasnÔÇÖt been able to test,
if you can make him discard early he might go wrong.

then leads a club to dummyÔÇÖs king. How do you defend?

Is it possible that declarer does not have the ´éĘQ? To have even 19 HCP he
would need all the other facecards. If his hand is
´éź A K Q 10 ´é¬ x x ´éę A K Q J ´éĘ x x x
he needs two club tricks if spades arenÔÇÖt running, and surely wouldnÔÇÖt play the suit
this way. It seems highly unlikely.
So, give declarer the ´éĘQ. If this is the case, then if declarer is missing the ´éęQ,
you can try messing with his communications. Duck two rounds of clubs and win
the third (partner follows, so declarer started with ´éĘQxxx). Now that dummy is
out of side entries, return the ´é¬J.
HereÔÇÖs the full deal:
´éź 8 6 5
´é¬ A K Q 7
´éę 7 5 4
´éĘ K J 10
´éź 10 9 2 ´éź J 7 4
´é¬ 9 8 6 ´é¬ J 10 3 2
´éę Q 9 3 2 ´éę 10 8 6

´éź A K Q 3
´é¬ 5 4
´éę A K J
´éĘ Q 9 6 2
Declarer has to cash the third heart now or lose it, and that means he has to guess
whether to pitch his spade or the ´éęJ. The percentage play is to throw the spade,
and down he will go. You have removed his options, but the squeeze is pseudo
(not legitimate) hence the name.
Declarer would have been advised to anticipate this problem and, after the
second club was ducked, to test the spades. As long as the player with the long
spade (should they not split) doesnÔÇÖt have the ´éĘA, he will be able to fall back on
the diamond finesse. Of course if spades split then he knows what to pitch on the
third heart winner.

In this part of the book, IÔÇÖm going to look at ÔÇśbig-pictureÔÇÖ defense ÔÇö broad general
ideas that you need to bear in mind when youÔÇÖre planning your strategy. Of course,
each of them breaks down into specific situations and tactics, and IÔÇÖll try to give as
many different examples as I can. IÔÇÖve also tried to organize the topics as coherently
as possible, but inevitably there are some that could have appeared in a different
spot with equal logic.
LetÔÇÖs begin by looking at some ideas that are specific to trump contracts ÔÇö
either you want to wrest trump control away from declarer or you are trying to
prevent him from using his trumps in the way that he would like to.
taPPIng the duMMY
We all learn as beginners how effective it can be to tap, or force, declarer, shortening
his trumps until he loses control of the hand. There are situations, however, where
it is necessary to tap the dummy, the short trump hand. LetÔÇÖs look at some of the
reasons why this might be a good idea:

1. To promote trump tricks
Tapping the dummy is most likely to produce extra trump tricks when it contains
strong, but not long, trumps.
Here is a deal from the 1999 Bermuda Bowl:

´éę K 8 6

played the nine to his queen. Next he played the ´éęQ, which West took with the
ace. Now West could simply have played another spade to ensure a trump trick for
his partner, but instead he returned a diamond. Declarer won the trick in dummy
and played a club to the king. West ducked that and won the next club perforce.
A spade would still beat the hand, but West played a diamond, forcing declarer
instead of dummy. Now declarer could ruff, draw trumps and enjoy the clubs.

to ruFF or not to ruFF
Refusing to ruff is not common (although I will talk about one such situation
shortly), but there are a number of reasons why a player may refuse to overruff and
many of them are well known. For example, in the following position:
´éź 7 4
´éź K 10 3 ´éź 5
´éź A Q J 9 8 6 2
If South ruffs something with the queen or jack, West gains a trick by refusing to
overruff. LetÔÇÖs take a look at some other reasons why a defender may need to keep
his trump holding intact, or to do something else unusual.
1. You can win the battle for trump control
This deal, one of the most famous examples of refusing to overruff, comes from the
final of the 1985 North American Team Trials:
´éź Q 6 2
´é¬ 10 9 8 7 4 2
´éę J 8 5
Dealer South
´éĘ 7

´éź K 10 8 7 ´éź A J 9 5 4 3
´é¬ Q J 3 ´é¬ A K 5 West North East South
´éę 4 3 ´éę 10 9 2 Wolff Cohen Hamman Bergen
´éĘ K Q 10 8 ´éĘ 4 1´éĘ
´éź ÔÇö pass 1´é¬ 2´éź 3´éę
´é¬ 6 4´éź pass pass 4NT
´éę A K Q 7 6 pass 5´éę dbl all pass
´éĘ A J 9 6 5 3 2
West led the ´éź7. Declarer ruffed EastÔÇÖs jack, cashed the ´éĘA and ruffed a club low.
Rather than overruff, Hamman made the far-sighted play of discarding a heart.
When declarer continued with a heart, East won and played a spade. Declarer
ruffed and then ruffed a club with the ´éęJ, but when he had to get back to hand on
the next trick he lost trump control and went three down for -800. If East overruffs
at Trick 3, declarer escapes for one down. If declarer ruffs the third club with the
eight, East does best to overruff (to stop eight trump tricks on a crossruff) and then
continue the tap. This defense leads to the same down three.

refusal to overruff sees declarer lose control:

3. You are attempting a deception
A deceptive overruff, using a higher card than necessary, can pay dividends ÔÇö
indeed it may be essential.
´éź Q 10 9 4
´éź K J 6 3
´éź A 8 7 5
In this situation, suppose dummy ruffs something with the ´éź9 and you are in
a position to overruff. If you overruff with the jack declarer can pick up your
remaining trumps, but if you overruff the nine with the king, declarer will almost
certainly cash the ace next.
This is a celebrated example from the 1964 Olympiad:

accurate ForcIng deFense when a deFender has
axxx oF truMPs Versus a 5-3 FIt
A defender who holds Axxx of trumps can always consider the possibility of a
forcing defense; by the same token, a defender who holds a singleton trump should
be alive to the possibility that partner has four trumps.
The general technique is as follows:
Tricks 1-2 Cash a winner, then tap the five-card trump length with your
long suit.
Trick 3 Duck the trump ace.
Trick 4 Win the trump ace.
Trick 5 Tap the five-card length again. Now you have two small
trumps while declarer and dummy each have a singleton
honor.
Trick 6+ Declarer must try for trump surrogates, running a suit through
you so that when you ruff he can overruff and then re-enter
his hand to draw your last trump. What can the defense do
to counter this? You have to try to dump all your cards in
declarerÔÇÖs re-entry suit before ruffing a surrogate winner. Then
declarer is pinned in the other hand, forced to give you a ruff.
This deal illustrates the process:
´éź Q 8 7
´é¬ 6 5 4 3
´éę Q J 10 2
´éĘ K 4
Dealer South
´éź A 5 4 3 ´éź 2
Neither Vul.
´é¬ A K J 8 7 ´é¬ Q 10
´éę 7 6 ´éę 9 8 5 4 3 West North East South
´éĘ 7 5 ´éĘ 9 8 6 3 2 1´éź
´éź K J 10 9 6 2´é¬ 2´éź pass 4´éź
´é¬ 9 2 all pass
´éę A K
´éĘ A Q J 10
You cash two high hearts and lead a third round, which is ruffed. Declarer leads
the ´éźJ, and you let that hold the trick. When a second spade is led, you rise ace and
play a fourth heart, which is ruffed again. You have achieved the desired position:
two small trumps in your hand and two big singletons in theirs. Declarer can try
running clubs but as long as you pitch all your diamonds before ruffing you will
set the hand. It doesnÔÇÖt help declarer to cash his diamonds because you can ruff
Trump Contracts * 193

---

the third club, leaving declarer stranded in dummy, and then you score your other
small trump.
Note that ducking the trump ace twice wouldnÔÇÖt work ÔÇö declarer would cash
the ´éęAK then play clubs through you. With only one small trump left, you have
no second small trump to promote. If declarer knew you had four spades (perhaps
you doubled), he could decide not to lead a second trump, instead cashing two
diamonds and running clubs as before (the difference is that he hasnÔÇÖt ruffed a
second heart so he is still in control). As it is, declarer might elect to try to cash
four tricks in the minors then crossruff, paying off to 3-2 trumps and a minor-suit
singleton in your hand ÔÇö a line of play that would work nicely on this layout.

Chapter 8
FoIlIng declArer
Back in Chapter 1, I talked about the issue of choosing between an ÔÇśactiveÔÇÖ and a
ÔÇśpassiveÔÇÖ line of defense. We usually think of active defense as doing something
to set up our own tricks; however, quite a lot of the time it involves simply doing
something to prevent declarer having it all his own way. Under three broad
headings, this chapter looks at some tactics defenders have available for foiling
declarerÔÇÖs dastardly plots.
deFendIng agaInst a PossIBle endPlaY
We all hate being endplayed, and surprisingly often, if we are alert to the possibilities
early in the play, we can prevent it. LetÔÇÖs look at some of the ways to accomplish
this.
1. Cash a winner in a suit where declarer has a singleton
This type of play usually happens when dummy has a minor tenace over you and
you cash the ace to avoid being ÔÇśforkedÔÇÖ later. In the fork situation, either you duck
and let them steal the king, or you win and get endplayed. One typical situation
arises when you have AQx in front of KJx(x).
Foiling Declarer * 197

---

In this example from the 2008 World Bridge Games, the defenders had to be
wide awake.
´éź 6
´é¬ Q J 9 8 5
´éę J 6 4 Dealer North
´éĘ A 9 4 3 NS Vul.
´éź A Q 10 7 5 ´éź 9 4 3 2
West North East South
´é¬ A K 10 6 ´é¬ 7 4 2
Malinowski Stefanov Sandqvist Aronov
´éę K Q 2 ´éę 8 5
pass pass 1´éę1
´éĘ 6 ´éĘ K 10 8 2

´éź K J 8 2´éź 3´éĘ 3´éź pass
´é¬ 3 4´éź all pass
´éę A 10 9 7 3
1. Precision.
´éĘ Q J 7 5
North led the ´é¬Q, and declarer won in hand and played the ´éęQ. When South won
and returned the suit, declarer took the king, ruffed a diamond and played a spade
to the ten. When that held he played a club ÔÇö the fork. North could win, but
was endplayed: a club provides an entry to dummy for a spade finesse, and a heart
eliminates declarerÔÇÖs loser in the suit. The winning defense is for South to switch to
a club at Trick 3. North takes the ace and now goes back to diamonds. With only
one entry to dummy, declarer must lose a trick in each suit.
I also declared 4´éź on this deal, but North led a helpful trump, more or less
handing me the contract.
2. Upset their entries
Attacking entries is a continuing theme, as youÔÇÖve probably realized by now. From
earlier in the book, youÔÇÖll remember Days of Thunder (see p. 156) as one way to
accomplish this. There are other tactics that have the same purpose, which I shall
cover in Part 4 (the Merrimac Coup is one example).
3. Duck to keep communications with partner open
It is sound defensive technique to retain communications with partner, and refusing
to win a trick is sometimes the way to do that. Apart from being a potential defense
to a throw-in, it prevents declarer taking on one opponent at a time.
Suppose dummy has KQx and you sit over it with the ace. If you can tell you
need two tricks from this suit, it can be a good move to duck with the ace, either as
a deceptive move or to avoid being endplayed, especially if you have nothing good
to return if you win the trick.

4. Play a middle card
Sometimes when you can see the endplay on the horizon, you have to play a middle
card and hope partner has the spots to save you.
´éĘ Q x x
´éĘ A J x
Play the jack when a low club is led from dummy, if you feel an endplay threatening.
Partner will need ´éĘ108x or better if declarer can be given the lead, and ´éĘ109x or
better if partner wins a late trick.
5. Unblock
One step further than playing a middle card is unblocking. You can follow with a
big card under their bigger card, cash winners, or unblock a doubleton or tripleton
honor.

Partner leads the ´éź10 to your queen and declarerÔÇÖs ace. Now declarer runs his six-

endplay as long as you keep a link card in clubs. In this case you would discard
two clubs and two spades.
The key is that partner has to signal to let you know whether or not he has the
´éĘK. If he shows club encouragement, you throw some spades; if he discourages
in clubs (or encourages in hearts), you stiff your ´é¬K and throw all your small
clubs. The defender at our table threw three clubs and a heart, in spite of partnerÔÇÖs
encouraging club discard. Jeff then dropped his ´é¬K to make the hand.

8. Create a loser
Once you are aware of the possibilities, it becomes easier to make spectacular
unblocking plays as a defender if you are anxious not to be put on lead. For
example, you might unblock from a holding of J10xx when declarer cashes the
AKQ. I call this trick trashing. Keep in mind that this type of play will gain only
if you were going to be forced to give them two or more tricks when thrown in, for
example by giving declarer access to several stranded winners.

9. Refuse to win a trick that would rectify the count for a squeeze
Even the Rueful Rabbit knows that when the Hog wants him to win a trick, itÔÇÖs

10. Kill the menace (the 007 play)
You will recall that the 007 play (see p. 165) involves continuing a suit even if it
sets up a winner for declarer, the idea being that partner will be able to ruff with a
worthless trump.
This deal is from the 1998 Rosenblum:

with the king and jack ÔÇö no endplays in clubs! In fact, that forced declarer to ruff
high (else West would score a club ruff) and since he needed to ruff his last spade
with the ´é¬A, the defenders had to score a trump trick.
Declarer is actually tightroped into winning the ´éĘA at Trick 1, as East should
shift to a trump and stop any spade ruffs if the ´éĘ8 is allowed to hold.

13. The Endplay Prevention Cover
The endplay prevention cover involves covering an early honor (from touching
honors) when it is led, something you wouldnÔÇÖt normally do. The idea is to prevent
your being thrown in to lead around to the other honor later. Typically, this occurs
when you hold Kxx(x) over QJx in dummy. Here is deal from a recent knockout
match:
´éź 9 5 3
´é¬ 10 8 4
´éę Q J 3
´éĘ Q 7 5 4
Dealer South
´éź K 10 8 2 ´éź J 6
Neither Vul.
´é¬ J 9 5 ´é¬ K 7 6 2
´éę 10 5 4 2 ´éę K 9 6 West North East South
´éĘ K J ´éĘ 10 8 6 2 2NT
´éź A Q 7 4 pass 3NT all pass
´é¬ A Q 3
´éę A 8 7
´éĘ A 9 3
West led the ´éź2, fourth best, which went to the jack and the queen. Things didnÔÇÖt
look good at all, but I led a low club to WestÔÇÖs king, and he played the ´éźK. Doing
an asset survey, I watched to see if East had ´éźJ8 doubleton, which would allow me
Foiling Declarer * 213

---

to power out a third spade trick, but no such luck. I decided to win this trick, both
in case I wanted to exit in spades later and to avoid giving West the count in spades
(if he pitches one that is good for me).
Rather hopefully, I now played the ´éĘA, West dropping the ´éĘJ, and led a low
club. West pitched the ´é¬5 (encouraging); he didnÔÇÖt know what I had and was
catering to my having king fourth of diamonds. I won this trick with the ´éĘQ and
led the ´éęQ from dummy, and East missed his chance when he didnÔÇÖt cover. With
both defenders fully isolated from each other, I led a heart to the queen, cashed the
´é¬A and exited with a third heart. As I was +3L, East could take the ´éĘ10 and two
heart tricks, but he had to give me a trick with the ´éęJ at the end.
Note that, since East holds the ´éę9, the cover is safe (if I had ace-ten doubleton
West would pitch a diamond). If he has ´éęKxx, there is some risk my diamonds
might be ´éęA98 and that covering will cost a trick. This will matter only if West has
the ´é¬Q (same endplay if he doesnÔÇÖt) and in that case three diamond tricks give me
only eight tricks. The real risk is that my hand is
´éź A Q x x ´é¬ A J ´éę A 9 8 x ´éĘ A 9 x
and now the diamond cover from Kxx will be a disaster. However, that leaves West
with ´éźK108x and ´é¬Q9xx as his majors, from which many players would lead a
heart, not a spade.
14. Avoid the trump-tight ending
It is common for a textbook to explain why you should not double a 6´éź contract
sitting behind the long suit with ´éźKQx: declarer will try to eliminate all your side-
suit cards, leading to a trump-tight ending, and endplay you. The same is true of
other ÔÇśstrongÔÇÖ trump holdings.
Try this deal, from the 2011 NABC in Louisville:
Dealer North
´éź A J 10 9
NS Vul.
´é¬ 7
´éę A K J 8 5 West North East South
´éĘ K 10 9 1´éę pass 1´é¬
2´é¬1 dbl2 pass 4´éĘ
´éź 2 pass 5´éĘ dbl all pass
´é¬ A J 6 3
1. Natural.
´éę Q 3 2. Extra values, takeout.
´éĘ J 6 5 4 3 2
West leads the ´é¬K and you win with the ace. How do you play from here?
214 * Part 3 ÔÇö Defensive Strategies

---

If you figure East to have ´éĘAQ87 for his double, then you need to get to the
right trump-tight ending. If East has a second heart, then if you lead trump now he
just leads his other heart to tap dummy and build a third trump trick. So you want
to ruff in hand with your small clubs. Playing the ´éźA and ruffing a spade seems like
a good start; now, what ending are you angling for?
If you ruff a heart and have to lead a club from hand at any point, East gets
three trump tricks. So, you have to be in dummy at a point when East is trump
tight, or has a heart left. In any event, East has seven cards in spades and diamonds
and you must eliminate them before he can ruff in or discard his remaining heart.
You want the key late lead to be off dummy. Since you need to ruff one or
perhaps two more spades, you need to use dummyÔÇÖs diamond honors as entries:
play a diamond to the ace, then a spade covered by the king and ruffed, and now
the ´éęQ overtaken in dummy and the ´éęJ. When West discards, it looks like East is
?=2=4=4, so 3=2=4=4 (fourth suit forcing).
Thus, you should lead a fourth diamond, ruffing in hand, and ruff a heart. East
is now trump tight. When you lead a spade from dummy he ruffs with the ´éĘ7.
You overruff, lead a heart and discard from dummy. East must ruff and lead from
his ´éĘAQ into dummyÔÇÖs ´éĘK10 at Trick 12 to let you make the hand.
I donÔÇÖt know if Steve Weinstein would have found this line of play if I had
doubled 5´éĘ with the East cards, but as I smoothly passed at all points he took the
normal line of leading a trump at Trick 2. I did have ´éĘAQ87, so I merely cashed
two high trumps and led a heart to tap dummy for the setting trick.
The full deal looked like this:
´éź A J 10 9

counterIng a holduP PlaY
There are many situations where declarer can cause the defenders problems by
holding up, i.e. delaying taking his winner in the suit they are attacking. This can
cut communications, and also can create tenace and crossover stopper positions.
However, the defense is not without potential counter-measures.
1. Crossing over to partner to return the suit
ItÔÇÖs usually obvious to a defender when a suit cannot be continued from his side.
In that situation, you need to find partnerÔÇÖs entry, or his most likely entry, for a
lead through.
´éź J 5 2
´é¬ 8 5 3
´éę A Q J
´éĘ K Q 10 8
Dealer South
´éź 9 8 3 ´éź A 10 7 6
Both Vul.
´é¬ K Q 10 9 ´é¬ 7 4 2
´éę 7 6 3 ´éę 9 5 2 West North East South
´éĘ A 9 2 ´éĘ 6 4 3 1NT1
´éź K Q 4 pass 3NT all pass
´é¬ A J 6
1. 14-16.
´éę K 10 8 4
´éĘ J 7 5
West leads the ´é¬K, asking East to unblock an honor or give count. When it holds
and East follows with the two, it is clear that South started with ´é¬AJx. West needs
to get his partner in to play a heart through declarer. A diamond is one possibility,
in the hope that partner might have the king, but declarer might well be able to go
up with the ace on a diamond switch and then drive out the ´éĘA. So switching to
the ´éź9 is a better chance. East takes the ace and switches back to hearts, leaving
declarer dead in the water.

2. Switching and never letting them get to a stranded holdup card
This is a deal from a recent team game:
´éź A 9 3
´é¬ 10 7 6 5
´éę 10 7 6
´éĘ 7 6 5
´éź Q 10 8 4 2 ´éź K J 5 Dealer South
´é¬ 8 2 ´é¬ Q J 9 3 Both Vul.
´éę 9 4 2 ´éę Q J 8 5
´éĘ 9 8 3 ´éĘ A 4 West North East South
´éź 7 6 2NT
´é¬ A K 4 pass 3NT all pass
´éę A K 3
´éĘ K Q J 10 2
Sitting West, I led the ´éź4, which was ducked to the king, and overtook partnerÔÇÖs ´éźJ
return, declarer again ducking. With the spade suit now dead, I felt my best hope
was to strand the ´éźA by exiting in another suit, and the ´éĘ9 looked like the safest
switch. Indeed, as long as East pitches one card from each suit on the run of the
clubs, 3NT goes down.
3. Stealing one or more tricks then switching to a new suit
One of the great arts of defending against notrump contracts is knowing when to
switch the attack to a new suit. This may happen (as on the previous example)
when a defender has taken one or more tricks in his long suit but is then entryless.
However, it can also happen even when a defender has a possible entry, as on this
deal from an international event cited in ForquetÔÇÖs Bridge with the Blue Team:

West led the ´éźQ. After winning perforce with dummyÔÇÖs king, declarer played a

ducks and is in control despite having to lose a club to East.
However, West ducked the ´éęQ. If declarer now went after the clubs, East
would win and the defenders would be able to clear the spade suit while West still
had an entry, so declarer played a second diamond. West had to win that, and now
he played a second top spade, which declarer had to duck. Having stolen a spade
trick, West switched to a diamond, and when East got in with the ´éĘQ he had two
diamond tricks to cash.

4. Bait-and-Switch
I talked about this in Chapter 6, but itÔÇÖs an important concept, so thereÔÇÖs no harm in
mentioning it again here ÔÇö especially as it is closely related to the last point. You
will recall that in bait-and-switch, the defenders start with one suit, steal a trick in
another suit, and finally switch back to their main threat. This is a typical example:

´éź A 8 2
´é¬ Q 2
´éę 10 6
´éĘ K J 10 8 6 3
Dealer South
´éź K 9 7 6 ´éź Q J 4 3
Both Vul.
´é¬ 10 8 7 ´é¬ 9 6 5 4 3
´éę K 5 3 2 ´éę Q 9 7 4 West North East South

´éź 10 5 pass 3NT all pass
´é¬ A K J
´éę A J 8
´éĘ Q 9 7 5 2
West leads the ´éź6. When declarer decides to duck, fearing the lead might be from
a five-card suit, East wins with the jack. East can see that his side can only score
three spade tricks, so he switches to the ´éę9 (second highest from a suit without the
ace or king). Declarer is forced to duck, so West wins and can now switch back to
spades, setting up enough defensive tricks.
oBlIgatorY Falsecards
I promised earlier that I would discuss falsecards, and the time has now come.
There are numerous situations where a defender can make declarer work for a
living by following with the right card. Many of these falsecards are obligatory, in
the sense that the play should be automatic.
´éź A J 7 4
´éź K 2 ´éź 10 9 5
´éź Q 8 6 3
Follow with the nine or ten when declarer plays low to the jack ÔÇö he may play you
for doubleton ten-nine.
´é¬ Q 9 4
´é¬ J 10 5
If declarer lays down the ace, you should play the jack or ten, again making him
guess next round.

Chapter 9
counterIng A menAcIng sIde suIt
One of the scariest situations for a defender is when declarer has a long side suit,
either in hand or (more usually) in dummy. You know all those tricks are coming
over the hill at you eventually unless you can do something about it. Fortunately,
there are many ways that you might be able to do just that.
when declarer has entrY trouBle
Again, we come back to entries: the lifeblood of bridge. If declarer is in trouble
for entries, there are a number of things the defenders may be able to do, some of
which we have already encountered.
1. Force the run of the long suit before an entry is built to the opposite hand
(suicide squeeze)
This revolves around timing ÔÇö the defenders force declarer to cash his long suit
before he has all his tricks set up elsewhere. In the ideal situation, discarding
becomes a problem.

---

´é¬ K Q 6
´éę K 8 4 2
´éĘ J 5

should realize that as long as his partner has the ´éźQ, he can always hold declarer
to eight tricks by the simple expedient of cashing the ´é¬A and then exiting with a
club. In the four-card ending, West keeps the ´éźA5 and ´éęA10. Declarer is down
to the ´éźK10 and ´éęK8 and must unguard one of the pointed suits on the last club.

1. Transfer to diamonds, invitational plus.
´éĘ A K 9 6
West leads the ´éź7. Declarer wins with dummyÔÇÖs king and plays a diamond to the

to clear the diamonds. The only hope is to attack the potential entry in dummy by
switching to a heart. Not just any old heart, though ÔÇö it must be the queen, and
if declarer covers, East must duck.
This might be called Chinese Soft Entry Destruction, to distinguish it from
leading the queen through A10x.
6. Days of Thunder
You will recall that this play (see p. 156) involves a defender deliberately leading

West leads the ´éĘJ and East wins with the king. It should not be difficult to see that
declarer must be close to twelve tricks ÔÇö five diamonds, a heart and five trump
tricks (counting a ruff in dummy). If declarer can secure a second club ruff he will
be home. A trump switch is a possibility, but if declarer has the ´éźQ10 or (as here)
the ´éźQ98, there will be enough entries (although on this layout declarer has to
finesse the ´éę9 to make sure of that). A better try is to switch to a diamond. Even
though declarer can win in hand with the nine and ruff a club, he does not have
enough entries to organize a second club ruff and get back to draw trumps.

When the long suit is in dummy, the situation is a little easier, since dummy often
has fewer high cards, and therefore the entries are more vulnerable to attack.
However, thatÔÇÖs not the only weapon available to the defense.
1. Get your own tricks first

3. The trump holdup
In some situations it can be a good idea to discard even if you could win the trick
by ruffing. In essence this is a type of holdup play, just like ducking a winner. The
most common type is when they have a running suit in dummy but no side entry.

4. Lead the long suit, hoping to kill one or more winners when partner can ruff
Sometimes it pays to lead the long suit itself, hoping partner can ruff some of the
winners with otherwise useless small trumps. This spectacular example comes
from the 2002 Blue Ribbon Pairs in Phoenix.
´éź A K Q 5 4

West ruffed with the ´é¬3 and switched to a club. East won with the queen, cashed
the ace and went back to spades. Declarer ruffed high and continued with a high
trump which East won to play a fourth spade. Declarer ruffed high again and
cashed the ´é¬10. Hoping the outstanding trumps were now 1-1 he exited with
a heart, but East could win, draw the last trump (squeezing dummy into parting
with a diamond) and play a diamond. West scored two tricks in that suit, leaving
declarer four down.

reAsons to PlAy second hAnd hIgh
Back in Chapter 1, I talked briefly about some of the basic rules for defensive
cardplay. I also warned you that there are times when those rules must be
disregarded, and such occasions are the subject of this part of the book.
LetÔÇÖs begin with a chapter on the many reasons why a defender may have to
reject the concept of playing second hand low.
PassIng the Freeze
The term frozen suit is well known and applies to any situation where no one can
play a suit to their advantage. This is a simple example:
´éź K 10 6
´éź Q 7 5 3 ´éź J 4 2
´éź A 9 8
If West or East lead this suit they risk surrendering a trick (the best shot is to lead
an honor, hoping declarer goes wrong). By the same token, declarer will avoid
playing the suit, hoping that eventually the defenders will have to broach it.
242 * Part 4 ÔÇö Counter-Intuitive Defense

---

Going up with a high card can force them to win when the suit is frozen.
´é¬ A J 6
´é¬ Q 10 4 ´é¬ K 7 3 2
´é¬ 9 8 5
When the nine or eight is led, play the queen. This ÔÇśpasses the freezeÔÇÖ to North,
who is forced to win the trick. If you duck instead, declarer lets the nine run and
you are Chinesed; if you cover the nine with the ten then East is endplayed if he
canÔÇÖt exit in another suit. Note, however, that the ten would be a good play from
´é¬107x. Even though East is still endplayed, at least there is a guess for declarer on
the way back instead of a sure thing, which would be the case if you ducked.
unBlockIng
With no sight of partnerÔÇÖs hand, it is harder for a defender to recognize the need
to unblock. Apart from unblocking a high card to avoid being thrown in (a topic I
covered in Chapter 8), one of the most obvious reasons is to enable partner to win
a trick in a suit, as on this type of deal:
´éź Q J 4 3
´é¬ Q 8 5 3
´éę 8 4 3
´éĘ 6 2

´é¬ A 7 4 all pass
´éę K 6
´éĘ A 8 4
West leads the ´éĘK. Declarer ducks, wins the second round and plays two rounds
of trumps, West discarding a diamond. If declarer now cashes the ´é¬A, West must
unblock the king, turning EastÔÇÖs jack into an entry. As you may have noticed,
declarer can do better. After two rounds of trumps he plays a low heart, planning
to put up dummyÔÇÖs queen. If that holds, declarer can then play a low heart from
dummy, intending to duck the trick to West. To counter that, West must play the
´é¬10 on the first round of the suit ÔÇö a different type of unblock in the same suit.

danger hand hIgh
This topic formed the basis of my Bols Bridge Tip back in 1989. HereÔÇÖs the gist
of it. If you are the danger hand (you have tricks to cash, or can lead through to
partner with effect, for example), playing second hand high can seriously embarrass
declarer, since he canÔÇÖt afford to let you hold the trick. You may prevent declarer
from ducking, a necessary play to establish a suit. Also, you may play on declarerÔÇÖs
fear that your holding is better than it is and that might prevent or persuade him
from continuing the suit. Sometimes you may convince declarer to try for a non-
existent endplay.
Playing second hand high can also destroy declarerÔÇÖs communications ÔÇö if he
ducks, he surrenders a trick and if he wins, a crucial entry is lost.
Reasons to Play Second Hand High * 249

---

LetÔÇÖs assume you are West, the danger hand, here:
´éź K 10 3 2
´éź J 8 7
Defending against notrump, when declarer leads toward dummy you should go up
with the jack when this suit is led in most cases. If declarer has ´éźAxx or ´éźQxx, he
would have stuck in the ten anyway, to lose to the safe opponent. Putting up the
jack helps if declarer has ´éźAxx and thinks you have ´éźQJ(x). The risk to keep in
mind is that partner might occasionally have ´éźQx.
´é¬ A 10 8 7 4
´é¬ Q 5
In this case, there is little risk in playing the queen (unless declarer has ´é¬Jx, or
´é¬Jxx, when your play will help to unblock the suit). If declarer has ´é¬Kxx and no
side entry to dummy, then this play can be devastating if he canÔÇÖt afford to lose a
trick to you.

In a match in the 2007 Vanderbilt, declarer (in a doubled club partscore) needed to
set up a long card in this suit for a spade discard, but could not allow West to get
on lead early for fear of a damaging switch (dummy held the ´éźAQ, declarer had a
doubleton and it was clear the king was offside). When declarer played a low heart
from his hand, West put up the ´é¬K. Declarer could not afford to duck so he took
the ace ÔÇö on which East dropped the jack!
With no quick entry back to hand declarer tried a low heart off dummy, but
East followed with the five! West was able to win with the ´é¬9 and make the killing
switch to a spade. It was PolandÔÇÖs Balicki and Zmudzinski who managed this piece
of brilliance. Notice that declarer could have prevailed by leading the ten to the
second round of hearts.

Reasons to Play Second Hand High * 253

---

This works if East has ´é¬Qx or a singleton heart, as there is no hope that he can get
a trump promotion. Once East ruffs and returns a spade, you exit with the other
high heart and get 500 in due course.
The defense is trickier if you continue with a high heart at Trick 2, East pitching
an encouraging club. Suppose you play a third heart, ruffed high, as East pitches a
spade. Now declarer tests a round of trumps and leads a club from dummy. Last
chance: East must insert an honor (second hand high) to stop declarer putting
in the ´éĘ9. If declarer can duck the club to you the defense is finished, as he can
eliminate the clubs and lead a spade toward the jack (he knows you are probably
3=7=0=3 so no other spade layout works for him).
Even after East puts in the ´éĘJ, the hand is not over. If declarer wins the ´éĘA,
plays a trump to dummy and leads another club, East must rise with his other high
club for a spade play.

258 * Part 4 ÔÇö Counter-Intuitive Defense

the IntraPoP
If you are marked with high cards in a suit, or with shortness in a suit, you might
give declarer a problem by going up from a doubleton honor if you think he is
going to try an intrafinesse.
´éź A 9 4
´éź K 8 6 3
Here declarer leads the ´éź3, planning to put in the nine. His next move will be
to cash the ace, perhaps planning to finesse on the next round if you produce an
honor.
However, suppose you (West) play an honor on the first round of the suit ÔÇö
an intrapop? Now declarer has to allow for the possibility that you started with a
stiff honor or two honors doubleton as well as honor doubleton. This is hard to
deal with, especially if dummy has no side entry.
If dummy has another entry, declarer can take the ace and run the nine
(winning if East covers then lobbing toward his eight when back in dummy). If
East plays low, declarer plans to take two finesses, losing only if you started with
QJ10 tight.
If East covers and declarer has no hand re-entry, he might duck then finesse
the third time, but this leads to ignomious defeat if West started with two honors
third.
´éź 8 3 2 Dealer South
´é¬ A 9 4 NS Vul.
´éę 7 6 4
West North East South
´éĘ 10 6 5 2
1´éź
´éź 10 7 4
pass 1NT pass 2´é¬
´é¬ J 3 dbl 2´éź 3´éę 4´éź
´éę K Q J 10 all pass
´éĘ K Q J 3
You cash the ´éęK, but partner overtakes the ´éęQ with the ace and returns the ´éĘ9 to
declarerÔÇÖs ace. Declarer draws three rounds of trumps, partner pitching two clubs.
How do you plan to defend from here?
It is clear that declarer is 6=4=2=1 and that you need two heart tricks to set the
contract. You need declarer to have only one top heart honor, but he knows by
now that you have one or two hearts, so if he has ´é¬Q10xx or ´é¬K10xx he will stick
in the nine, with a sure thing to come later. You need to hope partner has the ´é¬10,
and ´é¬Q10xx is his most likely holding (if he has ´é¬K10xx you are fine anyway).
However, if you play low when a heart is led, declarer will put in dummyÔÇÖs nine,
taking the marked finesse later.
Reasons to Play Second Hand High * 259

---

So you must plan to go up with the ´é¬J, the intrapop. Ducking the ´é¬J is fatal,
and declarer might try that if he places you with two honors doubleton. If instead
he wins the ace and returns the suit, he has to guess when partner plays low again.

With eight tricks in his own hand opposite an opening bid, West decided to forgo a
